a plane perpendicular to a given line.
\end{Problem}

% [** Illustrations] FIG. 1.    FIG. 2.
\begin{center}
\Input{043ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{\Inum{(1)}~Given a line~$l$ and a point~$P$ on it. \textsc{Fig.~1.}}
{To construct a plane $M \perp l$ at~$P$.}

\begin{Construction}
Through $P$ draw two lines $PA$ and $PB$ each
perpendicular to~$l$.\hfill ~\Sref{127},~\Sref{75}

Then the plane $M$ determined by $PA$ and~$PB$ is $\perp l$.\hfill ~\Sref{76}
\end{Construction}

\Objective{\Inum{(2)}~Given a line~$l$ and a point~$P$ not on it. \textsc{Fig.~2.}}
{To construct a plane~$N$ through~$P$ and $\perp l$.}

\begin{Construction}
From $P$ draw a line $PQ \perp l$.\hfill ~\Sref{127}

At $Q$ construct a plane $N \perp l$, as in~(1).

Then, by ~\Sref{79}, $PQ$ lies in $N$, and hence~$N$ is the plane required. \\
\QEF
\end{Construction}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 044.png---Folio 36-------

\Section{CONSTRUCTING A LINE PERPENDICULAR TO A PLANE}

\begin{Problem}{130.}
Through a given point to construct a
line perpendicular to a given plane.
\end{Problem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{044ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{\Inum{(1)}~Given a plane $M$ and a point $P$ in it.}
{To construct a line through $P$ and $\perp M$.}

\begin{Construction}
Let $l$ be any line in $M$ not passing through $P$.

Draw $PB \perp l$. Through $B$ draw $BK \perp l$ but not in~$M$.

In the plane $PBK$ draw $PC \perp PB$ and meeting $BK$ in~$C$.

Prove $PC \perp M$.\hfill ~\Sref{76}, \Sref{117}, \Sref{114}
\end{Construction}

\Objective{\Inum{(2)}~Given a plane $N$ and a point $P$ not in it.}
{To construct a line through $P$ and $\perp N$.}

\begin{Construction}
Let $l$ be any line in $N$. Draw $PB \perp l$ and in
$N$ draw $BK \perp l$. In the plane $PBK$ draw $PC \perp BK$.

Prove, as in~(1), $PC \perp N$.\hfill\hbox{(See also ~\Sref{80}.)} \\
\QEF
\end{Construction}

\Section{CONSTRUCTING A PLANE PARALLEL TO A LINE}

\begin{Problem}{131.}
Through one of two skew lines to
pass a plane parallel to the other.
\end{Problem}

\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Objective{Given two skew lines $l_1$ and $l_2$.}
{To construct a plane through $l_1 \parallel l_2$.}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{044c}{1in}}

\begin{Construction}
Through a point $P$ in $l_1$ pass a
line $l_3 \parallel l_2$.\hfill\hbox{(~\Sref{126}.)}

The plane determined by $l_1$ and $l_3$ is $\parallel l_2$.\hfill ~\Sref{90} \\
\QEF
\end{Construction}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 045.png---Folio 37-------

\Section{CONSTRUCTING A PLANE PERPENDICULAR TO A PLANE}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\begin{Problem}{132.}
Through a given line to pass a
plane perpendicular to a
given plane.
\end{Problem}

\Objective{Given a line $l$ and a plane $M$.}
{To pass a plane through $l$ and
$\perp M$.}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{045a}{2in}}

\begin{Construction}
Through a point $P$ in $l$ pass a line $l_1 \perp M$.
\hfill ~\Sref{130}

The plane $N$ determined by $l$ and $l_1$ passes through $l$ and
is $\perp M$.\\
\null\hfill ~\Sref{117} \\
\QEF
\end{Construction}


\Section{CONSTRUCTING A PLANE PARALLEL TO EACH OF TWO LINES}

\begin{Problem}{133.}
Through a point to pass a plane
parallel to each of two given lines.
\end{Problem}

\Objective{Given a point $P$ and two lines $l_1$ and~$l_2$.}
{To construct a plane through $P$ parallel to $l_1$ and~$l_2$.}

\begin{Construction}
Through $P$ pass lines $l'_1$ and $l'_2$ such that
$l'_1 \parallel l_1$ and $l'_2 \parallel l_2$.\hfill ~\Sref{126}

Then the plane determined by $l'_1$ and $l'_2$ is parallel to both
$l_1$ and~$l_2$. \\
\null\hfill ~\Sref{90} \\
\QEF
\end{Construction}


% SIGHT WORK
\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Study the special case of ~\Sref{132} in which the given line is perpendicular
to the given plane.

\item[2.] Is it possible that the plane constructed by the method of ~\Sref{133} may
contain one or both of the given lines? Study all possible cases.

\item[3.] Through a given point pass a plane $\perp$ each of two given planes.
How many such can be constructed? Study the case in which the two
given planes are parallel to each other.

\end{Sightwork}

\begin{Suggestion}
Consider the relation of the required plane to the intersection
of the two given planes.
\end{Suggestion}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 046.png---Folio 38-------

\Section{COMMON PERPENDICULAR TO TWO SKEW LINES}

\begin{Problem}{134.}
To construct a common perpendicular
to two skew lines.
\end{Problem}

% [** Illustration]
\vspace*{-0.375in}
\begin{center}
\Input{046a}{0.5\textwidth}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Objective{Given two skew lines $l_1$ and $l_2$.}
{To construct a line $BC$ perpendicular to each of them.}

\begin{Construction}
Through $A$, any point in $l_2$, draw $l_3 \parallel l_1$.

Let $M$ be the plane determined by $l_2$ and $l_3$.

Through $l_1$ pass a plane $N \perp M$ and meeting $M$ in $l_4$.

At $B$ the intersection of $l_2$ and $l_4$ erect $BC \perp M$.

Then $BC$ is the required line perpendicular to $l_1$ and $l_2$.
\end{Construction}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: Show each of the following:

(1)~$BC$ lies in the plane $N$, and $\therefore$ meets $l_1$.

(2)~$l_1 \parallel l_4$, (3)~$BC \parallel l_4$, and $\therefore BC \perp l_1$.

\begin{Corollary}{135.}{}
There cannot be more than one common
perpendicular to two skew lines.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Suggestion}
Suppose a second common perpendicular drawn from a
point $P_1$ in $l_1$ to a point $P_2$ in $l_2$. Then show that from $P_1$ it would be
possible to have two lines $\perp$ plane $M$.
\end{Suggestion}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If a line is $\perp$ a plane, show that its projection is a point.

\item[2.] If a line-segment is $\parallel$ a plane, show that its projection is a segment
equal to the given segment.

\item[3.] If a line-segment is oblique to a plane, show that its projection is
less than the given segment.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 047.png---Folio 39-------

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Show that the projections of two parallel lines on a plane
are parallel. Is the converse true? Illustrate with pieces of
cardboard.

\item[2.] If two parallel lines meet a plane, they make equal
angles with it. Why? Is the converse true? What is the
corresponding theorem in plane geometry? Is its converse
true?

\item[3.] If a line cuts two parallel planes, it makes equal angles
with them. Why? Is the converse true? Discuss the corresponding
theorems in plane geometry.

\item[4.] If two parallel line-segments are oblique to a plane,
their projections on the plane are in the same ratio as the given
segments.

\item[5.] A line and its projection on a plane determine a plane
perpendicular to the given plane.

\item[6.] If a line is parallel to one of two given planes and perpendicular
to the other, then the two planes are perpendicular
to each other.

\item[7.] Find the locus of all points equidistant from two points
$A$ and~$B$ and also at a given distance from a plane~$M$.
Discuss.\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|(}%

\item[8.] Find the locus of all points equidistant from two given
planes and also equidistant from two given points. Discuss.

\item[9.] Find the locus of all points equidistant from two given
planes $M$ and~$N$, and also equidistant from two other given
planes $Q$ and~$R$. Discuss. Compare with the corresponding
loci in plane geometry.
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|)}%

\item[10.] Prove that there is a line in space every point of which
is equidistant from three points $A$, $B$, $C$, provided these points
do not lie on one line.
\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 048.png---Folio 40-------

\Section{POLYHEDRAL ANGLES}
\SecToc{Polyhedral Angles}

\Paragraph{136.}{Polyhedral Angle.} Given a convex polygon and a point
\index{Angle@\textbf{Angle}!polyhedral}%{\Pnum{136}}%
\index{Polyhedral@\textbf{Polyhedral angles}}%{\Pnum{136}}%
$P$ not in its plane. If a half-line~$l$ with its end point fixed at
$P$ moves so that it always touches the polygon and is made to
traverse it completely, it is said to generate a \emph{convex polyhedral
angle}.

\begin{wrapfigure}[7]{r}{1.5in}
% [** Illustration]
\Input{048a}{1.5in}
\end{wrapfigure}
\Paragraph{137.}{Vertex. Edges.} The fixed point is the \emph{vertex} of the
\index{Edge@\textbf{Edge}!of a polyhedral angle}%{\Pnum{137}}%
\index{Faces@\textbf{Faces}!of a polyhedral angle}%{\Pnum{137}}%
\index{Polyhedral@\textbf{Polyhedral angles}!edges of}%{\Pnum{137}}%
\index{Vertex@\textbf{Vertex}!of a polyhedral angle}%{\Pnum{137}}%
polyhedral angle, and the rays through
the vertices of the polygon are the \emph{edges}
of the polyhedral angle.

Any two consecutive edges determine a
plane, and the portion of such a plane
included between these edges is called a
\emph{face} of the polyhedral angle.

\Paragraph{138.}{Face Angles.} The plane angles at the vertex are called
\index{Angle@\textbf{Angle}!face}%{\Pnum{138}}%
the \emph{face angles} of the polyhedral angle. A polyhedral angle
having three faces is called a \emph{trihedral angle}.
\index{Angle@\textbf{Angle}!trihedral}%{\Pnum{138}}%
\index{Polyhedral@\textbf{Polyhedral angles}!faces of}%{\Pnum{138}}%
\index{Trihedral@\textbf{Trihedral angles}}%{\Pnum{138}}%

\begin{Remark}
Thus in the figure, $P$ is the vertex, $PA$, $PB$, etc., are the edges, and
$\angle APB$, $\angle BPC$, etc., are the face angles.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{139.}{Equal Polyhedral Angles.} Two polyhedral angles are
\emph{equal} if they can be made to coincide.
\index{Polyhedral@\textbf{Polyhedral angles}!equal}%{\Pnum{139}}%

\begin{Remark}
A polyhedral angle is read by naming the vertex and one letter in
each edge, as $P-ABCDE$, or by naming the vertex alone where no ambiguity
would arise.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{140.}{Order of Parts in Triangles.} In  the triangles $ABC$,
\begin{wrapfigure}[5]{r}{3in}
% [** Illustration]
\Input{048bc}{3in}
\end{wrapfigure}
\index{Order@\textbf{Order of parts}!in a triangle}%{\Pnum{140}}%
and $A'B'C'$, $AB = A'B'$,
$BC = B'C'$,  $CA = C'A'$.
However, the sides $AB$,
$BC$, $CA$, are arranged
in counter-clockwise order, and the sides $A'B'$, $B'C'$, $C'A'$, are
arranged in clockwise order. That is, the parts of the two
triangles are arranged in opposite orders.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 049.png---Folio 41-------

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.875in}
\Indent\Paragraph{141.}{Order of Parts in Polyhedral Angles.}
\index{Order@\textbf{Order of parts}!in a polyhedral angle}%{\Pnum{141}}%
In the trihedral angles $O$
and $O'$ the face angles $AOB$, $BOC$,
$COA$ are arranged in \emph{counter-clockwise}
order as viewed from the vertex,
while  $A'O'B'$, $B'O'C'$, $C'O'A'$ are
arranged in \emph{clockwise} order.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{049ab}{1.875in}

\begin{Remark}
The trihedral angles $O$ and $O'$ cannot be made to coincide, even though
their corresponding parts are equal. This can be illustrated by attempting
to put a left glove on the right hand.
\end{Remark}

\Section{CONDITIONS WHICH MAKE TRIHEDRAL ANGLES EQUAL}

\begin{Theorem}{142.}{XXIII.}
Two trihedral angles are
equal if two face angles and the included dihedral
angle of one are equal respectively to two face angles
and the included dihedral angle of the other and arranged
in the same order.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{049cd}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\textbf{Suggestion for Proof}: This and the following theorem may be
proved by superposition in the same manner as the corresponding
theorems on the equality of triangles in plane geometry.

\begin{Theorem}{143.}{XXIV.} Two trihedral angles are
equal if a face angle and the two adjacent dihedral
angles of one are equal respectively to a face angle and
the adjacent dihedral angles of the other and arranged
in the same order.
\end{Theorem}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 050.png---Folio 42-------

\Section{EQUAL DIHEDRAL ANGLES OPPOSITE EQUAL FACE ANGLES}

\begin{Theorem}{144.}{XXV.}
If two trihedral angles have
the three face angles of the one equal respectively to the
three face angles of the other, the dihedral angles opposite
the equal face angles are equal.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{050ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Objective{Given the trihedral angles $P$ and $P'$, in which $\angle a = \angle a'$, $\angle b
= \angle b'$, $\angle c = \angle c'$.}
{To prove that the corresponding dihedral angles are equal.}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: Let $P$ and $P'$ be cut by the planes $ABC$ and
$A'B'C'$, making $PA = PB = PC = P'A' = P'B' = P'C'$.

On the edges $PA$ and $P'A'$ lay off $AF = A'F'$, and through
$F$ and $F'$ pass planes $\perp$ to $PA$ and $P'A'$ respectively.

\begin{Remark}
$AF$ and $A'F'$ are to be taken short enough so that these planes shall
cut the segments $AB$, $AC$ and $A'B'$, $A'C'$ respectively.
\end{Remark}

In order to prove that $\angle B-AP-C = \angle B'-A'P'-C'$, i.e.~that
$\angle DFE = \angle D'F'E'$, use the pairs of $\triangles APB$, $A'P'B'$; $BPC$,
$B'P'C'$; $CPA$, $C'P'A'$; $ABC$, $A'B'C'$.    Then prove in order:
\begin{gather*}
(1)\quad \triangle ADF = \triangle A'D'F'; \qquad (2)\quad \triangle AEF = \triangle A'E'F'; \\
(3)\quad \triangle ADE = \triangle A'D'E'; \qquad (4)\quad \triangle DEF = \triangle D'E'F'. \\
\therefore\quad \angle DFE = \angle D'F'E'.
\end{gather*}

\begin{Corollary}{145.}{}
Two trihedral angles are equal if the face
angles of one are equal respectively to the face angles of the other
and arranged in the same order.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 051.png---Folio 43-------

\Section{SYMMETRICAL TRIHEDRAL ANGLES}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{146.}{Symmetrical Trihedral Angles.} Two
\index{Polyhedral@\textbf{Polyhedral angles}!symmetrical}%{\Pnum{146}}%
\index{Symmetrical@\textbf{Symmetrical}!trihedral angles}%{\Pnum{146}}%
\index{Trihedral@\textbf{Trihedral angles}!symmetrical}%{\Pnum{146}}%
trihedral angles are \emph{symmetrical}, one to the
other, if the face angles and the dihedral
angles of one are equal respectively to the
face angles and the dihedral angles of the
other, but arranged in the \emph{opposite order}.

\Indent\Paragraph{147.}{Vertical Trihedral Angles.} Two trihedral
\index{Trihedral@\textbf{Trihedral angles}!vertical}%{\Pnum{147}}%
angles are \emph{vertical} if the edges of
one are the prolongations of the edges of
the other.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{051a}{1.25in}


\begin{Corollary}{148.}{1.}
Two vertical trihedral angles are symmetrical.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Remark}
The proof is evident from the figure above.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Corollary}{149.}{2.}
Two trihedral angles are symmetrical if
the face angles of one are equal to the face angles of the other and
arranged in the opposite order.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Is it possible that all three face angles of a trihedral angle shall be
right angles? Is it possible that all three dihedral angles shall be right
angles?

\begin{Suggestion}
Consider a corner of a rectangular box.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[2.] If three planes meet in a point how many trihedral angles are
formed? If all face angles of one of these trihedral angles are right
angles, what about the face angles of the other trihedral angles?

\item[3.] If in the figure of ~\Sref{144} the $\angle a = \angle b = \angle c = 60$, what can be said
about the triangles $PAB$, $PBC$, $PCA$, and $ABC$?

\item[4.] Show how to locate a point which is at a distance of $2$~feet from
each of the three planes determined by the faces of a trihedral angle.
How many such points are there?

\begin{Suggestion}
Pass planes parallel to each face of the trihedral angle,
one on each side of it, and at a distance of $2$~feet from it.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 052.png---Folio 44-------

\Section{CONDITIONS WHICH MAKE TRIHEDRAL ANGLES  SYMMETRICAL}

\begin{Theorem}{150.}{XXVI.}
Two trihedral angles are
symmetrical
\end{Theorem}

\Inum{(1)}~if two face angles and the included dihedral
angle of one are equal respectively to two face angles
and the included dihedral angle of the other, but arranged
in the opposite order; or

\Inum{(2)}~if a face angle and the adjacent dihedral angles
of one are equal respectively to a face angle and the
adjacent dihedral angles of the other, but arranged in
the opposite order.

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{052abc}{}
\end{center}

\Proof: Let $t_1$ and $t_2$ be two trihedral angles having the
properties specified under~(1). Let $t_3$ be a trihedral angle
symmetrical to $t_1$. Then $t_2 = t_3$.\hfill ~\Sref{142}
\[
\therefore\quad\text{$t_1$ and $t_2$ are symmetrical.}
\]

The proof of the second part is left to the student.

\begin{Theorem}{151.}{XXVII.}
The sum of two face angles
of a trihedral angle is greater than the third face angle.
\end{Theorem}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\textbf{Outline of Proof}: Connect points $A$ and $C$ on two
sides. Suppose $\angle ADC > \angle ADB$. Construct $DE$
making $\angle ADE = \angle ADB$. Suppose that point $B$ is
taken so that $DB = DE$, and that $AB$ and $BC$ are
drawn. Then prove
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{-0.25in}{\smash{\Input{052d}{1.25in}}}
\Indent\Indent\begin{minipage}{3.5in}
\noindent%
(1)\quad $AB = AE$,\qquad (2)\quad $AC < AB + BC$, \\
(3)\quad $EC < BC$,\qquad (4)\quad $\angle EDC < \angle BDC$, \\
(5)\quad $\angle ADC < \angle ADB + \angle BDC$.
\end{minipage}%

\NewFolio %% -----File: 053.png---Folio 45-------

\Section{SUM OF THE FACE ANGLES OF A POLYHEDRAL ANGLE}

\begin{Theorem}{152.}{XXVIII.}
The sum of the face angles
of any convex polyhedral angle is less than four right
angles.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{053a}{0.5\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Proof: Let $ABCDE$ be a plane section of the given polyhedral
angle. The number of triangles thus formed having
$P$ for a vertex is equal to the number of face angles of the
polyhedral angle.

Let $O$ be any point in the base, and draw $OA$, $OB$, $OC$, etc.

Then $\angle PBA + \angle PBC > \angle ABC$, $\angle PCB + \angle PCD >
\angle BCD$, and so on.\hfill ~\Sref{151}

Now the sum of the $\Angles$ of the $\triangles OAB$, $OBC$, etc., is equal to
the sum of the $\Angles$ of the $\triangles PAB$, $PBC$, etc.

Hence, $\angle APB + \angle BPC + \dotsb < \angle AOB + \angle BOC + \dotsb$.

But the sum of the $\Angles$ about $O$ is four right angles.

Therefore, the sum of the face angles of the polyhedral
angle is less than four right angles.
\QED

\textit{Note}. If as in ~\Sref{144} the three edges of a trihedral angle are cut by a
plane, the intersection is a triangle. Then the sides and angles of such
a triangle \emph{correspond} to the face angles and dihedral angles respectively
of the trihedral angle.

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] What theorem in plane geometry corresponds to the theorem of
~\Sref{151} in the sense of the above note?

\item[2.] Discuss propositions of plane geometry corresponding to those
of ~\Sref{150}.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 054.png---Folio 46-------

\Section{SUMMARY OF BOOK I}
\index{Summaries@\textbf{Summaries}}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] State the axioms used in this Book.

\item[2.] Describe the various ways of determining a plane.

\item[3.] State the definitions on perpendicular lines and planes.

\item[4.] State a theorem on a line through a point perpendicular
to a plane.

\item[5.] State a theorem on a plane through a point perpendicular
to a line.

\item[6.] State the definitions on parallel lines and planes.

\item[7.] State propositions on (1)~a plane through a point parallel
to a given plane; (2)~a plane through a line parallel to a given
line; (3)~a plane through a point parallel to two given lines.

\item[8.] State some facts about perpendiculars in the plane
which do not hold in space.

\item[9.] Define a dihedral angle and the plane angle of a dihedral
angle.

\item[10.] What theorems on perpendicular planes are proved in
connection with dihedral angles? Notice that these theorems
include all those on one plane perpendicular to another. Why
should this be the case?

\item[11.] State the definitions and theorems on projections given
in this Book.

\item[12.] State the definitions and theorems on polyhedral angles
thus far given.

\item[13.] Define \emph{symmetrical polyhedral angles}. Are symmetrical
trihedral angles equal?

\item[14.] State theorems on equal and symmetrical trihedral
angles.

\item[15.] Give examples of loci in plane geometry which differ from
corresponding loci in solid geometry. See, for instance, ~\Sref{6}.
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 055.png---Folio 47-------

\Section{MISCELLANEOUS EXERCISES ON BOOK I}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] A Christmas tree is made to stand on a cross-shaped
base. What must be the relation of the tree to each piece of
the cross in order that it may be perpendicular to the floor?

\item[2.] If $A$, $B$, and~$C$ do not lie in the same line, and if their
projections on a plane~$M$ do lie in a straight line, what is the
relation of the planes $M$ and~$ABC$?

\item[3.] Is it possible to project a circle upon a plane so that the
projection shall be a straight line-segment? If so, how must
the circle and the plane be related?

\item[4.] If the projections of a set of points on each of two planes
not parallel to each other lie in straight lines, show that the
points themselves lie in a straight line.

\item[5.] Find the locus of points $3$~feet from one of two intersecting
planes and $6$~feet from the other.
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|(}%

\item[6.] It is required that a series of electric lights shall be
$7$~feet above the floor of a room and $3$~feet from the walls.
Find the locus of all points at which such lights may be placed.

\item[7.] Find the locus of a point in space such that the difference
of the squares of its distances from two fixed points, $A$ and $B$,
is constant.

\begin{Suggestion}
Through either $A$ or~$B$ construct a plane $\perp AB$.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[8.] Given two points $A$ and~$B$ on the same side of a
plane~$M$. Determine a point~$P$ in $M$ such that $AP + PB$
shall be a minimum.

\begin{Suggestion}
Pass a plane through $A$ and~$B$ perpendicular to~$M$, and
proceed as in Ex.~10, page~280, Plane Geometry.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[9.] Show that if the edge of a dihedral angle is cut by two
parallel planes, the sections which they make with the faces
form equal angles. Is the converse proposition true?

\item[10.] Show that if all edges of a trihedral angle are cut by a
series of parallel planes, the intersections form a series of
similar triangles. Is the converse proposition true?
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 056.png---Folio 48-------

\begin{Itemize}
\item[11.] Find the locus of the intersection points of the medians
of the triangles obtained in Ex.~10. Also of the altitudes.

\item[12.] How many planes may be made to pass through a given
point parallel to a given line? Discuss the mutual relation of
all such planes.

\item[13.] Through a point~$P$ construct a line meeting each of two
lines $l_1$ and $l_2$. %[** PP: Missing end-of-sentence period added]

\begin{Suggestion}
Let $M$ and $N$ be the planes determined by $P$ and~$l_1$, $P$
and~$l_2$. Consider their intersection. Is this construction always possible?
\end{Suggestion}

\item[14.] A line $l$ is parallel to a plane $M$, and lines $l_1$ and~$l_2$ in~$M$
are not parallel to~$l$. Show that the shortest distance between
$l$ and~$l_1$ is equal to the shortest distance between $l$ and~$l_2$.

\item[15.] Find the locus of all points equidistant from the planes
determined by the faces of a trihedral angle.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent\item[16.] Find the locus of all points equidistant
from the edges of a trihedral angle.
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|)}%

\begin{Suggestion}
On the edges lay off $PA = PB = PC$.
Let $O$ be equidistant from $A$, $B$, and~$C$. Then
any point $Q$ in $PO$ is equidistant from the edges.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[17.] Planes determined  by the edges of
a trihedral angle and the bisectors of the
opposite face angles meet in a line.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.5in}{\smash{\Input{056a}{1.5in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent\item[18.] If three planes are so related that the segments intercepted
on any transversal line are in the
same ratio as the segments intercepted on
any other transversal then the planes are
parallel.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{056b}{1.55in}}

\begin{Suggestion}
Let $M$, $N$, $Q$ be the three planes.
From $A$ any point in $M$ draw three lines, not in the
same plane, meeting $N$ in $A'$, $B'$, $C'$, and $Q$ in
$A''$, $B''$, $C''$. Use the hypothesis to show that $A'B' \parallel A''B''$ and
$C'B' \parallel C''B''$. Hence $Q \parallel N$. Similarly show that $M \parallel N$.
\end{Suggestion}

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 057.png---Folio 49-------

\begin{BioSketch}{Thales}{Thales of Miletus}
  (640--542~B.C\@.) %[** PP: Added missing period, was B C.]
  was one of the Seven Wise Men of
  Greece. He learned astronomy and geometry in Egypt and was the first
  to introduce the scientific study of geometry in Greece. He measured
  the height of the Pyramids in Egypt by similar triangles and found a
  method of computing the distance of a ship at sea. He predicted the
  solar eclipse of 585~B.C\@.
\end{BioSketch}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 058.png---Folio 50-------

\Chapter{BOOK II}{Regular Polyhedrons}
% [** PP: Explicit pagination to accommodate large illo on next page]
\enlargethispage{36pt}

\Paragraph{153.}{Polyhedron.} A \emph{polyhedron} % [** PP: Emphasis added]
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}}%{\Pnum{153}}%
is a geometric solid whose
boundary consists of plane polygons.

\Paragraph{154.}{Convex Polyhedron.} A polyhedron
is \emph{convex} if every section of it made by a
plane is a convex polygon.
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!convex}%{\Pnum{154}}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{155.}{Faces, Vertices, Edges.} The polygons
\index{Edge@\textbf{Edge}!of a polyhedron}%{\Pnum{155}}%
\index{Faces@\textbf{Faces}!of a polyhedron}%{\Pnum{155}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!edges of}%{\Pnum{155}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!faces of}%{\Pnum{155}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!vertices of}%{\Pnum{155}}%
\index{Vertices@\textbf{Vertices}!of a polyhedron}%{\Pnum{155}}%
which bound the polyhedron are its \emph{faces};
the sides of these faces are the \emph{edges} and their vertices are the
\emph{vertices} of the polyhedron.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{058a}{1.25in}}

\Paragraph{156.}{Surface of Polyhedron.} The faces, edges, and vertices
taken together form the surface of the polyhedron.
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!surface of}%{\Pnum{156}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!of a polyhedron}%{\Pnum{156}}%

\Paragraph{157.}{Polyhedrons Classified According to the Number of Faces.}
\index{Dodecahedron@\textbf{Dodecahedron}}%{\Pnum{157}}%
\index{Icosahedron@\textbf{Icosahedron}}%{\Pnum{157}}%
\index{Octahedron@\textbf{Octahedron}}%{\Pnum{157}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!classified}%{\Pnum{157}}%
\index{Tetrahedron@\textbf{Tetrahedron}}%{\Pnum{157}}%
A polyhedron of four faces is a \emph{tetrahedron}, one of six faces is
a \emph{hexahedron}, one of eight faces an \emph{octahedron}, one of twelve
faces a \emph{dodecahedron}, and one of twenty faces an \emph{icosahedron}.

\begin{Remark}
These names are all derived from the Greek and refer to the number of
faces. Compare other words derived from the same Greek roots such as
hexagon, octagon, dodecagon, etc.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] What is the smallest number of polygons which maybe used to
inclose a portion of space?

\Indent\begin{Suggestion}
Remembering that a triangle is regarded
as a polygon, consider a triangular pyramid.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\item[2.] How many edges and how many vertices has a
tetrahedron?

\Indent\item[3.] How many faces, edges, and vertices has a hexahedron?
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{058b}{1.25in}}

\Erratum{err058a}%
\begin{Suggestion}
Consider a cube.\footnoteTrans{See \Tref{058a}{Transcriber's Notes}.}
%[** PP: Mathematical error]
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Sightwork}

\Section{MODELS OF THE REGULAR POLYHEDRONS}

\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!regular}%{\Pnum{158}}%
\index{Regular@\textbf{Regular}!polyhedrons}%{\Pnum{158}}%
\index{Tetrahedron@\textbf{Tetrahedron}!regular}%{\Pnum{158}}%
angles are equal and whose faces are equal regular polygons is
a \emph{regular polyhedron}. The following are regular polyhedrons:

\begin{Sightwork}

\Page{51}%
\item[1.] What is the smallest possible number of face angles in a polyhedral
angle?

\item[2.] How many faces meet in a vertex of a regular tetrahedron?
What is the sum of the face angles forming one of its polyhedral angles?

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{060abc}{\textwidth}
\end{center}

\item[3.] How many faces meet in a vertex of a regular octahedron? in a
regular icosahedron?

\item[4.] What is the sum of the face angles forming a polyhedral angle in
a regular octahedron? in a regular icosahedron?

\item[5.] Why may not six or more equilateral triangles be used to form a
polyhedral angle?

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\item[6.] What is the sum of the face angles forming
a polyhedral angle of a cube (hexahedron)?

\Indent\item[7.] Why may not four or more squares be used
to form a polyhedral angle?

% [** Illustration]

\Indent\item[8.] How many faces meet in a vertex of a
regular dodecahedron? What is the sum of the
face angles forming one of its polyhedral angles?

\Indent\item[9.] Why may not more than three regular pentagons
be used to form a polyhedral angle?
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{-0.25in}{\smash{\Input{060de}{1.25in}}}

\Indent\item[10.] May regular hexagons be used to form a polyhedral
angle? Why?

\item[11.] May any regular polygons of more than six
sides be used to form polyhedral angles? Why?

\item[12.] Can we show that not more than three different
regular polyhedrons may be formed having
triangles as their faces?

\item[13.] How many regular polyhedrons may be formed having squares as
their faces? How many having pentagons as their faces?
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 061.png---Folio 53-------

\Section{THE NUMBER OF REGULAR POLYHEDRONS}
\SecToc{Construction of Regular Polyhedrons}

\begin{Theorem}{160.}{I.}
There are exactly five  regular
polyhedrons.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: On page~\Pgref{50} it was shown how models of five different
regular polyhedrons may be made.

Hence there are at least five such polyhedrons.

That there cannot be more than five regular polyhedrons
follows from the two propositions:

\begin{Remark}
(a)~\textit{Every polyhedral angle has at least three face angles}.

(b)~\textit{The sum of the face angles of a polyhedral angle is less than~$360$}.

From these propositions it follows that each of the polyhedral angles of
a regular polyhedron may be formed by three, four, or five (but not six)
equilateral triangles; by three (but not four) squares; or by three (but
not four) regular pentagons. Regular polygons having more than five
sides cannot form a polyhedral angle.
\end{Remark}

The details of the proof are left to the student. The sight
work on page~\Pgref{51} will furnish suggestions.

\Paragraph{161.}{Construction of Regular Polyhedrons.} The regular polyhedrons
\index{Regular@\textbf{Regular}!polyhedrons}%{\Pnum{161}}%
\index{Regular@\textbf{Regular}!tetrahedrons}%{\Pnum{161}}%
may be constructed by use of ~\Sref{130}. The construction
for the tetrahedron and octahedron are given below.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent(1)~\textbf{The regular tetrahedron}.\quad At the center~$E$ of
an equilateral triangle~$ABC$ erect a perpendicular
to the plane of the triangle. On this take a point~$D$
so that $AD = AC$.

Then the four triangles, $ABC$, $ACD$, $ABD$, $BCD$,
are \emph{regular} and equal, and the four trihedral angles
are equal. Why?
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{061a}{1.25in}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent(2)~\textbf{The regular octahedron}.\quad Through the center
$O$ of a square~$ABCD$ draw a perpendicular to the
plane of the square and on it take points $E$ and~$F$
such that $AF = AE = AB$. Join $E$ and~$F$ to each
of the four vertices, $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$.

Then the eight faces are equal regular triangles,
and the six polyhedral angles are equal.
Why?
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{-0.125in}{\smash{\Input{061b}{1.5in}}}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 062.png---Folio 54-------

\Chapter{BOOK III}{Prisms and Cylinders}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{162.}{Closed Prismatic Surface.} Given a convex polygon and
\index{Prismatic@\textbf{Prismatic surface}}%{\Pnum{162}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!prismatic}%{\Pnum{162}}%
a straight line not in its plane. If the
line moves so as to remain parallel to
itself while it touches the boundary of
the polygon and is made to traverse it
completely, the line is said to generate
a \emph{closed prismatic surface}.

\Indent\Paragraph{163.}{Generator. Element.} The moving
\index{Generator@\textbf{Generator}!of a prismatic surface}%{\Pnum{163}}%
\index{Prismatic@\textbf{Prismatic surface}!generator of}%{\Pnum{163}}%
line is the \emph{generator} of the surface,
and the generator in any one of its positions
is an \emph{element} of the surface.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{062a}{1.5in}

\Paragraph{164.}{Prism.} A polyhedron bounded by a prismatic surface
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}}%{\Pnum{164}}%
\index{Section@\textbf{Section}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{164}}%
and two parallel plane sections cutting all
its elements is called a \emph{prism}.

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{165.}{Bases. Lateral Surface. Edges.}
\index{Base@\textbf{Base}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{165}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!bases of}%{\Pnum{165}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!lateral edges of}%{\Pnum{165}}%
\index{Lateral@\textbf{Lateral}!edges}%{\Pnum{165}}%
\index{Lateral@\textbf{Lateral}!faces}%{\Pnum{165}}%
\index{Lateral@\textbf{Lateral}!surfaces}%{\Pnum{165}}%
The two parallel cross-sections which
bound a prism are its \emph{bases} and the other
faces form its \emph{lateral surface}. The \emph{edges}
are the lines in which its lateral faces meet.

\Indent\Paragraph{166.}{Right Section. Right Prism.} A \emph{right section} of a prism
cuts all its edges at right angles. A \emph{right prism}
is one whose bases are right sections.
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!right}%{\Pnum{166}}%
\index{Right@\textbf{Right}!prism}%{\Pnum{166}}%
\index{Right@\textbf{Right}!section}%{\Pnum{166}}%
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{062b}{1.25in}

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{167.}{1.}
The lateral faces of a
prism are parallelograms.
\end{Corollary}

\Indent\begin{Corollary}{168.}{2.}
The lateral edges of a
prism are equal and parallel.
\end{Corollary}

\Indent\begin{Corollary}{169.}{3.}
The lateral faces of a right prism are
rectangles.
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{062c}{1in}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 063.png---Folio 55-------

\Section{CLASSIFICATION OF PRISMS}
\SecToc{Properties of Prisms}

\Paragraph{170.}{Kinds of Prisms.} Prisms are classified according to
the form of their right sections, as \emph{triangular}, \emph{quadrangular},
\emph{pentagonal}, \emph{hexagonal}, etc. A \emph{regular prism} is one whose right
section is a regular polygon.
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!hexagonal}%{\Pnum{170}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!quadrangular}%{\Pnum{170}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!regular}%{\Pnum{170}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!triangular}%{\Pnum{170}}%
\index{Regular@\textbf{Regular}!prisms}%{\Pnum{170}}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-3in}
\Indent\Paragraph{171.}{Altitude. Area.} The \emph{altitude} of a prism is the
\index{Altitude@\textbf{Altitude}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{171}}%
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{171}}%
\index{Faces@\textbf{Faces}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{171}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!altitude of}%{\Pnum{171}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!area of}%{\Pnum{171}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!lateral faces of}%{\Pnum{171}}%
perpendicular distance between
the planes of its
bases. The altitude of a
right prism is equal to its
edge.

\Indent The \emph{lateral area} of a
prism is the sum of the
areas of its lateral faces.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{063abc}{3in}

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent The \emph{total area} is the sum of its lateral area and the areas
of its bases.

\Indent\Paragraph{172.}{Truncated Prism.} A polyhedron which
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!added}%{\Pnum{172}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!truncated}%{\Pnum{172}}%
\index{Truncated@\textbf{Truncated}!prism}%{\Pnum{172}}%
is a part of a prism cut off by a plane meeting
all the lateral edges, but not parallel to
the base, is called a \emph{truncated prism}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{063d}{1in}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent Two polyhedrons are said to be \emph{added} when
they are placed so that a face of one coincides
wholly or in part with a face
of the other, but otherwise
each lies outside the other.

\Indent\Paragraph{173.}{Parallelopiped.} A parallelopiped
is a prism whose
bases, as well as lateral faces,
are parallelograms.
\index{Parallelopiped@\textbf{Parallelopiped}!rectangular}%{\Pnum{173}}%
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.5in}{\smash{\Input{063e}{2in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent A \emph{rectangular parallelopiped} has its bases and
all its faces rectangles.

\Indent A \emph{cube} is a parallelopiped whose bases and
faces are all squares.
\index{Cube@\textbf{Cube}}%{\Pnum{173}}%
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{063f}{1in}}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 064.png---Folio 56-------

\Section{PARALLEL CROSS-SECTIONS OF A PRISM ARE EQUAL}

\begin{Theorem}{174.}{I.}
The cross-sections of a prism
made by parallel planes are equal polygons.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{064ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given a prism cut by two parallel planes forming the polygons
$ABCDE$ and $A'B'C'D'E'$.}
{To prove that $ABCDE = A'B'C'D'E'$.}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: (1)~Show that $AB = A'B'$, $BC = B'C'$, etc.,
by proving that $ABB'A'$, $BCC'B'$, etc., are $\Pgrams$.

(2)~Show that $\angle ABC = \angle A'B'C'$, $\angle BCD = \angle B'C'D'$, etc.

(3)~Hence show that $ABCDE$ and~$A'B'C'D'E'$ can be made
to coincide.

\begin{Corollary}{175.}{}
Every cross-section of a prism parallel to
the base is equal to the base.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Can a parallelopiped be a \emph{right} prism without being a \emph{rectangular}
parallelopiped? Illustrate.

\item[2.] Show that in a rectangular parallelopiped each edge is perpendicular
to the other edges which meet it.

\item[3.] Show that any section of a prism made by a plane parallel to an edge
is a parallelogram.

\begin{Suggestion}
Use in order ~\Sref{89}, \Sref{84}, and~\Sref{92}.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[4.] Is it possible to construct a prism such that there is no plane perpendicular
to its edges and cutting all of them unless some of them are
extended?

\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 065.png---Folio 57-------

\Section{THE LATERAL AREA OF A PRISM}

\begin{Theorem}{176.}{II.}
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!area of}%{\Pnum{176}}%
The lateral area of a prism is
equal to the product of a lateral edge and the perimeter
of a right section.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{065ab}{}
\end{center}

\begin{Suggestion}
Show that the lateral edges are mutually equal and that
the area of each face is the product of a lateral edge and one side of
the right-section polygon.

Complete the proof.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1in}
\Indent\begin{Remark}
\textit{Note}. The form of statement in this theorem is the usual abbreviation
for the more \emph{precise} form:

\Indent The lateral area of a prism is equal to the product of the \emph{numerical
measures} of a lateral edge and the perimeter of  a right
section.

\Indent Similar abbreviations are used throughout this text.
\end{Remark}

\Indent\begin{Corollary}{177.}{}
The lateral area of a right
prism is equal to the product of its altitude and the
perimeter of its base.
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{065c}{1in}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If the lateral edge of a prism is $8$~inches and the perimeter of its
right section $30$~inches, what is the lateral area?

\item[2.] Of all prisms having an altitude $6$~inches and the perimeter of a
right section $24$~inches, which one has the smallest lateral area?

\begin{Suggestion}
Which one has the shortest lateral edge?
\end{Suggestion}

\item[3.] Why is not the lateral area of an oblique prism determined by the
perimeter of the base and a lateral edge?
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 066.png---Folio 58-------

\Section{CONDITIONS MAKING PRISMS EQUAL}

\begin{Theorem}{178.}{III.}
Two prisms are equal if three
faces having a common vertex in the one are equal
respectively to three faces having a common vertex in
the other, and similarly placed.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{066ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the three faces meeting at~$B$ in prism~$P$ equal respectively
to the three faces meeting at~$B'$ in prism~$P'$, and similarly placed.}
{To prove that~$P$ can be made to coincide with $P'$.}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: Trihedral $\Angles B$ and~$B'$ are equal. Why?

Now apply the two prisms, making $B$ coincide with $B'$, and
then show in detail that:

(1)~The lower bases coincide.

(2)~The lateral faces at $B$ and $B'$ coincide.

(3)~The upper bases coincide.

(4)~All the lateral faces coincide.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.25in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{179.}{1.}
Two truncated prisms are equal under the
conditions of \upshape~\Sref{178}.
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustrations]
\hfill\smash{\Input{066cd}{2in}}

\begin{Corollary}{180.}{2.}
Two right prisms are equal if they have
equal bases and equal altitudes.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 067.png---Folio 59-------

\Section{OPPOSITE FACES OF A PARALLELOPIPED}

\begin{Theorem}{181.}{IV.}
Opposite faces of a parallelopiped
are equal and parallel.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{067a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}

\begin{Suggestion}[s]
Consider the opposite faces $ABFE$ and $DCGH$.

(1)~Show that the sides of the angles $ABF$ and~$DCG$ are parallel, and
hence that the planes determined by them are parallel.

(2)~Show that these faces are equal.

In like manner argue about any other pair of opposite faces.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{182.}{}
Any section of a
parallelopiped made by a plane cutting
four parallel edges is a parallelogram.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Suggestion}
In the figure show that $RQ \parallel ST$
and $QT \parallel RS$.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{067b}{1.25in}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] A section made by a plane passed through two diagonally opposite
edges of a parallelopiped is a parallelogram, for example, the section
through $DH$ and $BF$ in the figure of ~\Sref{181}.

\item[2.] Show that any two of the four diagonals of a parallelopiped bisect
each other.

\begin{Suggestion}
Make use of example~1.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[3.] If the parallelopiped in ~\Sref{182} is rectangular, is the section necessarily
a rectangle? May it be so?

\item[4.] Show that the section of a cube made by a plane through two diagonally
opposite edges is a rectangle. Why can it not be a square? Can
you construct a parallelopiped in which such a section would be a square?

\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 068.png---Folio 60-------

\Section{PLANE THROUGH OPPOSITE EDGES OF A PARALLELOPIPED}

\begin{Theorem}{183.}{V.}
A plane through diagonally
opposite edges of a parallelopiped divides it into two
triangular prisms having equal right sections and equal
lateral edges.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{068a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the parallelopiped $ABCD-F$ with opposite edges $AE$
and $CG$.}
{To prove that the plane through $AE$ and $CG$ divides it into
two triangular prisms having equal lateral edges and equal right
sections.}

\Proof: By ~\Sref{182} the right section $KLMO$ of the given prism
is a parallelogram. Hence $KM$ divides it into two equal
triangles. Hence the right sections of the prisms $ABC-F$ and
$ACD-H$ are equal.

The lateral edges $BF$ and $DH$ are equal by ~\Sref{168}.

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If a right section of a prismatic space is a parallelogram, what kind
of prism will be cut out by two right sections?

\item[2.] If a right section of a prismatic space is a rectangle, what kind of
prism will be cut out by two right sections?

\item[3.] If a right section of a prismatic space is a regular hexagon, what
kind of prism will be cut out by two right sections?

\item[4.] What kind of prism will be cut out by two right sections of any
closed prismatic surface?

\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 069.png---Folio 61-------

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] The face angles of one trihedral angle of a parallelopiped
are $65$, $70$, and~$75$ respectively. What are the face angles of
the other trihedral angles?

\item[2.] Show that two prisms cut from the same prismatic
space and having equal lateral edges have equal lateral areas.

\item[3.] Show that the diagonals of a rectangular
parallelopiped are all equal to each other.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[4.] Show that the square on the diagonal of
a rectangular parallelopiped is equal to the
sum of the squares on the sides meeting at a
vertex from which it is drawn.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{069a}{1.375in}}

\begin{Remark}
E.g.~in the figure $\overline{AG}^2 = \overline{AC}^2 + \overline{CG}^2 = \overline{AB}^2 + \overline{BC}^2 + \overline{CG}^2$.
\end{Remark}

\item[5.] Find the ratio of the diagonal to one edge of a cube.

\item[6.] Find the edge of a cube whose diagonal is $14$~inches.
Find the diagonal of a cube whose edge is $16$~inches.

\item[7.] Find a diagonal of a rectangular parallelopiped whose
edges are $6$, $8$, and~$10$ inches respectively.

\item[8.] Are the diagonals of a cube perpendicular to each other?

\item[9.] In a rectangular parallelopiped the diagonal of the base
is $12$~in.\ and the altitude is $5$~in. Find the diagonal of the
parallelopiped.

\item[10.] If two equal right prisms whose bases are equilateral
triangles are placed together so as to form one prism whose
base is a parallelogram, compare the lateral area of the prism
so formed with the sum of the lateral areas of the original
prisms.

\item[11.] A right prism whose bases are regular hexagons is
divided into six prisms whose bases are equilateral triangles.
Compare the lateral area of the original prism with the sum of
the lateral areas of the resulting prisms.

\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 070.png---Folio 62-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A RECTANGULAR PARALLELOPIPED}

\Paragraph{184.}{} Thus far certain properties of prisms have been studied,
\index{Parallelopiped@\textbf{Parallelopiped}!volume of}%{\Pnum{184}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!volume of}%{\Pnum{184}}%
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{184}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{184}}%
but no attempt has been made to \emph{measure the space occupied} by
such a solid. For this purpose we consider first a rectangular
parallelopiped.

\Paragraph{185.}{Numerical Measure.} In case each edge of a rectangular
parallelopiped is commensurable with a unit segment, the number
of times which a unit cube is contained in it is the \emph{numerical
measure} or the \emph{volume} of the parallelopiped.

\Paragraph{186.}{The Commensurable Case.} In the commensurable
case just described, the volume
is easily computed.

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.375in}
\Indent\begin{Remark}
E.g.~if in the figure one edge $AC$ is $4$~units, and
an adjoining edge $AB$ is $3$~units, then a cube as $AK$,
whose edge is one unit, may be laid off $4$~times along
$AC$ and a tier of $3\cdot 4 = 12$ such cubes will adjoin
the face~$AD$. Since the edge~$BE$ is $5$~units long, $5$
such tiers will exactly fill the space within the solid. That is, $5\cdot 3\cdot 4 = 60$
is the number of cubic units in the solid.
\end{Remark}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{070a}{1.25in}

Hence in this case
\[
\textit{Volume} = \textit{Length}\/  \textit{Width}\/  \textit{Height}.
\]

\begin{Remark}
Again, if the given dimensions are $3.4$, $2.6$, $4.5$ decimeters respectively,
then unit cubes, with edge one \emph{decimeter}, cannot be made to fill exactly
the space inclosed by the figure, but cubes with edge each one \emph{centimeter}
will do so, giving $34$, $26$, and~$45$ respectively along the three edges of the
figure.

Therefore, the volume is

$34\cdot 26\cdot 45 = 39,780$ cubic centimeters, or $39.78$ cubic decimeters.
\end{Remark}

Hence in this case also
\[
\textit{Volume} = \textit{Length}\/  \textit{Width}\/  \textit{Height}.
\]

\Paragraph{187.}{Formula for Volume of a Rectangular Solid.} We therefore
conclude that the volume of a rectangular parallelopiped with
commensurable dimensions is given by the formula
\[
\textbf{Volume} = \textbf{Length}  \textbf{Width}  \textbf{Height}.
\]

\NewFolio %% -----File: 071.png---Folio 63-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A RECTANGULAR SOLID}

\Paragraph{188.}{The Incommensurable Case.} If a rectangular parallelopiped
\index{Incommensurables@\textbf{Incommensurables}}%{\Pnum{188}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!volume of}%{\Pnum{188}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{188}}%
is such that any two of its dimensions are incommensurable
with each other, then there is no unit cube, however
small, in terms of which the volume can be exactly expressed.

But by choosing a unit cube sufficiently small we may determine
the volume of a rectangular solid which differs from the
given one by as little as we please.

\begin{Remark}
E.g.,~if the length is $5$~inches and the width is $\sqrt{3}$~inches, then the
base cannot be exactly covered with equal cubes, however small.

But since $\sqrt{3} = 1.732\dots$, if we take as a unit of volume a cube one one-hundredth
of an inch on a side, then the base of a rectangular solid whose
length is $5$~inches and whose width is $1.73$~inches can be exactly covered
with a layer of these cubes and the number of cubes in such a layer is
\[
500  173 = 86,500.
\]

If the height is $2$~inches, then $200$~layers will just reach the top, making
\[
500  173  200 = 17,300,000~\text{cubes}.
\]

And since $1,000,000$ such small cubes make a cubic inch, the volume
of this solid is $17.3$~cubic inches.

The portion of the given solid not thus filled is $5$~inches long, $2$~inches
high and less than $.003$ of an inch in thickness.

Hence, its volume is less than $5\times 2\times .003 = .03$ of a cubic inch.

By expressing $\sqrt{3}$ to further places of decimals and then using smaller
and smaller units of volume, successive rectangular solids may be found
which differ less and less from the given solid.
\end{Remark}

The foregoing considerations constitute an informal proof of
the following theorem:

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\begin{Theorem}{189.}{VI.}
The volume
of a rectangular parallelopiped is
equal to the product of its length,
width, and height.
\end{Theorem}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.125in}{\Input{071a}{1.5in}}}
\[
\text{\bfseries In symbols},\  V = l\cdot w\cdot h.
\]

\begin{Remark}
The above argument shows that this theorem holds for all rectangular
parallelopipeds used in the process of approximation, and hence it applies
to all practical measurements of the volumes of such solids.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 072.png---Folio 64-------

\begin{Corollary}{190.}{1.}
The  volume of a rectangular parallelopiped
is equal to the product of the numerical measures of its base
and altitude.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{191.}{2.}
If two rectangular parallelopipeds have
two dimensions respectively equal to each other, their volumes are
in the same ratio as their third dimensions; and if they have one
dimension the same in each, their volumes are in the same ratio
as the products of their other two dimensions.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Remark}
For if $V$ and $V'$ are the volumes, and $a$, $b$, $c$, and $a'$, $b'$, $c'$ the dimensions,
then $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{a\cdot b\cdot c} {a'\cdot b'\cdot c'} = \dfrac{a}{a'}$ if $b = b'$ and $c = c'$; or $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{a\cdot b}{a'\cdot b'}$ if $c = c'$.
\end{Remark}
\medskip % [** PP: Manually-added space]

\Paragraph{192.}{Volumes of Prisms in General.} From the formula for
volumes of rectangular solids, $V = l\cdot w\cdot h$, we deduce the volumes
of prisms in general by means of the principle:

\textit{Two polyhedrons have the same volume if they can be made to
coincide, or if they can be divided into parts which can be made to
coincide in pairs}.
\index{Equal@\textbf{Equal}!volumes}%{\Pnum{192}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!equal, equivalent}%{\Pnum{192}}%
\index{Solids@\textbf{Solids}!equal}%{\Pnum{192}}%
\index{Solids@\textbf{Solids}!equivalent}%{\Pnum{192}}%
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!of a prism}%{\Pnum{192}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{192}}%

\begin{Remark}
The sign ${}={}$ between two polyhedrons means that they are equal in
all respects; that is, can be made to coincide. The word \emph{equivalent} is
used to mean \emph{equal in volume}.
\index{Equivalent@\textbf{Equivalent solids}}%{\Pnum{192}}%
\end{Remark}

\begin{Exercises}

\Page{63}%
\item[1.] The dimensions in inches of a rectangular parallelopiped
are $5$, $10$, and~$2\sqrt{2}$. Taking the approximate value of~$\sqrt{2}$ as
$1.4142$, find the approximate volume. Show that this differs
from the exact volume by less than $.001$ of a cubic inch, it being
given that $\sqrt{2}$ lies between $1.41421$ and~$1.41422$.

\item[2.] If the dimensions in inches of a rectangular parallelopiped
are $5$, $4\sqrt{2}$, and~$5\sqrt{3}$, find how near an approximation
to the volume is possible by taking $\sqrt{2}$ between $1.414$ and~$1.4143$
and $\sqrt{3}$ between $1.732$ and~$1.7321$.

\item[3.] If the dimensions in inches of a rectangular parallelopiped
are $3\sqrt{2}$, $3\sqrt{3}$, $2\sqrt{5}$, find the values to be used for $\sqrt{2}$, $\sqrt{3}$, $\sqrt{5}$,
to obtain the volume within $.001$ of a cubic inch.
\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 073.png---Folio 65-------

\Section{VOLUME OF AN OBLIQUE PRISM}

\begin{Theorem}{193.}{VII.}
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!volume of}%{\Pnum{193}}%
The volume of an oblique
prism is equal to that of a right prism having for its
base a right section of the oblique prism and for its
altitude a lateral edge of the oblique prism.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{073ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

\Objective{Given the oblique prism $AD'$ with $FGHJK$ a right section and
$F'G'H'J'K'$ a right section of the prism extended so that $AA' = KK'$.}
{To prove that the oblique prism $AD'$ has the same volume as the
right prism $KH'$.}

\Proof: In the truncated prisms $AH$ and~$A'H'$,
base $ABCDE = {}$ base $A'B'C'D'E'$.\hfill ~\Sref{175}

In faces $ABFK$ and $A'B'F'K'$, we have
\begin{flalign*}
&&                 AB &= A'B',\quad \angle 1 = \angle 3,\ \angle 2 = \angle 4, && \tag*{Why?} \\
&\text{and}\quad & AK &= A'K' \text{ and } BF = B'F'. && \tag*{Why?}
\end{flalign*}

$\therefore ABFK$ and $A'B'F'K'$ will coincide and are equal.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Likewise},& AKJE &= A'K'J'E'. && && \\
&&          \therefore\quad AH &= A'H'.     && && \tag*{~\Sref{179}} \\
&\indent\text{But the given prism} & AD' &= AH + KD', && && \\
&\text{and the right prism} & KH' &= A'H' + KD'. && && \\
&&          \therefore\quad AD' &= KH'.     && && \tag*{\QED}
\end{flalign*}

\begin{Corollary}{194.}{}
Two prisms having equal lateral edges
and equal right sections are equal in volume.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 074.png---Folio 66-------

\Section{VOLUME OF ANY PARALLELOPIPED}

\begin{Theorem}{195.}{VIII.}
The volume of any parallelopiped
is equal to the product of its base and altitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{074a}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Objective{Given any parallelopiped $P$ with area of base~$b$ and altitude~$h$.}
{To prove that its volume is equal to $b  h$.}

\Proof: Considering face $AK$ as the base of prism~$P$, produce
the four edges parallel to~$AB$, and lay off $A'B' = AB$.

Through $A'$ and $B'$ erect planes $\perp A'B'$, thus cutting off
the right prism~$Q$ with $A'L$ as one base.

Now considering $CL$ as the base of prism~$Q$, produce the
four edges $\parallel CB'$ and lay off $C'D = CB'$.

At $C'$ and $D$ erect planes $\perp C'D$, cutting off the right
prism~$R$, which is a rectangular parallelopiped with base~$b''$.

Now prove in detail: (1)~$h = h' = h''$,\quad (2)~$b = b' = b''$,
\begin{align*}
(3)\quad\text{Vol.\ } P &= \text{Vol.\ } Q = \text{Vol.\ } R,
\tag*{~\Sref{193}} \\
(4)\quad\text{Vol.\ } R &= b'' h'',\qquad (5)\quad \text{Vol.\ } P = bh.
\end{align*}

\begin{Corollary}{196.}{}
Two parallelopipeds are equal in volume
if they have equal altitudes and bases of equal areas.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 075.png---Folio 67-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A TRIANGULAR PRISM}

\begin{Theorem}{197.}{IX.}
The volume of a triangular
prism is equal to the product of its base and altitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{075a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the triangular prism whose base is~$ABC$.}
{To prove that the volume is equal to the area of $\triangle ABC$ multiplied
by the altitude~$h$.}

\Proof: Complete the $\Pgrams ABCD$ and $EFGH$ and draw~$DH$.
Now show that $CDHG$ and~$ADHE$ are $\Pgrams$, and hence that
$BH$ is a parallelopiped.\hfill \mbox{Use ~\Sref{43}~(2)}

Let $KLMO$ be a right section of~$BH$, and let~$KM$ be the
line in which the plane $ACGE$ cuts the plane $KLMO$.

Then (1)~prism $ABC-F = \tfrac{1}{2}$ prism~$BH$,
\hfill ~\Sref{183},~\Sref{194}

(2)~But prism $BH = h  \text{area of } \Pgram ABCD$,
\hfill ~\Sref{196}

(3)~Hence, prism $ABC - F = h  \tfrac{1}{2} \text{area of } \Pgram ABCD$, that
is, prism $ABC - F = h  \text{area of } \Pgram ABC$.

Therefore the volume of prism $ABC-F$ is equal to the area
of its base times its altitude.
\QED

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Find the volume of a prism whose altitude is $8$~in.\ and whose base
is a right triangle with legs $5$~in.\ and $6$~in.\ respectively.

\item[2.] Find the volume of a right prism whose altitude is $6$~in.\ and whose
base is a rectangle with sides $3$~in.\ and $5$~in.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 076.png---Folio 68-------

\Section{VOLUME OF ANY PRISM}

\begin{Theorem}{198.}{X.}
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!volume of}%{\Pnum{198}}%
The  volume  of any prism is
equal to the product of its base and altitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [**Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{076ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: Any prism, can be divided into triangular
prisms by planes passing through one edge and each of the
other non-adjacent edges.

The altitudes of the triangular prisms are the same as that
of the given prism, and the sum of their bases is equal to the
base of the given prism.

Now use ~\Sref{197} and complete the proof.

\begin{Corollary}{199.}{1.}
The product of the base and altitude of
any prism is equal to the product of a lateral edge and the area
of a right section.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Remark}
For each equals the volume of the prism. See ~\Sref{198},~\Sref{193}.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Corollary}{200.}{2.}
If two prisms have equivalent bases,
their volumes are in the same ratio as their altitudes; and if they
have equal altitudes, their volumes are in the same ratio as the
areas of their bases.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Find the volume of a prism whose altitude is $8$~in.\ and the area of
whose base is $42$~sq.~in.

\item[2.] The volume of a prism is $264$~cu.~in.\ and its altitude is $8$~in. Find
the area of its base.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 077.png---Folio 69-------

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] The theorem that the volume of any prism is equal to
the product of its base and altitude is of great importance.
What theorems of Book~III have been used directly or indirectly
in proving it?

\item[2.] What dimensions of a prism must be known in order to
determine its lateral area by means of the preceding theorems
of Book~III? What dimensions must be known to determine
its volume?

\item[3.] The edge of a cube is~$e$. Find the total surface and the
volume in terms of~$e$.

\item[4.] Find the edge of a cube if its total area is equal numerically
to its volume, an inch being used as the unit.

\item[5.] Find the volume of a regular right triangular prism
whose edges are all equal to $6$~inches.

\item[6.] Find the volume of a regular right hexagonal prism
whose edges are all equal to $10$~inches.

\item[7.] A side of the base of a regular right hexagonal prism is
$3$~inches. Find its altitude if its volume is $54\sqrt{3}$ cubic inches.
What is the total area of this prism?

\item[8.] The volume of a triangular prism is equal to the area of
one lateral face multiplied by half the perpendicular distance
of this face from the remaining edge. Prove.

\item[9.] The volume of a regular right prism is equal to the
lateral area multiplied by half the apothem of the base.
Prove.

\item[10.] Prove that the sum of the squares of the four diagonals
of a rectangular parallelopiped is equal to the sum of the
squares of the twelve edges of the parallelopiped.

\item[11.] A prismatic space is cut by two pairs of parallel planes
which are the same distance apart. Does it follow that the volumes
of the prisms thus formed are equal? The lateral areas?
\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 078.png---Folio 70-------

\Section{CYLINDERS}
\SecToc{Properties of Cylinders}

\Paragraph{201.}{Curved Surface.} A surface no part of which is plane is
called a \emph{curved surface}.
\index{Curved surface@\textbf{Curved surface}}%{\Pnum{201}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!curved}%{\Pnum{201}}%

\begin{Remark}
E.g.~the surface of an eggshell or of a stovepipe is a curved surface.
\end{Remark}

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\Paragraph{202.}{A Closed Plane Curve.} A curve which can be traced continuously
\index{Curves@\textbf{Curves}!convex, closed}%{\Pnum{202}}%
by a point moving in a
plane so as to return to its original
position without crossing its path
is a \emph{closed plane curve}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustrations]
\hfill\smash{\Input{078a}{2in}}

A closed plane curve is \emph{convex}
if it can be cut by a straight line in not more than two
points.\footnoteTrans{See \Tref{078a}{Transcriber's Notes}.}
\Erratum{err078a}%
% [** F1: Modern parsing makes this mathematically incorrect]

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{203.}{A Cylindrical Surface.} If a straight
\index{Cylindrical@\textbf{Cylindrical surface}}%{\Pnum{203}}%
\index{Cylindrical@\textbf{Cylindrical surface}!element of}%{\Pnum{203}}%
\index{Cylindrical@\textbf{Cylindrical surface}!generator of}%{\Pnum{203}}%
\index{Generator@\textbf{Generator}!of a cylindrical surface}%{\Pnum{203}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!cylindrical}%{\Pnum{203}}%
line moves so as to remain parallel to
itself, while it always touches a closed
convex plane curve and is made to traverse
it completely, the line is said to
generate a \emph{closed convex cylindrical surface}.
The moving line is the \emph{generator}, and the
generator in any one of its positions is an
\emph{element} of the surface.

\Indent\Paragraph{204.}{Cylinder.} A solid bounded by a
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!bases of}%{\Pnum{204}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!right}%{\Pnum{204}}%
\index{Section@\textbf{Section}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{204}}%
cylindrical surface and two parallel plane
sections cutting all its elements is called
a \emph{cylinder}.

\Indent\Paragraph{205.}{Bases. Lateral Surface.} The two
\index{Base@\textbf{Base}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{205}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!bases of}%{\Pnum{205}}%
parallel cross-sections which bound a cylinder
are its \emph{bases}, and the curved surface
is its \emph{lateral surface}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{078bc}{1.25in}

% [** Illustration]

\Paragraph{206.}{Element. Altitude.} That part of the generator of a
\index{Altitude@\textbf{Altitude}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{206}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!element of}%{\Pnum{206}}%
\index{Element@\textbf{Element}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{206}}%
cylindrical surface which is included between the bases of a
cylinder is called an \emph{element} of the cylinder. The \emph{altitude} of
a cylinder is the perpendicular distance between its bases.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 079.png---Folio 71-------

\Section{CIRCULAR CYLINDERS}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{207.}{Right Section.} A \emph{right section of a cylinder} is made by
\index{Right@\textbf{Right}!section}%{\Pnum{207}}%
a plane cutting each of its elements at right angles.

\Indent\Paragraph{208.}{Circular Cylinders.} A \emph{circular
cylinder} is one whose right section is
a circle.
\index{Circular@\textbf{Circular}!cylinder}%{\Pnum{208}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!circular}%{\Pnum{208}}%
\index{Radius@\textbf{Radius}!of a circular cylinder}%{\Pnum{208}}%
\index{Right@\textbf{Right}!cylinder}%{\Pnum{208}}%
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{079a}{1.5in}}

\Indent The radius of a circular cylinder is
the radius of its right section.

A cylinder whose elements are at
right angles to its bases is called a
\emph{right cylinder}. Otherwise it is an \emph{oblique cylinder}. A right
cylinder whose bases are circles is a \emph{right
circular cylinder}.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{209.}{Axis of a Cylinder.} The line passing
\index{Axis@\textbf{Axis}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{209}}%
through the centers of two right sections of
a circular cylinder is the \emph{axis} of the cylinder.

\Indent\begin{Remark}
A right circular cylinder may be generated by
revolving a rectangle about one of its sides as an
axis. The side opposite the axis generates the lateral surface, and the
sides adjacent to the axis generate the bases.
\end{Remark}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{079b}{1in}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] What is the locus of all points in space which are at a perpendicular
distance of $6$~in.\ from a straight line $10$~in.\ long?
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}}%

\item[2.] Show that any two elements of a cylinder determine a plane section
of the cylinder which cuts the two bases in parallel lines.

\item[3.] If every plane determined by two elements of a cylinder is perpendicular
to its bases, what kind of cylinder is it?

\item[4.] If one right section of a cylinder is a circle, what would appear to
be true of all its right sections? For proof see ~\Sref{213}.

\item[5.] If a circular cylinder is oblique, are its bases circles?

\item[6.] If an oblique cylinder has a circular base, is its right section a
circle?
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 080.png---Folio 72-------

\Section{LONGITUDINAL SECTIONS OF A CYLINDER}

\begin{Theorem}{210.}{XI.}
If a plane contains an element
of a cylinder and meets it in any other point, then it
contains another element also, and the section is a
parallelogram.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{080ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the cylinder $AC$ and a plane~$M$ containing the element~$AD$
and another point as $P$ or~$P'$.}
{To prove that it contains another element and that the section
containing these two elements is a parallelogram.}

\Proof: \Case{(1)}{When the point~$P$ is in the lateral surface.}

Let $BC$ be the element through~$P$.

Then $BC\parallel AD$ and they determine a plane~$M$.\hfill ~\Sref{203},~\Sref{73}

Also $AB\parallel DC$ and $ABCD$ is a parallelogram.\hfill ~\Sref{92}

\Case{(2)}{When the point $P'$ is in one of the bases.}

Draw $AB$ through $P'$. Then by~(1) the plane~$M$ contains
the element~$BC$ and the section is a
parallelogram.
\QED

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{211.}{Plane Tangent to a Cylinder.} If a
\index{Tangent@\textbf{Tangent}!to a cylinder}%{\Pnum{211}}%
plane contains an element of a cylinder
and no other point in it, the plane is said
to be \emph{tangent to the cylinder}, and the element
is called the \emph{line of contact}.

\Indent It may be noted that a cross-section of a cylinder and its
tangent plane consists of a plane curve and a line tangent to it.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{080c}{1.25in}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 081.png---Folio 73-------

\Section{THE BASES OF A CYLINDER ARE EQUAL}

\begin{Theorem}{212.}{XII.}
The bases of a cylinder are
equal plane figures.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{081ab}{0.6\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given a cylinder with the bases $b$ and $b'$.}
{To prove that $b = b'$.}

\Proof: Take any three points $A$, $B$, $C$ in the rim of the base~$b$
and draw elements through these points, meeting the base~$b'$
in $D$, $E$, $F$.

Show that $\triangle ABC = \triangle DEF$.\hfill Use~~\Sref{210},~\Sref{27}

Now, while the elements $AD$ and $BE$ remain fixed, conceive
$CF$ to generate the surface of the cylinder.

Evidently $\triangle ABC = \triangle DEF$ in every position of $CF$.

Hence, if base~$b'$ is applied to base~$b$ with these triangles
coinciding in \emph{one} position, they will coincide in every position
corresponding to the moving generator.

That is, $b'$ coincides with~$b$.
\QED

\begin{Remark}
The proposition of ~\Sref{212} may also be stated in the form of the following
corollary:
\end{Remark}

\begin{Corollary}{213.}{1.}
Parallel plane sections of a cylinder are
equal, if they cut all the elements.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{214.}{2.}
The axis of a circular cylinder passes
through the center of all its right sections.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 082.png---Folio 74-------

\Section{MEASURING THE SURFACE AND VOLUME OF A CYLINDER}

\Paragraph{215.}{Area of a Curved Surface.} Thus far in geometry the
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a curved surface}%{\Pnum{215}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{215}}%
word \emph{area} has been used in connection with plane figures only.
In some cases the computation of an area has been made by
an approximation process only, as in the case of some rectangles
and of the circle. Indeed, in these cases approximate measurement
only is possible, since no square unit exists in terms
of which such areas can be \emph{exactly} measured.

In the case of \emph{any curved surface} it is evident that approximate
measurement is the \emph{only kind possible} in terms of a plane
area unit, since no such unit, however small, can be made to
coincide with a part of such a surface.\footnoteTrans%
  {See \Tref{082a}{Transcriber's Notes}.}
\Erratum{err082a} % [** PP: Subtle geometric issue]

\Paragraph{216.}{Volume of a Solid Having a Curved Surface.} Since the
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{216}}%
unit of measure for solids is the cube, it is evident that a solid
having a curved surface cannot contain such a unit an integral
number of times. Hence approximate measurement only of
the volumes of such solids is possible.

\begin{Remark}
To measure approximately the area and volume of a cylinder use is
made of inscribed and circumscribed prisms.
\end{Remark}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{217.}{Inscribed Prisms.} A prism is said to be \emph{inscribed in a
cylinder} if its lateral edges are elements of the
cylinder, and their bases lie in the same
planes.
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!cylinder}%{\Pnum{217}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!circumscribed}%{\Pnum{217}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!prism}%{\Pnum{217}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{217}}%

\Indent\Paragraph{218.}{Circumscribed Prisms.} A prism is said
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!prism}%{\Pnum{218}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{218}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!cylinder}%{\Pnum{218}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!circumscribed}%{\Pnum{218}}%
to be \emph{circumscribed about a cylinder} if its
lateral faces are all tangent to the cylinder,
and their bases lie in the same planes.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{082a}{1.25in}

\begin{Remark}
It is evident that by increasing the number of lateral faces of the inscribed
or circumscribed prisms the surface of the latter may be made to lie
as near the surface of the cylinder as we please. The lateral edges of the
prisms will remain equal to an element of the cylinder, while the right
sections of the prisms can be made to differ as little as we please from the
right section of the cylinder.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 083.png---Folio 75-------

\Section{LATERAL AREA OF A CYLINDER}

\Paragraph{219.}{Fundamental Assumption on the Area and Volume of a
Cylinder.} We now assume that
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{219}}%
\index{Axioms@\textbf{Axioms}}%{\Pnum{219}}%
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!lateral surface of}%{\Pnum{219}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{219}}%

\textit{A cylinder has a definite lateral area and a definite volume
which may be approximated as nearly as we please by taking the
lateral areas and the volumes of successive inscribed or circumscribed
prisms}.

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{083ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Paragraph{220.}{Lateral Area of a Cylinder.} Since \textit{the lateral area of a
prism is equal to the product of a lateral edge and the perimeter
of a right section} (~\Sref{176}), it follows that this theorem holds for
every inscribed or circumscribed prism used in approximating
the lateral area of a cylinder.

The foregoing considerations constitute an informal proof of
the following theorem:

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\begin{Theorem}{221.}{XIII.}
The lateral area
of a cylinder is equal to the product of an
element of the cylinder and the perimeter
of a right section.
\end{Theorem}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration] % \smash *after* \raise
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.375in}{\Input{083c}{1.5in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{222.}{1.}
If $r$ is the radius of a
right section of a circular cylinder, and~$e$ is an element,
the lateral surface is $S = 2 \pi re$ \Inum{(~\Sref{176})}.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{223.}{2.}
If~$r$ is the radius and~$h$ is the
altitude of a right circular cylinder, then the lateral
surface is $S = 2 \pi rh$ \Inum{(~\Sref{177})}.
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.25in}{\Input{083d}{1in}}}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 084.png---Folio 76-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A CYLINDER}

\Paragraph{224.}{} Since \textit{the volume of a prism is equal to the product of its
altitude and the area of its base}, it follows that this theorem
holds for every inscribed or circumscribed prism used in approximating
the volume of a cylinder.
\index{Cylinder@\textbf{Cylinder}!volume of}%{\Pnum{224}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{224}}%

\begin{Remark}
\emph{All practical computations} of the surfaces and the volumes of cylinders
are \emph{approximations} based upon the propositions stated in ~\Sref{220},~\Sref{224}.
\end{Remark}

The foregoing constitutes an informal proof of the following
theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{225.}{XIV.}
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!of a cylinder}%{\Pnum{272}}%
The volume of a cylinder is
equal to the product of its altitude and the area of its
base.
\end{Theorem}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{226.}{1.}
If $r_1$ is the radius of the right
section of a circular cylinder and $e$ is an element, then
the volume is \Inum{(~\Sref{199})} $V = \pi r_1^2 e$.
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.25in}{\Input{084a}{1in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
