\Indent\begin{Corollary}{227.}{2.}
If a cylinder of altitude~$h$
has a circular base whose radius is~$r_2$, then the volume
is
\[
V = \pi r_2^2 h.
\]
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\smash{\raisebox{0.125in}{\Input{084b}{1in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{228.}{3.}
If $h$ is the altitude and $r$ the
radius of the base of a right circular cylinder, then
the volume is $V = \pi r^2h$.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Remark}
In this case $r = r_1 = r_2$, and $e = h$, and the formulas of
corollaries~1 and~2 become identical.
\end{Remark}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{084c}{1in}}

{\small%
\textit{Note}. The theorems of ~\Sref{221}, \Sref{225} were stated (and are true) for any
cylinders whatever. However, the theorem of ~\Sref{225} is available for the
computation of the volume of a cylinder only in case the area of the base
or of the right section can be computed. And this is possible by
\emph{elementary methods} only in case these are circles.

The lateral area of a cylinder can be computed only in case the perimeter
of a right section can be found, and this is possible by elementary
methods only in case the right section is a circle.}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 085.png---Folio 77-------

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If a prism is inscribed in a cylinder, is its lateral area greater or
less than that of the cylinder? Is its volume greater or less than that of
the cylinder?

\item[2.] If a prism of say $1000$~faces is inscribed in a cylinder, would the
theorems of ~\Sref{221} and ~\Sref{225} apply directly to this prism? Would it be
easy to detect experimentally the difference between this prism and the
cylinder?

\item[3.] How would the right section of the prism and the cylinder of example~2
be related?

\item[4.] Discuss the questions in examples 1, 2, 3 as related to prisms circumscribed
about a cylinder.

\item[5.] What is the lateral area of a cylinder whose element is $8$~inches
and the perimeter of whose right section is $24$~inches?

\item[6.] What is the volume of a cylinder whose altitude is $10$~inches and
the area of whose base is $50$~square inches?

\item[7.] What is the volume of a cylinder whose edge is $4$~inches, and the
area of whose right section is $24$~square inches?

\end{Sightwork}

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Find the volume of a right circular cylinder with radius~$5$
and altitude~$8$.

\item[2.] Find the volume of a circular cylinder if the radius of
a right section is~$6$ and the length of an element is~$10$.

\item[3.] Find the lateral area of a cylinder if the perimeter of a
right section is~$39$ and the length of an element is~$8$.

\item[4.] Find the lateral area of a right cylinder if the perimeter
of the base is~$30$ and the altitude is~$5$.

\item[5.] Find the total surface area of a right circular cylinder of
radius~$3$ and altitude~$5$.

\item[6.] Find the volume of an oblique cylinder whose base is a
circle of radius~$4$ and whose altitude is~$8$.
\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 086.png---Folio 78-------


\Section{SUMMARY OF BOOK III}
\index{Summaries@\textbf{Summaries}}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] Define prismatic surface and prismatic space.

\item[2.] Define prism, base of prism, lateral surface, right section,
right prism, parallelopiped, truncated prism.

\item[3.] Give the rule for finding the lateral area of an oblique
prism. How may this rule be modified in the case of a right
prism?

\item[4.] What can be said about opposite faces of a parallelopiped
as to shape and size?

\item[5.] Give two rules for finding the volume of an oblique
prism. See ~\Sref{193}, \Sref{198}.

\item[6.] In proving the theorem on the volume of a prism, we
considered triangular prisms, general parallelopipeds, rectangular
parallelopipeds, and general prisms. In what order were
these taken? Why?

\item[7.] Show how you would find the approximate volume of a
rectangular parallelopiped whose dimensions are $\sqrt{2}$, $\sqrt{3}$, $\sqrt{5}$.
Find this volume accurately to two places of decimals. Discuss
the problem of finding this volume correct to four places of
decimals. See exercises on page~\Pgref{63}.

\item[8.] Define closed plane curve, convex plane curve, cylindrical
surface, cylindrical space, cylinder, right cylinder, circular
cylinder, tangent plane to a cylinder.

\item[9.] Give the rule for finding the lateral surface of any
cylinder, of a right circular cylinder.

\item[10.] Give two rules for finding the volume of an oblique
cylinder. Show that these both hold true for a right circular
cylinder.

\item[11.] Describe what is meant by the statement ``the area and
the volume of a cylinder may be approximated by taking the
area and the volume of inscribed or circumscribed prisms.''
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 087.png---Folio 79-------

\Section{MISCELLANEOUS EXERCISES ON BOOK III}

\begin{Itemize}
\index{Problems@\textbf{Problems and Applications}|(}

\item[1.] If the lateral surface of a cylinder and the length of an
element are known, can the perimeter of a right section be
found? If the lateral area is~$400\pi$, and an element~$15$, find
the perimeter of a right section.

\item[2.] The diameter of a right circular cylinder is~$8$, and the
diagonal of the largest rectangle made by a vertical section is~$16$.
Find its altitude.

\item[3.] The volume of a right circular cylinder is $128\pi$~cubic
inches and its altitude is equal to its diameter. Find the
altitude and the diameter.

\item[4.] A rectangle whose sides are $a$ and $b$ is turned about the
side~$a$ as an axis and then about the side~$b$. Find the ratio of
the volumes of the two cylinders thus developed. Also find
the ratio of the total surfaces of these cylinders.

\item[5.] Find the diameter of a right circular cylinder if its
lateral area is equal numerically to its volume. Does the
result depend upon the altitude of the cylinder?

\item[6.] If the altitude of a right circular cylinder is equal to its
diameter, find the ratio of the numerical values of its total area
and its volume. Does this depend on the radius?

\item[7.] A regular hexagonal prism is inscribed in a right circular
cylinder whose altitude is equal to the diameter. Find the
difference between the volumes of the cylinder and the prism,
if a side of the hexagon is $4$~inches.

\item[8.] A cylindrical tank $8$~feet in diameter, partly filled with
water, is lying on its side. If the greatest depth of the water
is $6$~feet, what fraction of the volume
of the tank is filled with water?

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\item[9.] In the preceding problem find
the fraction of the volume occupied by
water if the width of the top of the
water along a right cross-section of the tank is $4$~feet.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{087a}{1.75in}
\index{Problems@\textbf{Problems and Applications}|)}
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 088.png---Folio 80-------

\Chapter{BOOK IV}{Pyramids and Cones}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\Paragraph{229.}{Pyramidal Surface.} Given a convex
\index{Generator@\textbf{Generator}!of a pyramidal surface}%{\Pnum{229}}%
\index{Pyramidal@\textbf{Pyramidal surface}}%{\Pnum{229}}%
\index{Pyramidal@\textbf{Pyramidal surface}!element of}%{\Pnum{229}}%
\index{Pyramidal@\textbf{Pyramidal surface}!vertex of}%{\Pnum{229}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!pyramidal}%{\Pnum{229}}%
\index{Vertex@\textbf{Vertex}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{229}}%
polygon and a fixed point~$P$ not in
its plane. If a line through the fixed
point moves so as to touch the boundary
of the polygon and is made to traverse
it completely, the line is said to generate
a convex \emph{pyramidal surface}.

\Indent The moving line is the \emph{generator} of the
surface, and in any of its positions it is
an \emph{element} of the surface. The fixed
point is the \emph{vertex} of the pyramidal
surface.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{088a}{1.75in}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{230.}{Nappes.} A pyramidal surface has
two parts, one on each side of the vertex,
which are called \emph{nappes}.
\index{Pyramidal@\textbf{Pyramidal surface}!nappes of}%{\Pnum{230}}%
\index{Nappes@\textbf{Nappes}!of a pyramidal surface}%{\Pnum{230}}%

\Indent\Paragraph{231.}{Pyramid.} The solid bounded by a
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}}%{\Pnum{231}}%
\index{Section@\textbf{Section}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{253}}%
pyramidal surface and a plane section cutting
all the elements, and not passing
through the vertex, is called a \emph{pyramid}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{088b}{1.25in}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[] If a plane cuts both nappes of a pyramidal surface, is it possible that it
should cut every element of the surface?

\begin{Suggestion}
\Page{79}%
If a plane~$M$ cuts both nappes, then a plane through the
vertex $\parallel M$ meets the surface in two elements which are $\parallel M$ and hence
are not cut by~$M$.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 089.png---Folio 81-------

\Section{CLASSIFICATION OF PYRAMIDS}
\SecToc{Properties of Pyramids}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{232.}{Faces. Edges.} The lateral surface of a pyramid
\index{Base@\textbf{Base}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Faces@\textbf{Faces}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!base of}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!lateral faces of}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!triangular}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Tetrahedron@\textbf{Tetrahedron}}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Lateral@\textbf{Lateral}!edges}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Lateral@\textbf{Lateral}!faces}%{\Pnum{232}}%
\index{Lateral@\textbf{Lateral}!surfaces}%{\Pnum{232}}%
is composed of \emph{triangular faces} having a
common vertex at the vertex of the pyramid, and
having as bases the sides of the polygon forming
the base. The sides common to two such triangles
are the \emph{edges} of the pyramid.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{089a}{1in}}

\textbf{Pyramids are classified}, according to the shape of the base, as
\emph{triangular}, \emph{quadrangular}, \emph{pentagonal}, etc.

\Paragraph{233.}{Tetrahedron.} A pyramid having a triangular base has
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!altitude of}%{\Pnum{233}}%
in all four faces, and is called a \emph{tetrahedron}. In this case
every face is a triangle, and any one of them may be taken as
the base.

% [** PP: Changed bold to emph]
The \emph{altitude of a pyramid} is the perpendicular distance from
\index{Altitude@\textbf{Altitude}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{233}}%
the vertex to the plane of the base.

\Paragraph{234.}{Regular Pyramid.} A \emph{regular pyramid} is one whose base
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!regular}%{\Pnum{234}}%
\index{Regular@\textbf{Regular}!pyramids}%{\Pnum{234}}%
\index{Right@\textbf{Right}!pyramid}%{\Pnum{234}}%
is a regular polygon such that the perpendicular from the vertex
upon it meets it at the center.

\Paragraph{235.}{Properties of a Regular Pyramid.}

(1)~\textit{The edges are equal to each other}.

\begin{Remark}
For they cut off equal distances from the foot of the perpendicular.
\end{Remark}

(2)~\textit{The faces are equal isosceles triangles}.

(3)~\textit{The altitudes of the triangular faces are equal to each other}.

\Paragraph{236.}{Slant Height.} The altitude of any one of the triangular
faces of a regular pyramid is called its \emph{slant height}.
\index{Slant@\textbf{Slant height}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{236}}%

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If the slant height of a regular pyramid and the apothem of its base
are given, how may its altitude be computed? If the altitude and
apothem are given, how may the slant height be found?

\item[2.] Can the expression ``slant height'' be applied to a pyramid if the
altitudes of its faces are not all equal? Discuss fully.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 090.png---Folio 82-------

\Section{LATERAL AREA OF A REGULAR PYRAMID}

\begin{Theorem}{237.}{I.}
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{237}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{237}}%
The lateral area of a regular
pyramid is equal to one half the product of its slant
height and the perimeter of the base.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{090ab}{}
\end{center}

\textbf{Suggestion for Proof}: Let $l = PK$ represent the slant height, $S$~the
lateral area, and $p$ the perimeter of the base. We are to show that
\[
S = \tfrac{1}{2} l\cdot p.
\]

Give all the steps.

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{238.}{Pyramids Cut by a Plane.} The part
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!frustum of}%{\Pnum{238}}%
\index{Pyramid@\textbf{Pyramid}!truncated}%{\Pnum{238}}%
\index{Truncated@\textbf{Truncated}!pyramid}%{\Pnum{238}}%
of a pyramid between its base and a plane
cutting all its edges is called a \emph{frustum} if
the cutting plane is parallel to the base,
and a \emph{truncated pyramid} if the cutting
plane is not parallel to the base.
%[** Illustration]

\Paragraph{239.}{The Parts of a Frustum.} The two
\index{Altitude@\textbf{Altitude}!of a frustum}%{\Pnum{239}}%
\index{Frustum@\textbf{Frustum}!of a pyramid}%{\Pnum{239}}%
\index{Slant@\textbf{Slant height}!of a frustum}%{\Pnum{239}}%
parallel faces of a frustum are its \emph{upper
and lower bases}, the perpendicular distance
between the bases is its \emph{altitude}, the trapezoidal
faces are its \emph{lateral surface}, and the
common altitude of these faces is the \emph{slant
height} of the frustum.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{090cd}{1.25in}

\begin{Corollary}{240.}{}
The lateral area of the frustum of a regular
pyramid is equal to half the sum of the perimeters of the bases
multiplied by the slant height.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Suggestion}
Use ~\Sref{62} and the figure in ~\Sref{238}.
\end{Suggestion}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 091.png---Folio 83-------

\Section{SECTION PARALLEL TO THE BASE OF A PYRAMID}

\begin{Theorem}{241.}{II.}
If a pyramid is cut by a plane
parallel to the base, then

\Inum{(1)}~the edges and altitude are divided proportionally;

\Inum{(2)}~the polygonal section is similar to the base;

\Inum{(3)}~the areas of this section and of the base are in the
same ratio as the squares of their perpendicular distances
from the vertex.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{091ab}{}
\end{center}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: Let $A'B'C'D'E'$ be a section $\parallel ABCDE$.

(1)~To prove that $\dfrac{PK'}{PK} = \dfrac{PA'}{PA} = \dfrac{PB'}{PB}$, etc., pass another plane
through $P$ parallel to the base and then use ~\Sref{103}.

(2)~To prove that $ABCDE\sim A'B'C'D'E'$, we show that
$\angle A = \angle A'$, $\angle B = \angle B'$, etc., and also $\dfrac{A'B'}{AB} = \dfrac{B'C'}{BC}$, etc.

(3)~Calling the area of the section $b'$ and that of the base $b$, we
are to prove that $\dfrac{b'}{b} = \dfrac{\overline{PK'}^2}{\overline{PK}^2}$, and for this we need to show that
\[
\dfrac{A'B'}{AB} = \dfrac{PA'}{PA} = \dfrac{PK'}{PK}.
\]

Give all the steps in detail.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 092.png---Folio 84-------

\begin{Corollary}{242.}{}
If two pyramids have equal altitudes and
bases of equal areas lying in the same plane, the sections made
by a plane parallel to the plane of the bases have equal areas.
\end{Corollary}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{092ab}{}
\end{center}

\begin{Suggestion}
If $t$ and $t'$ are the areas of the sections and $b$ and $b'$
those of the bases, show that $\dfrac{t}{b} = \dfrac{\overline{QL'}^2}{\overline{QL}^2}$, $\dfrac{t'}{b'} = \dfrac{\overline{PK'}^2}{\overline{PK}^2}$, and hence $\dfrac{t}{b} = \dfrac{t'}{b'}$, from
which it follows that $t = t'$ if $b = b'$.
\end{Suggestion}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Find the lateral area of a regular pyramid if its slant height is $10$~in.\
and the perimeter of its base is $25$~in.

\item[2.] What is the perimeter of the base of a regular pyramid if its lateral
area is $72$~sq.~in.\ and its slant height is $8$~in.?

\item[3.] What is the slant height of a regular pyramid if its lateral area is
$160$~sq.~in.\ and the perimeter of its base is $20$~in.?

\item[4.] What is the perimeter of the base of a regular pyramid if its lateral
area is $250$~sq.~in.\ and its slant height is $20$~in.?

\item[5.] The area of the base of a pyramid is $36$~sq.~in.\ and its altitude is
$8$~in. What is the area of a section of this pyramid made by a plane
parallel to the base and $4$~in.\ from the vertex?

\item[6.] The area of the base of a pyramid is $64$~sq.~in.\ and its altitude is
$8$~in. Find the distance from the vertex to a plane parallel to the base if
the area of its section is $16$~sq.~in.

\item[7.] If the area of one section of a pyramid is double that of another,
both sections being parallel to the base, find the ratio of their distances
from the vertex. Find this ratio if the area of one section is three times
that of the other; also if it is four times.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 093.png---Folio 85-------

\Section{INSCRIBED AND CIRCUMSCRIBED PRISMS}

\Paragraph{243.}{Construction.} A triangular pyramid is cut by a series
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!prism}%{\Pnum{243}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!prism}%{\Pnum{243}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!circumscribed}%{\Pnum{243}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{243}}%
\begin{wrapfigure}[10]{r}{2.5in}
% [** Illustrations]
\Input{093ab}{2.5in}
\end{wrapfigure}
of planes parallel to the base, including one through the vertex
and also the one in
which the base lies.

Through the lines of
intersection of these
planes with one of the
faces, planes are constructed
parallel to the
opposite edge, thus forming
a set of prisms all
lying within the pyramid,
as $a'$, $b'$, $c'$ in pyramid
$P'$, or a set lying partly outside the pyramid, as $a$, $b$, $c$, $d$
in pyramid~$P$.

The inner prisms thus constructed are called \emph{a set of inscribed
prisms}, and the outer prisms are called \emph{a set of circumscribed
prisms}. For our purpose equidistant planes are used.

\begin{Remark}
This process may be repeated by doubling the number of planes drawn
parallel to the base and thus doubling the number of inscribed or circumscribed
prisms. By continuing in this way either set of prisms may
be made to coincide as nearly as we please with the pyramid.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{244.}{Fundamental Assumption on the Volume of a Pyramid.}
We now assume that
\index{Axioms@\textbf{Axioms}}%{\Pnum{244}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{244}}%

\textit{A pyramid has a definite volume which is less than the combined
volume of any set of circumscribed prisms and greater
than that of any set of inscribed prisms}.

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Show that in the above figure prisms $b$ and $a'$ are equal in volume
if $PM = P'M'$ and $\triangles ABC$ and $A'B'C'$ have equal areas. See ~\Sref{188},
\Sref{242}. Also prove that volume $c = {}$ volume $b'$, etc.

\item[2.] Show that the sum of the volumes of the circumscribed prisms exceeds
the sum of the volumes of the inscribed prisms by the volume of~$a$.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 094.png---Folio 86-------

\Section{PYRAMIDS HAVING EQUAL VOLUMES}

\begin{Theorem}{245.}{III.}
If two triangular pyramids
have equal altitudes and bases of equal areas, their
volumes are equal.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{094ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the pyramids $P$ and $P'$ in which altitudes $PM$ and $P'M'$
are equal, and the bases $ABC$ and $A'B'C'$ have equal areas.}
{To prove that $P$ and $P'$ have equal volumes.}

\Proof: If $P$ differs at all in volume from $P'$, let $P$ be the
greater, and let the difference be some fixed number $K$, so
that\hfill $\text{Vol.\ } P - \text{Vol.\ } P' = K$.\hfill    (1)

Construct a set of inscribed and circumscribed prisms as in
~\Sref{243}.

Then\hfill $a' = b$, $b' = c$, $c' = d$.\hfill    Why?

Denote\hfill $a + b + c + d$ by $V$ and $a' + b' + c'$ by $V'$.\hfill\null

Then\hfill $V - V' = a$.\hfill (2)

We have $\text{Vol.\ } P < V$, and $\text{Vol.\ } P' > V$.\hfill ~\Sref{244}

\null\hfill $\therefore\quad \text{Vol.\ } P - \text{Vol. \ } P' < V - V'$.\hfill Why?

Hence from (2)\qquad $\text{Vol.\ } P - \text{Vol.\ } P' < a$.

Now take the divisions on $PM$ small enough to make $a < K$. \hfill ~\Sref{243}

Hence,\hfill $\text{Vol.\ } P - \text{Vol. \ } P' < K$.\hfill (3)

Thus (3) contradicts~(1), and hence the supposition that $P$
and $P'$ differ in volume is impossible.
\QED

\NewFolio %% -----File: 095.png---Folio 87-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A TRIANGULAR PYRAMID}

\begin{Theorem}{246.}{IV.}
The volume of a triangular
pyramid is one third of the product of its base and
altitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{095ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the triangular pyramid $E-ABC$. Let $h$, $b$, and $V$ be the
numerical measures respectively of the altitude $EM$, the area of the
base $ABC$, and the volume of the pyramid.}
{To prove that\qquad $V = \tfrac{1}{3} bh$.}

\Proof: On the base $ABC$ construct a triangular prism with
altitude~$h$ and lateral edge~$EB$.

This prism may be cut into three pyramids by the plane
sections through $DEC$ and~$AEC$, as shown in the figure on
the right. % [**PP: Was ``...figure to the right'']

The pyramids $E-ABC$ and $C-DEF$ have equal volumes
since they have equal bases, $ABC$ and~$DEF$ (~\Sref{175}), and the
same altitude,~$EM$.

Likewise volume $E-ACD = {}$ volume $E-CFD$.

But $C-DEF$ and $E-CFD$ are the same pyramid.

Hence, $\text{Vol.\ } E-ABC = \text{Vol.\ } C-DEF = \text{Vol.\ } E-ACD$.

That is, $\text{Vol.\ } E-ABC$ is one third of the volume of the
prism.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{But}    &     \text{volume of prism}   &= bh. \tag*{~\Sref{198}} && \\
&\indent\text{Hence,} & V = \text{volume of pyramid} &= \tfrac{1}{3} bh. && \tag*{\QED}
\end{flalign*}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 096.png---Folio 88-------

\Section{VOLUME OF ANY PYRAMID}

\begin{Theorem}{247.}{V.}
The volume of any pyramid is
one third of the product of its base and altitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{096abc}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the pyramid $P-ABCDE$. Let $V$, $b$, and~$h$ be the numerical
measures respectively of the volume, base, and altitude.}
{To prove that\qquad $V = \tfrac{1}{3} bh$.}

\Proof: By means of diagonal planes such as $PAC$ and $PAD$,
divide the given pyramid into triangular pyramids. Call the
bases of the triangular pyramids $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, etc., and their common
altitude~$h$. Then by ~\Sref{246}, the volumes of the triangular
pyramids are $\tfrac{1}{3} b_1 h$, $\tfrac{1}{3} b_2 h$, $\tfrac{1}{3} b_3 h$.

Hence the total volume is $\tfrac{1}{3}(b_1 + b_2 + b_3)h = \tfrac{1}{3} bh$.
\QED

\begin{Corollary}{248.}{1.}
The volumes of any two pyramids having
equal altitudes are proportional to the areas of their bases.
\end{Corollary}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{096d_g}{\textwidth}
\end{center}

\begin{Corollary}{249.}{2.}
The volumes of any two pyramids having
bases of equal areas are proportional to their altitudes.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 097.png---Folio 89-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A FRUSTUM OF A PYRAMID}

\begin{Theorem}{250.}{VI.}
The volume of a frustum of a
pyramid is equal to the combined volumes of three
pyramids whose common altitude is the same as that
of the frustum, and the areas of whose bases are those
of the upper and lower bases of the frustum and the
mean proportional between these areas.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{097ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the frustum $AC'$ with area of lower base~$b$, area of upper
base~$b'$, and altitude~$h$. Then $\sqrt{bb'}$ is the mean proportional between
$b$ and $b'$.}
{To prove that the volume of $AC'$ is
\[
V = \tfrac{1}{3} hb + \tfrac{1}{3} hb' + \tfrac{1}{3} h \sqrt{bb'} = \tfrac{1}{3} h [b + b' + \sqrt{bb'}].
\]}

\Proof: Let $h'$ be the altitude $PK$ of the complete pyramid
$P-ABCDE$. Then the altitude of the pyramid $P-A'B'C'D'E'$
is $h' - h$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Hence,} & \dfrac{b}{b'} &= \dfrac{h'^2}{(h' - h)^2} && \tag*{~\Sref{241}~(3)} \\
&\text{from which} & \dfrac{\sqrt{b}}{\sqrt{b'}} &= \dfrac{h'}{h' - h}. && \\
\intertext{\indent Clearing of fractions and solving for $h'$,}
&& h' &= \dfrac{h\sqrt{b}}{\sqrt{b} - \sqrt{b'}}. && \tag{1} % [** PP: Period added]
\end{flalign*}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 098.png---Folio 90-------

Now $V$ is the difference between the pyramids whose
altitudes are $h'$ and $h' - h$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent\text{Hence,}    & V &= \tfrac{1}{3} bh' - \tfrac{1}{3} b'(h' - h), && \tag*{~\Sref{247}} \\
&\text{or, rearranging,} & V &= \tfrac{1}{3} b'h + \tfrac{1}{3} h'(b - b'). && \tag*{(2)}
\end{flalign*}

Substituting the value of $h'$ from~(1) in~(2) we have
\begin{align*}
V &= \dfrac{1}{3} b'h + \dfrac{1}{3} \dfrac{h\sqrt{b}}{\sqrt{b} - \sqrt{b'}} (b - b') \\
  &= \dfrac{1}{3} b'h + \dfrac{1}{3} \dfrac{h\sqrt{b}}{\sqrt{b} - \sqrt{b'}} (\sqrt{b} - \sqrt{b'}) (\sqrt{b} + \sqrt{b'}) \\
  &= \tfrac{1}{3} b'h + \tfrac{1}{3} hb + \tfrac{1}{3} h\sqrt{bb'} \\
  &= \tfrac{1}{3} h [b' + b + \sqrt{bb'}]. \tag*{\QED}
\end{align*}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] A flower bed is in the form of a regular right pyramid, with a
square base $5$~ft.\ on a side. The altitude is $3$~ft. Find the number of
cubic feet of soil used in its construction.

\item[2.] The altitude of a certain pyramid is $15$~in.\ and its volume is $380$~cu.~in.
Find the area of its base.

\item[3.] The area of the base of a pyramid is $48$~sq.~ft.\ and its volume
$160$~cu.~ft. Find its altitude.

\item[4.] Find the locus of the vertices of pyramids having the same base
and equal volumes.
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}}%

\item[5.] Two monuments having bases of equal areas are pyramidal in shape,
one being $15$~ft.\ high and the other $18$~ft. Find the ratio of their volumes.

\item[6.] Two pyramids with equal altitudes have bases whose areas are
$7$~sq.~ft.\ and $13$~sq.~ft. Find the ratio of the volumes of the pyramids.

\item[7.] Find the volume of a frustum of a pyramid the areas of whose bases
are $36$~sq.~in.\ and $144$~sq.~in., and whose altitude is $10$~in.

\item[8.] The volume of a frustum of a pyramid is $332$~cu.~in.\ and the areas
of its bases are $9$~sq.~in.\ and $36$~sq.~in. Find its altitude.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 099.png---Folio 91-------

\Section{EXERCISES INVOLVING NUMERICAL COMPUTATION}

\begin{Itemize}
\index{Problems@\textbf{Problems and Applications}|(}

\item[1.] The base of a regular pyramid is a square whose sides
are $16$~ft. Find the slant height of the pyramid if its altitude
is $6$~ft. Also find the lateral area.

\item[2.] The lateral area of a regular hexagonal pyramid is
$72$~sq.~ft.\ and the slant height is $12$~ft. Find the perimeter
of the base, the apothem of the base, and the altitude of the
pyramid.

\item[3.] Find the lateral area of the frustum of a pyramid if the
perimeters of its bases are $27$ and $54$~in.\ respectively and its
slant height is $12$~in. Does the result depend upon the number
of sides of the frustum?

\item[4.] The bases of a frustum of a pyramid are squares whose
sides are $8$~in.\ and $2$~in.\ respectively. Find the volume of the
frustum if its altitude is $6$~in.

\item[5.] Find the volume of a regular triangular pyramid the
sides of whose base are $5$~in.\ and whose altitude is $6$~in.

\begin{Suggestion}
The area of a regular triangle with side~$a$ is $\dfrac{a^2}{4}\sqrt{3}$.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[6.] Find the volume of a regular hexagonal pyramid the
sides of whose base are $8$~in.\ and whose altitude is $10$~in.

\begin{Suggestion}
The area of a regular hexagon with side~$a$ is $\dfrac{3 a^2}{2}\sqrt{3}$.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[7.] Two marble ornaments of equal altitudes are pyramidal
in form. One has a square base $2$~in.\ on a side and the other
a regular hexagonal base $1$~in.\ on a side. Find the ratio of
their volumes.

\item[8.] A pyramid has for its base a right triangle with hypotenuse
$10$ and shortest side~$6$. Find the volume of the pyramid
if its altitude is~$9$.

\item[9.] The slant height of a frustum of a regular pyramid is
$10$~in.\ and the apothems of its bases are $8$~in.\ and $6$~in.\ respectively.
Find its altitude.
\index{Problems@\textbf{Problems and Applications}|)}
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 100.png---Folio 92-------

\Section{EXERCISES INVOLVING ALGEBRAIC COMPUTATION}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] A tent is to be made in the form of a right pyramid
with a regular hexagonal base. If the altitude is fixed at
$12$~ft., what must be the side of the base in order that the
tent may inclose $400$~cu.~ft.\ of space?

\item[2.] A pyramid with altitude $8$~in.\ and a base whose area is
$36$~sq.~in.\ is cut by a plane parallel to the base so that the
area of the section is $18$~sq.~in. Find the distance from the
base to the cutting plane.

\item[3.] A frustum of a pyramid is cut from a pyramid the
perimeter of whose base is $60$~in.\ and whose altitude is $15$~in.
What is the altitude of the frustum if the perimeter of its
upper base is $20$~in.? Does the result depend upon the number
of sides of the pyramid?

\item[4.] Solve the preceding problem if the perimeter of the
upper base of the frustum is one~$n$th that of the lower base.

\item[5.] The area of the base of a pyramid is $180$~sq.~in.\ and its
altitude is $20$~in. Cut from it a frustum the area of whose
upper base is $45$~sq.~in.; also one the area of whose upper base
is one~$n$th of $180$~sq.~in. Do these results depend upon the
number of sides of the pyramid?

\item[6.] If the altitude of a pyramid is $h$, how far from the base
must a plane parallel to it be drawn so that the area of its
cross-section shall be half that of the base of the pyramid?

\item[7.] In a regular right pyramid a plane parallel to the base
cuts it so as to make a section whose area is one half that of
the base. Find the ratio between the lateral area of the pyramid
and that of the small pyramid cut off by the plane.

\item[8.] Find the volume and the total surface of a regular tetrahedron
whose edges are $9$~in.

\item[9.] Find the total surface and the volume of a regular hexagonal
pyramid the sides of whose base are each~$a$ and whose
altitude is~$a$.
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 101.png---Folio 93-------

\Section{CONES}
\SecToc{Properties of Cones}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{251.}{Conical Surface.} Given a closed convex
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!element of}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!vertex of}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Conical@\textbf{Conical surface}!element of}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Conical@\textbf{Conical surface}!generator of}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Conical@\textbf{Conical surface}!vertex of}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Element@\textbf{Element}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Generator@\textbf{Generator}!of a conical surface}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!conical}%{\Pnum{251}}%
\index{Vertex@\textbf{Vertex}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{251}}%
plane curve and a fixed point $P$ not in its
plane. If a line through $P$ moves so as
always to touch the curve and is made to
traverse it completely, it is said to generate
a \emph{convex conical surface}.

\Indent The moving line is the \emph{generator} of the surface,
and in any of its positions it is an element
of the surface. The fixed point is the \emph{vertex}.

\Indent\Paragraph{252.}{Nappes.} A conical surface has two
\index{Conical@\textbf{Conical surface}!nappes of}%{\Pnum{252}}%
\index{Nappes@\textbf{Nappes}!of a conical surface}%{\Pnum{252}}%
parts, one on each side of the vertex, which are called \emph{nappes}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{101a}{1.25in}}

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{253.}{Cone.} The solid bounded by a conical surface and a
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!altitude of}%{\Pnum{253}}%
\index{Section@\textbf{Section}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{253}}%
plane section cutting all its elements, and
not passing through the vertex, is called a
\emph{cone}.

\Indent\Paragraph{254.}{Base. Lateral Surface. Altitude.} The
plane part of the surface of a cone is its
\emph{base} and the curved part is the \emph{lateral surface}.
The \emph{altitude} of a cone
\index{Altitude@\textbf{Altitude}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{254}}%
\index{Base@\textbf{Base}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{254}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!altitude of}%{\Pnum{254}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!base of}%{\Pnum{254}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!lateral surface of}%{\Pnum{254}}%
is the perpendicular
distance from the vertex to the plane
of the base.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\Input{101b}{1.25in}

\Paragraph{255.}{Circular Cone.} A cone which has a circular cross-section
such that the perpendicular upon it from the vertex meets it
at the center is called a \emph{circular cone}. If the base is such a
circle, the cone is then a \emph{right circular cone}. Otherwise, it is
an \emph{oblique circular cone}.

\index{Axis@\textbf{Axis}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{255}}%
\index{Circular@\textbf{Circular}!cone}%{\Pnum{255}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!circular}%{\Pnum{255}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!oblique}%{\Pnum{255}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!right circular}%{\Pnum{255}}%
\index{Right@\textbf{Right}!cone}%{\Pnum{255}}%
The \emph{axis} of a circular cone is the line from the vertex
through the center of a circular section.

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[] If a plane cuts both nappes of a conical surface, show that it cannot
cut all the elements of the surface. See \textit{suggestion} on page~\Pgref{79}.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 102.png---Folio 94-------

\Paragraph{256.}{Generating a Right Circular Cone.} A right circular cone
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!right circular}%{\Pnum{256}}%
may be \emph{generated} by rotating a right triangle $PMB$ about one
of its legs, $PM$, as an axis. The hypotenuse $PB$ generates the
lateral surface, and the other leg, $MB$,
generates the base.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\Paragraph{257.}{Slant Height.} The generator
of the convex surface of a right circular
cone in any of its positions is
called the \emph{slant height} of the cone.
\index{Slant@\textbf{Slant height}!of a right cone}%{\Pnum{257}}%

\Indent\Paragraph{258.}{Frustum of a Cone.} The part of a cone included between
\index{Frustum@\textbf{Frustum}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{258}}%
the base and a plane
section parallel to the base is
called a \emph{frustum of a cone}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustrations]
\hfill\Input{102ab}{2in}

The base of the cone and the
parallel section are the \emph{bases of
the frustum}.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.25in}
\Indent\Paragraph{259.}{Slant Height of a Frustum.} A frustum cut from a right
\index{Slant@\textbf{Slant height}!of a frustum}%{\Pnum{259}}%
circular cone has two circular bases. The segments of all the
elements of the cone intercepted between these bases are equal
and their common length is the \emph{slant height} of the frustum.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustrations]
\hfill\Input{102cd}{2.25in}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Compare the definitions of pyramidal and conical surfaces.

\item[2.] Compare the definitions of a pyramid and a cone, and of a frustum
of each.

\item[3.] What kind of cone corresponds to a regular pyramid?

\item[4.] Can the expression ``slant height'' be applied to any other cone
than a right circular cone? Discuss this question for the frustum of a
cone.

\item[5.] In a right circular cone, if any two of the three quantities, altitude,
slant height, radius of the base, are given, show that the third may be
found.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 103.png---Folio 95-------

\Section{SECTION OF A CONE THROUGH AN ELEMENT}

\begin{Theorem}{260.}{VII.}
If a plane contains an element
of a cone and meets its surface in any other point, then
it contains another element also, and the section is a
triangle.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{103ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Objective{Let a plane contain the element~$PB$ of the cone $P-ABC$, and also
one other point $K$ or~$K'$.}
{To prove that this plane contains another element~$PD$, and that
the section is a triangle~$PBD$.}

\textbf{Outline of Proof}: \Case{(1)}{When the point~$K$ is in the lateral surface.}

Draw the element $PD$ through $K$, and let the plane determined
by $PB$ and~$PD$ meet the base in~$BD$.

Then $\triangle PBD$ is the section made by the plane containing $PB$
and the point~$K$.

\Case{(2)}{When the point $K'$ is in the base.}

Draw $BD$ through $K'$ and also the element $PD$.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent Then the plane determined by $BD$
and $PD$ contains $PB$ and $K'$ and cuts the
cone in the $\triangle PBD$.

\Indent\Paragraph{261.}{Plane Tangent to a Cone.} If a
\index{Tangent@\textbf{Tangent}!to a cone}%{\Pnum{261}}%
plane contains an element of a cone and no
other point of the cone, the plane is \emph{tangent}
to the cone, and the element is called
the \emph{element of contact}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{103c}{1.25in}}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 104.png---Folio 96-------

\Section{SECTION OF A CONE PARALLEL TO THE BASE}

\begin{Theorem}{262.}{VIII.}
If the base of a cone is circular,
every plane section parallel to the base is also circular.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{104ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Objective{Given a cone with a circular base~$AD$.}
{To prove that the $\parallel$ section $EH$ is also circular.}

\Proof: Draw the straight line from $P$ to the center $M$ of the
base, and let it meet the section $EH$ in the point~$O$. Let $F$
and~$G$ be any two points on the perimeter of the section $EH$.

Pass planes containing $PM$ through the points $F$ and~$G$, and
let them cut the base in $MB$ and~$MC$ respectively.

Now in $\triangles PMB$ and $PMC$, $OF\parallel MB$ and $OG\parallel MC$.\hfill ~\Sref{92}
\[
\therefore\quad \dfrac{OF}{MB} = \dfrac{OP}{MP} = \dfrac{OG}{MC}. % [** PP: Added period]
\]

But\hfill $MB = MC$.\quad $\therefore\ OF = OG$.\hfill Why?

Hence, since $F$ and $G$, \emph{any} two points on the perimeter of
this section, are equally distant from~$O$, this shows that $EH$
is a circle whose center is~$O$.
\QED

\begin{Corollary}{263.}{}
If a cone has a circular base, the areas of
two cross-sections parallel to it are in the same ratio as the squares
of their perpendicular distances from the vertex and also as the
squares of the distances of their centers from the vertex.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 105.png---Folio 97-------

\Section{INSCRIBED AND CIRCUMSCRIBED PYRAMIDS}

\Paragraph{264.}{} The lateral surface of a regular pyramid inscribed in a
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!cone}%{\Pnum{264}}%
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!pyramid}%{\Pnum{264}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!cone}%{\Pnum{264}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!pyramid}%{\Pnum{264}}%
right circular cone may be computed and is equal to one half
the product of the slant height and the perimeter
of the base (~\Sref{237}).
\Page{96}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1in}
\Indent If the number of faces of the inscribed pyramid
is doubled, the lateral surface of the resulting
pyramid may again be computed in terms of its
slant height and the perimeter of its base.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{-0.125in}{\smash{\Input{105a}{1in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent In a similar manner, the lateral surface of a
regular pyramid circumscribed about a right
circular cone may be computed in terms of the
slant height of the cone and the perimeter of
the polygon circumscribed about the base.
The number of faces may be doubled and the
lateral surface again computed, and so on.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{105b}{1.25in}

Evidently either of these processes may be repeated indefinitely
and the surfaces of the inscribed or circumscribed
pyramids may be made to lie as close to the surface of the cone
as we please.

\begin{Remark}
The circumscribed pyramids all have the same slant height as that of
the cone, and in case of the inscribed pyramids, the slant height may be
made to differ by as little as we please from that of the cone by making
the number of faces great enough.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{265.}{Fundamental Assumption on the Lateral Area of a Right
Circular Cone.} We now assume that
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{265}}%
\index{Axioms@\textbf{Axioms}}%{\Pnum{265}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{265}}%

\textit{A right circular cone has a definite lateral area which can be
approximated as nearly as we please by taking the lateral area of
the successive inscribed or circumscribed pyramids}.

\begin{Remark}
Since, by the theorem of ~\Sref{237}, the lateral area of any regular pyramid
is half the product of the perimeter of its base and its slant height, it
follows that this theorem holds for all pyramids used in approximating
the lateral area of a right circular cone and that all practical measurements
of such lateral areas are based on this theorem.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 106.png---Folio 98-------

\Section{LATERAL AREA OF A RIGHT CIRCULAR CONE}

The foregoing considerations constitute an informal proof
of the following theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{266.}{IX.}
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!lateral surface of}%{\Pnum{266}}%
The area of the lateral surface
of a right circular cone is equal to one half the product
of its slant height and the circumference of its base.
\end{Theorem}

The argument used on page~\Pgref{96} shows that a theorem of this
kind holds for every inscribed or circumscribed pyramid used in
the approximation process, and hence this theorem for the cone
is established for all purposes of practical measurement.

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{267.}{}
If $l$ is the slant height
of a right circular cone and $r$ is the radius of
its base, the area of the lateral surface is
\[
S = \tfrac{1}{2}\cdot 2 \pi r\cdot l = \pi r l.
\]
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.25in}{\smash{\Input{106a}{2in}}}

\textit{Note}. In the case of a cone which is not a right circular cone the
slant height varies from point to point and the process of computation of
~\Sref{266} fails. Finding the lateral surface of such a cone depends on
methods first introduced in the calculus and is a much more difficult
problem than those solved in elementary plane and solid geometry.

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If a pyramid is inscribed in a cone, is its lateral area greater or less
than that of the cone?

\item[2.] Does the theorem of ~\Sref{237} apply to an irregular pyramid or to an
oblique pyramid? Discuss fully.

\item[3.] If a regular pyramid of $1000$~faces is inscribed in a right circular
cone, would the theorem of ~\Sref{237} apply to this pyramid? Would it be
easy to detect experimentally the difference between this pyramid and the
cone? Would the size of the cone affect the answer to this question?
Discuss similar questions about a circumscribed pyramid of $1000$~faces.

\item[4.] Find the lateral surface of a right circular cone whose altitude is
$7$~in.\ and the radius of whose base is $2$~in.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 107.png---Folio 99-------

\Section{LATERAL AREA OF A FRUSTUM}

\begin{Theorem}{268.}{X.}
The lateral area of a frustum
of a right circular cone is equal to one half the sum
of the circumferences of the bases multiplied by the
slant height.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{107ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the frustum $ABCD$, with slant height $l$ and radii of bases
$r$ and $r'$. \\
\indent Let $S$ represent its lateral area.}
{To prove that $S = \tfrac{1}{2} (2\pi r + 2\pi r')l = \pi l(r + r')$.}

\Proof: Complete the cone, and let $PC = l'$.
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{Then} &   S &= \tfrac{1}{2} \bigl[2\pi r(l + l') - 2\pi r'l'\bigr] && \tag*{~\Sref{267}} \\
&&                 &= \pi rl + \pi l'(r - r'). && \tag*{(1)} \\
&\text{But} & \dfrac{r}{r'} &= \dfrac{l + l'}{l'}, \text{ from which } l' = \dfrac{r'l}{r - r'}. && \tag*{(2)} \\
\intertext{Substituting $l'$ from (2) in (1),}
&&               S &= \pi rl + \pi r'l = \pi l(r + r'). && \tag*{\QED}
\end{flalign*}

\begin{Corollary}{269.}{}
The lateral area of a frustum of a right
circular cone is equal to the circumference of a section midway
between the bases multiplied by the slant height.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Suggestion}
In the formula of the theorem we have
\[
S = \pi l(r + r') = 2\pi \dfrac{(r + r')}{2} l.
\]

Now show that $\dfrac{r + r'}{2}$ is the radius of the section midway between the
two bases.\hfill ~\Sref{46}
\end{Suggestion}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 108.png---Folio 100-------

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If a triangle which is not a right triangle revolves about one of its
sides, does it generate a cone?

Show that the figure generated by revolving any triangle about its
longest side may be divided into two cones.

\item[2.] Find the lateral area of a right circular cone with radius of base $6$~in.\
and slant height $10$~in. In this and the succeeding exercises, express the
results in terms of~$\pi$, e.g., $60 \pi$~sq.~in.

\item[3.] Find the lateral area of a right circular cone with radius of base $8$~in.\
and altitude $6$~in.

\item[4.] Find the lateral area of a frustum of a right circular cone, the
radii of whose bases are $8$~in.\ and $4$~in.\ and whose slant height is $6$~in.

\item[5.] A right circular cone having a base with radius $6$~ft.\ and altitude
$8$~ft.\ is cut by a plane parallel to its base and at a distance of $4$~ft.\ from
the vertex. Find the radius of this section. Also find its area.

\end{Sightwork}

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] The lateral area of the surface of a right circular cone is
$120\pi$~sq.~in., and its radius is $4$~in. Find its slant height.

\item[2.] A circular chimney $100$~ft.\ high is in the form of a
frustum of a right circular cone whose lower base is $10$~ft.\ in
diameter and upper base $8$~ft. Find the lateral surface.

\item[3.] The lateral area of a frustum of a right circular cone is
$60\pi$~sq.~in.; the radii of the two bases are $6$~in.\ and $4$~in. Find
the slant height of the frustum.

\item[4.] The lateral area of a right circular cone is $S$, and the slant
height is~$l$. Express the radius of the base and also the altitude
in terms of $S$ and~$l$.

\item[5.] If the radius of the base of a right circular cone is $r$, and
the lateral area is $S$, express the slant height in terms of $r$
and~$S$.

\item[6.] If the slant height of a right circular cone is~$l$, and the
lateral area is~$S$, express the circumference of the base in
terms of $l$ and~$S$.
\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 109.png---Folio 101-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A CONE}

\Paragraph{270.}{} Consider inscribed and circumscribed pyramids similar
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!volume of}%{\Pnum{270}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{270}}%
to those used in ~\Sref{264}, except that they need not be regular
since the cone is not now required to be a right circular cone.

By repeatedly increasing the number of faces of either the
inscribed or circumscribed pyramids, they may be made to
approach coincidence with the cone as nearly as we please.

\Paragraph{271.}{Fundamental Assumption on the Volume of a Cone.} We
\index{Axioms@\textbf{Axioms}}%{\Pnum{271}}%
\index{Cone@\textbf{Cone}!volume of}%{\Pnum{271}}%
now assume that \textit{a cone has a definite volume which can be approximated
as nearly as we please by taking the volume of successive
inscribed or circumscribed prisms}.

Recalling that the volume of a pyramid is equal to one
third of the product of its base and altitude, we see that the
above considerations constitute an informal proof of the
following theorem:

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\begin{Theorem}{272.}{XI.}
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!of a cone}%{\Pnum{272}}%
The volume of a
cone is equal to one third of the product
of its base and altitude.
\end{Theorem}

\Indent\begin{Corollary}{273.}{}
If the base of a cone is a
circle with radius~$r$ and if the altitude is~$h$, then
the volume of the cone is
\[
V = \tfrac{1}{3}\cdot \pi r^2\cdot h = \tfrac{1}{3} \pi r^2 h.
\]
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.375in}{\Input{109a}{1.25in}}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Find the volume of an oblique cone with altitude $8$~in.\ and a circular
base whose radius is $6$~in.

\item[2.] Find the volume of a right circular cone with slant height $10$~in.\
and radius of base $6$~in.

\item[3.] The area of the base of a cone is $50$~sq.~in.\ and its volume is $600$~cu.~in.
Is the altitude the same whether the cone is right or oblique?

\item[4.] Show that if two cones have bases of equal areas their volumes are
proportional to their altitudes.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 110.png---Folio 102-------

\Section{VOLUME OF A FRUSTUM OF A CONE}

\begin{Theorem}{274.}{XII.}
The volume of the frustum of
a cone is equal to the combined volumes of three cones
whose common altitude is the altitude of the frustum
and whose bases are the upper and lower bases of the
frustum and a mean proportional between these bases.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.125in}
\begin{center}
\Input{110ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

\begin{Suggestion}
The proof is exactly like that of ~\Sref{250}, making use of
~\Sref{272}, instead of ~\Sref{247}. The result in symbols is $V = \tfrac{1}{3} h(b + b' + \sqrt{bb'})$.
\end{Suggestion}

\begin{Corollary}{275.}{}
The volume of a frustum of a right circular
cone is $V = \tfrac{1}{3} \pi h(r^2 + {r'}^2 + rr')$, where $h$ is the altitude and
$r$ and~$r'$ are the radii of the bases.
\end{Corollary}
\begin{flalign*}
& \text{For } & b = \pi r^2,\ b' = \pi {r'}^2.\quad \therefore\
V &= \tfrac{1}{3} h(\pi r^2 + \pi {r'}^2 + \sqrt{\pi r^2\cdot \pi {r'}^2}) && && \\
&&  &= \tfrac{1}{3} \pi h(r^2 + {r'}^2 + rr'). && &&
\end{flalign*}

\textit{Note}. The theorem of ~\Sref{272} holds for any cone whatever, whether
right or oblique. However, when the base is not a circle we have no
means in elementary mathematics for \emph{computing} its area. Hence the
volume of such a cone cannot be found at this stage even though ~\Sref{272}
does apply.

Similarly ~\Sref{274} applies to a frustum of any cone whatever, but we are
able to \emph{compute} its volume by elementary methods only in case the bases
are circles.

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[] The radius of the base of a cone is $5$~in.\ and its altitude is $10$~in. Find
the volume of a frustum formed by a plane parallel to the base and $6$~in.\
from it. Find the total surface of this frustum in case the cone is a right
circular cone.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 111.png---Folio 103-------

\Section{SUMMARY OF BOOK IV}
\index{Summaries@\textbf{Summaries}}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] Define pyramidal surface and conical surface. In what
respects do they differ?

\item[2.] Define pyramid, cone, regular pyramid, circular cone,
and right circular cone.

\item[3.] For what kind of cones may the lateral area be found
by means given in this Book? What is the corresponding
kind of pyramid?

\item[4.] For what kind of frustum of a cone may the lateral
area be found by means given in this Book? What is the
corresponding kind of frustum of a pyramid?

\item[5.] What assumption about the volume of a pyramid is
made in Book~IV? In the proof of what theorems is this
assumption used?

\item[6.] Beginning with the theorem of ~\Sref{245} state in order the
theorems which lead to the rule for finding the volume of any
pyramid.

\item[7.] What assumption about the lateral area of a cone is
made in this Book? Compare this assumption with the one
in ~\Sref{219}.

\item[8.] What assumption is made about the volume of a cone?
Compare this with the assumption in ~\Sref{219}.

\item[9.] What theorems on cylinders have no corresponding
theorems for cones?

\item[10.] Show that a frustum of a cone becomes more and more
nearly identical with a cylinder if the vertex of the cone is
removed farther and farther from the base.

\item[11.] If a cone were regarded as a pyramid with a very large
number of very narrow lateral faces, what pages in this Book
would be superfluous?

\item[12.] Describe the difficulty in finding the lateral area of an
oblique cone or of a cone whose base is not circular. Does the
same difficulty exist in finding the volume of such a cone?
\end{Itemize}

\Section{EXERCISES ON BOOK IV}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] If several planes are tangent to the same cone, find one
point common to them all.

\item[2.] Find the locus of all lines which make a given angle with
a given line at a given point in it.
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[3.] Find the locus of all lines which make
a given angle with a given plane at a given
point.

% [** Illustration]

\Indent\item[4.] If the middle points of four edges of
a tetrahedron, no three of which meet at the
same vertex, are joined, prove that a parallelogram
is formed.

\Indent\item[5.] Show how to pass a plane through a
tetrahedron so that the section shall be a
parallelogram.

\Indent\begin{Suggestion}
Pass a plane parallel to each of two
opposite edges. See ~\Sref{133}.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{112ab}{1.25in}}


\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\item[6.] A mound of earth of the shape shown in the figure has
a rectangular base $16$~yards long and $8$~yards
wide. Its perpendicular height
is $5$~yards, and the length on top is
$8$~yards. Find the number of cubic
yards of earth in the mound.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{112c}{1.75in}

\begin{Suggestion}
If from each end a pyramid with a base $8$~yd.\ by $4$~yd.\
is removed, the remaining part is a triangular prism.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[7.] Given a figure in general shape the same as the preceding,
with a rectangular base of length $24$~ft.\ and width $6$~ft.
Find its volume and lateral area if the dihedral angles
around the base are each~$45$.

\item[8.] Find the area and volume of the figure developed by an
equilateral triangle with sides a if it is revolved about one of
its sides.

\begin{Suggestion}
The figure may be divided into two cones.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 113.png---Folio 105-------

\begin{Itemize}

\item[9.] Find the volume and area of the figure formed by revolving
an equilateral triangle with sides s about an altitude.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\item[10.] Find the area and volume of the figure developed
by revolving a square whose side is a
about one of its diagonals.

\Indent\item[11.] Through one vertex of an equilateral triangle
with sides a draw a line~$l$ perpendicular to
the altitude upon the opposite side. Find the
volume and area of the figure developed by revolving
the triangle about the line~$l$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{113a}{1in}}

\begin{Suggestion}
The volume may be obtained by subtracting the volumes
of two cones from the volume of a cylinder.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.25in}
\Indent\item[12.] Through a vertex of a square with sides~$a$
draw a line~$l$ perpendicular to the diagonal at that
point. Find the area and volume of the figure
developed by turning the square around~$l$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{113b}{1in}}

\begin{Suggestion}
Notice that two frustums and two cones are developed.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.125in}
\Indent\item[13.] In a regular hexagon with sides~$a$ draw a line~$l$
bisecting two opposite sides. Find the area and
volume of the figure developed by turning the hexagon
about $l$ as an axis.

\Indent\item[14.] One angle of a right triangle is~$30$. Find
the ratios of the surfaces and also of the volumes
of the solids developed by revolving this triangle around each
of its three sides in succession.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{113c}{1in}}

\begin{Suggestion}
The sides of the $\triangle$ are $a$, $2a$,
$a\sqrt{3}$.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.25in}
\Indent\item[15.] If through any point~$P$ in a diagonal
of a parallelopiped planes $KN$ and
$RM$ are drawn parallel to two faces,
show that the parallelopipeds $DQ$ and $LN$ thus formed have
equal volumes.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.375in}{\Input{113d}{2in}}}

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 114.png---Folio 106-------

\begin{BioSketch}{Archimedes}{Archimedes}
(287--212 B.C.) was without doubt the greatest mathematician of his
time. He is known not through any extended treatise, like that of
Euclid, but through a series of monographs on practically every
mathematical subject then known, including physics, mechanics,
astronomy, and many phases of geometry. His work on the circle, cone,
cylinder, and sphere is reflected in our treatment of surfaces and
volumes at the present day.
\end{BioSketch}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 115.png---Folio 107-------

\Chapter{BOOK V}{The Sphere}

\Paragraph{276.}{Sphere. Center.} A \emph{sphere} is a solid bounded by a surface
\index{Center@\textbf{Center}!of a sphere}%{\Pnum{276}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}}%{\Pnum{276}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!center of}%{\Pnum{276}}%
all points of which are equally distant from a point
within called the \emph{center}.

\Paragraph{277.}{Diameter. Radius.} A line-segment connecting two
points on the surface of a sphere and passing through its
center is a \emph{diameter}. A segment joining the center to a point
on the surface is a \emph{radius}.
\index{Radius@\textbf{Radius}!of a sphere}%{\Pnum{277}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!radius of}%{\Pnum{277}}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\Paragraph{278.}{Notation for a Sphere.} A sphere
may be designated by a single letter at
the center or by two letters giving a
radius.

\Indent\begin{Remark}
Thus the sphere $C$ means one whose center
is $C$, and the sphere $CA$ is one whose center
is $C$ and whose radius is $CA$.
\end{Remark}

\Indent\Paragraph{279.}{Generating a Sphere.} The surface
of a sphere may be \emph{generated} by revolving
a semicircle about its diameter as an
axis.
\index{Spherical@\textbf{Spherical}!surface}%{\Pnum{279}}%
\index{Surface@\textbf{Surface}!spherical}%{\Pnum{279}}%

% [** Illustration]

\Indent\begin{Remark}
Thus the surface of the sphere $CA$ may be
generated by revolving the semicircle $MNA$
about the diameter $MN$.
\end{Remark}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\Input{115ab}{1.5in}\hfil

\begin{Corollary}{280.}{1.}
If two spheres have equal radii they may
be made to coincide and hence are equal.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{281.}{2.}
All radii of the same sphere or of equal
spheres are equal.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{282.}{3.}
All diameters of the same sphere or of
equal spheres are equal.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 116.png---Folio 108-------

\Section{PLANE SECTION OF A SPHERE}

\begin{Theorem}{283.}{I.}
\index{Section@\textbf{Section}!of a sphere}%{\Pnum{283}}%
A section of a spherical surface
made by a plane is a circle.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{116ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given a sphere with center $C$ cut by a plane~$M$.}
{To prove that the points common to the surface of the sphere
and the plane form a circle.}

\Proof: From the center $C$ draw $CA$ perpendicular to the
plane~$M$.

Let $B$ and $D$ be any two points common to the plane and
the surface of the sphere. Complete the figure and prove
$AB = AD$.

Hence any two points common to the surface of the sphere
and the plane~$M$ are equidistant from the point~$A$. That is,
these points lie on a circle.

How must this proof be modified in case the plane~$M$
passes through the center of the sphere?

\begin{Corollary}{284.}{1.}
Through three points on a spherical surface
there is one and only one circle.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Suggestion}
How many planes pass through these points?
\end{Suggestion}

\begin{Corollary}{285.}{2.}
A radius of a sphere through the center
of a circle on its surface is perpendicular to the plane of the
circle; and conversely, a radius of a sphere perpendicular to the
plane of a circle on its surface passes through the center of
the circle.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 117.png---Folio 109-------

\Section{PROPERTIES OF CIRCLES ON A SPHERE}

\Paragraph{286.}{Figures on a Sphere.} Any figure drawn on the surface
\index{Figures@\textbf{Figures}!on a sphere}%{\Pnum{286}}%
of a sphere is said to \emph{lie on the sphere}.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\Paragraph{287.}{Axis and Poles.} The line perpendicular
\index{Axis@\textbf{Axis}!of a circle on a sphere}%{\Pnum{287}}%
\index{Circle@\textbf{Circle}!axis of}%{\Pnum{287}}%
\index{Circle@\textbf{Circle}!poles of}%{\Pnum{287}}%
\index{Great@\textbf{Great circle}!axis of}%{\Pnum{287}}%
\index{Great@\textbf{Great circle}!pole of}%{\Pnum{287}}%
\index{Poles@\textbf{Poles}!of a circle}%{\Pnum{287}}%
to the plane of a circle at its
center is called the \emph{axis} of the circle.

\Indent The points in which the axis of a
circle on a sphere meets the surface of
the sphere are called the \emph{poles of the
circle}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\Input{117a}{1.5in}\hfil

\Paragraph{288.}{Great and Small Circles on a Sphere.} If the plane of a
\index{Circle@\textbf{Circle}!great}%{\Pnum{288}}%
\index{Circle@\textbf{Circle}!small}%{\Pnum{288}}%
\index{Great@\textbf{Great circle}!on a sphere}%{\Pnum{288}}%
\index{Small@\textbf{Small circle} on a sphere}%{\Pnum{288}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!great circle of}%{\Pnum{288}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!small circle of}%{\Pnum{288}}%
circle on a sphere passes through the center of the sphere, it is
called a \emph{great circle} of the sphere, and if not, it is called a \emph{small
circle}.

\begin{Remark}
Thus, in the figure, $AB$ is a small circle, $PP'$ is its axis, and $P$ and~$P'$
are its poles.

The circle passing through $P$ and~$P'$ and containing the center~$C$ is a
great circle.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{289.}{Inside and Outside of a Sphere.} A point is inside, outside,
or on a sphere according as its distance from the center is less
than, greater than, or equal to the radius of the sphere.
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!points within, without}%{\Pnum{289}}%

\begin{Corollary}{290.}{1.}
The center of a great circle on a sphere is
the center of the sphere.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{291.}{2.}
All great circles on a sphere are equal
and any two such circles bisect each other.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{292.}{3.}
The axis of any circle on a sphere passes
through the center of the sphere.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{293.}{4.}
Through two given points on a sphere
there is one and only one great circle unless these points are at
opposite ends of a diameter.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{294.}{5.}
Circles on a sphere formed by parallel
planes have the same axis and the same poles.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 118.png---Folio 110-------

\Section{DISTANCES FROM POINTS ON A CIRCLE TO ITS POLE}

\Paragraph{295.}{Distance on a Sphere.} The \emph{spherical distance}, or simply
\index{Arc@\textbf{Arc} of a great circle}%{\Pnum{295}}%
\index{Distance@\textbf{Distance}!between two points on a sphere}%{\Pnum{295}}%
the \emph{distance} between two points on a sphere is the distance
measured between these points along the minor arc of the great
circle through them.

\begin{Theorem}{296.}{II.}
All points of a circle on a sphere
are equidistant from either pole of the circle.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{118ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given $P$ a pole of the circle whose center is $A$, with $B$, $C$, and $D$
any points on this circle.}
{To prove that the great circle arcs $PB$, $PC$, and $PD$ are equal.}

\begin{Suggestion}
Prove chord $PB = {}$chord $PC = {}$chord $PD$, and hence
arc $PB = {}$arc $PC = {}$arc $PD$.\hfill ~\Sref{48}
\end{Suggestion}

\Paragraph{297.}{Polar Distance of a Circle.} The spherical distance from
\index{Distance@\textbf{Distance}!polar}%{\Pnum{297}}%
\index{Polar@\textbf{Polar}!distance}%{\Pnum{297}}%
the points of a circle on a sphere to its nearest pole is called
the \emph{polar distance} of the circle.

\Paragraph{298.}{A Quadrant.} One fourth of a great circle is a \emph{quadrant}.
\index{Quadrant@\textbf{Quadrant}}%{\Pnum{298}}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{299.}{1.}
The polar distance
of a great circle is a quadrant.
\end{Corollary}

\Indent\begin{Corollary}{300.}{2.}
If a point on a
sphere is at a quadrant's distance from
each of two points not at the extremities of
the same diameter, it is the pole of the
great circle through these points.
\end{Corollary}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{118c}{1.5in}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 119.png---Folio 111-------

\Section{USE OF A SPHERICAL BLACKBOARD}

\begin{Corollary}{301.}{3.}
Polar distances of equal circles on a
sphere are equal.
\end{Corollary}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\begin{Corollary}{302.}{4.}
\index{Spherical@\textbf{Spherical}!blackboard}%{\Pnum{302}}%
Straight line-segments joining points of a
circle on a sphere, to one of its poles are
equal.
\end{Corollary}

\Indent\begin{Remark}
It follows from the preceding theorem and
corollaries that, if a \emph{spherical} blackboard is
at hand, circles may be constructed on it by
means of crayon and string the same as on
a \emph{plane} blackboard. Likewise, curve-legged
compasses may be used.
\end{Remark}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\Input{119a}{1.75in}\hfil

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If two points are at the extremities of the same diameter of a
sphere, how many great circles can be passed through these points?

\item[2.] What great circles on the earth's surface pass through both poles?
Where are the poles of these circles located?

\item[3.] If $A$ and $B$ are at opposite ends of a diameter, can a small circle be
passed through them?

\item[4.] If two circles on a sphere have the same poles, prove that their
planes are parallel. See ~\Sref{294}.

\item[5.] What is the locus of all points on a sphere at a quadrant's distance
from a given point?
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|(}%

\item[6.] What is the locus of all points on a sphere at any fixed distance
from a given point on the sphere? What is the greatest such distance
possible? Discuss fully.

\item[7.] If two planes cutting a sphere are parallel, what can be said of the
centers of the circles thus formed? What can be said of the poles of these
circles? Are these statements true of three or more such circles?

\item[8.] Find the locus of the centers of a set of circles on a sphere formed
by a set of parallel planes cutting it.

\item[9.] $AB$ is a fixed diameter of a sphere. A plane containing $AB$ is
made to revolve about it as an axis. Find the locus of the poles of the
great circles on the sphere made by this revolving plane. How are the
points $A$ and $B$ related to this locus?
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|)}%
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 120.png---Folio 112-------

\Section{CIRCLES EQUIDISTANT FROM THE CENTER}

\begin{Theorem}{303.}{III.}
If the planes of two circles on
a sphere are equidistant from the center, the circles are
equal; and conversely, if two circles on a sphere are
equal, their planes are equidistant from the center.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations] FIG. 1   FIG. 2
\begin{center}
\Input{120ab}{}
\end{center}

\begin{Suggestion}
In the first figure show that (1)~if $CA = CA'$, then
$AB = A'B'$, and (2)~if $AB = A'B'$, then $CA = CA'$.
\end{Suggestion}

\Section{CIRCLES UNEQUALLY DISTANT FROM THE CENTER}

\begin{Theorem}{304.}{IV.}
If the planes of two circles on a
sphere are unequally distant from the center, the circles
are unequal, the one nearer the center being the greater;
and conversely, if two circles on a sphere are unequal,
the plane of the greater circle is nearer the center.
\end{Theorem}

\begin{Suggestion}
In \textsc{Fig}.~2 above, show (1)~that if $CA < CA'$ then
$AB > A'B'$; and (2)~if $AB > A'B'$ then $CA < CA'$.
\end{Suggestion}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\Paragraph{305.}{Plane Tangent to a Sphere.} A
plane which meets a sphere in only
one point is \emph{tangent to the sphere}.
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!tangent to}%{\Pnum{305}}%
\index{Tangent@\textbf{Tangent}!to a sphere}%{\Pnum{305}}%

\Indent Two spheres are \emph{tangent to each other}
if they are both tangent to the same
plane at the same point.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.25in}{\smash{\Input{120c}{1.5in}}}

A \emph{line is tangent to a sphere} if it contains one and only one
point of the sphere.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 121.png---Folio 113-------

\Section{PLANE TANGENT TO A SPHERE}

\begin{Theorem}{306.}{V.}
A plane tangent to a sphere is
perpendicular to the radius from the point of tangency;
and conversely, a plane perpendicular to a radius at its
extremity is tangent to the sphere.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{121ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given a sphere $C$ with plane $M$ tangent to it at $A$.}
{To prove that the radius $CA$ is perpendicular to the plane~$M$.}

\Proof: (1)~It is necessary to prove that $CA$ is perpendicular
to every line in $M$ through~$A$. (Why?)

Draw any such line as~$AB$.

The plane $BAC$ cuts the sphere in a circle. Then $AB$ is
tangent to this circle, and hence perpendicular to $AC$.

Since $CA$ is $\perp$ to every line in~$M$ through~$A$, it is $\perp$ to~$M$.

(2)~To prove the converse, note that $CA$ is the shortest
distance from $C$ to the plane~$M$.\hfill ~\Sref{82}

Hence, every point of $M$ except $A$ is exterior to the sphere.

That is, $M$ is a tangent plane. (~\Sref{305}.)
\QED

\Paragraph{307.}{Sphere Inscribed in a Polyhedron.} A sphere is said to
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!polyhedron}%{\Pnum{307}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!sphere}%{\Pnum{307}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!circumscribed}%{\Pnum{307}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{307}}%
be \emph{inscribed in a polyhedron} if every face of the polyhedron is
tangent to the sphere. The polyhedron is then said to be \emph{circumscribed
about the sphere}.

\Paragraph{308.}{Polyhedron Inscribed in a Sphere.} A polyhedron is \emph{inscribed
in a sphere} if all its vertices lie in the surface of the
sphere. The sphere is then \emph{circumscribed about the polyhedron}.
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!polyhedron}%{\Pnum{217}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{308}}%

\NewFolio %% -----File: 122.png---Folio 114-------

\Section{SPHERE INSCRIBED IN A TETRAHEDRON}

\begin{Problem}{309.}
\index{Tetrahedron@\textbf{Tetrahedron}!circumscribed}%{\Pnum{309}}%
To inscribe a sphere in a given tetrahedron.
\end{Problem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{122ab}{\textwidth}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\Objective{Given a tetrahedron $D-ABC$.}
{To construct a sphere tangent to each of its four faces.}

\begin{Construction}
Construct planes bisecting the dihedral angles
whose edges are $AB$, $BC$, and $CA$. These three planes meet in
a point~$P$, which is equally distant from the four faces of the
tetrahedron. With the point~$P$ as a center and a radius~$PE$
equal to the distance from $P$ to one of the faces construct a
sphere. This sphere is inscribed in the tetrahedron.
\end{Construction}

\Proof: Every point in the plane $PAB$ is equidistant from the
faces $ABD$ and $ABC$.\hfill ~\Sref{123}

Similarly every point in the plane $PBC$ is equidistant from
the planes $ABC$ and $DBC$, and every point in the plane $PAC$
is equidistant from the planes $ABC$ and $DAC$.

Hence, the point $P$, common to all three planes, is equidistant
from each of the four faces of the tetrahedron.

$\therefore$ each plane is tangent to the sphere, and the sphere is
inscribed in the tetrahedron. (~\Sref{307}.)
\QEF

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[] Discuss the problem of finding a point equally distant from three planes
two of which are parallel. How many such points are there?
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 123.png---Folio 115-------

\Section{SPHERE CIRCUMSCRIBED ABOUT A TETRAHEDRON}

\begin{Problem}{310.}
To find a point equally distant from
the four vertices of a tetrahedron.
\end{Problem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{123ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the tetrahedron $P-ABC$.}
{To find a point $O$ equidistant from $P$, $A$, $B$, $C$.}

\begin{Construction}
At $D$, the middle point of $BC$, construct a plane
perpendicular to~$BC$.

This plane contains $E$, the center of the circle circumscribed
about $\triangle PBC$ and also~$F$, the center of the circle circumscribed
about $\triangle ABC$. Why?

In the plane $FDE$ draw $EG\perp ED$ and $FH\perp FD$.

Then $EG$ and $FH$ cannot be parallel and hence meet in some
point~$O$. Then~$O$ is the point required.
\end{Construction}

\Proof: Since $BC$ is $\perp$ to the plane $FDE$, it follows that each
of the planes $PBC$ and~$ABC$ is $\perp$ to plane $FDE$.\hfill ~\Sref{117}

Hence $OE$ is $\perp$ plane $PBC$ and $OF$ is $\perp$ plane $ABC$.\hfill ~\Sref{114}

Then $O$ is equidistant from $P$, $B$, and $C$, and also from $A$, $B$,
and~$C$. \\ % [** PP: Width-dependent line break]
\null\hfill ~\Sref{81}

Hence $OA = OB = OC = OP$.
\QEF

\begin{Corollary}{311.}{1.}
A sphere may be passed through any four
points, not all of which lie in the same plane.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Corollary}{312.}{2.}
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!sphere}%{\Pnum{312}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!circumscribed}%{\Pnum{312}}%
\index{Tetrahedron@\textbf{Tetrahedron}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{312}}%
A sphere may be circumscribed about any
tetrahedron.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 124.png---Folio 116-------

\Section{TO FIND THE DIAMETER OF A SPHERE}

\begin{Problem}{313.}
To find the diameter of a given
material sphere.
\end{Problem}

With any point $P$ of the sphere as a pole, construct a
circle, and on this circle select any three points $A$, $B$, $C$.

Using a pair of compasses, measure the straight line-segments
$AB$, $BC$, $CA$, and construct the triangle $A'B'C'$ equal
to $ABC$.

Let $B'D'$ be the radius of the circle circumscribed about
$A'B'C'$.

If $PP'$ is the axis of the circle $ABC$ on the sphere and $BD$
the radius of this circle, then $BD = B'D'$.

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{124ab}{}
\end{center}

Measure $PB$ by means of the compasses.

Then $PBP'$ is a right triangle, with $BD$ perpendicular to
its hypotenuse $PP'$.

$PB$ and $BD$ being known, we may now \emph{compute} $PD$ from
the right triangle $PBD$, and then compute $PP'$ from the similar
triangles $PBD$ and $PP'B$, for the latter using the relation
$PD : PB = PB : PP'$ or $PD  PP' = \overline{PB}^2$.

The segment $PP'$ may also be found by \emph{geometric} construction;
namely, by drawing a triangle equal to $P'BP$.

Show how to do this when $BP$ and $BD$ are known.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 125.png---Folio 117-------

\Section{INTERSECTING SPHERICAL SURFACES}

\begin{Theorem}{314.}{VI.}
The intersection of two spherical
surfaces is a circle.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{125ab}{}
\end{center}

\Proof: Two intersecting spherical surfaces may be developed
by two intersecting circles rotating about the line connecting
their centers $C$ and~$C'$. Let $A$ and $B$ be the two points common
to the two circles.

Then $BA$ is $\perp CC'$. \\
\null\hfill (\textit{$CC'$ is the $\perp$ bisector of $BA$}.)\hfill ~\Sref{28}

As the figure rotates about the line $CC'$, $AB$ remains $\perp CC'$
and therefore all its positions lie in a plane.\hfill ~\Sref{79}

Also $DB$ and $DA$ remain fixed in length.

Therefore the points $A$ and $B$ trace out a circle.
\QED

\begin{Sightwork}
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|(}%

\item[1.] Find the locus of the centers of all spheres tangent to a given plane
at a given point.

\item[2.] Find the locus of the centers of all spheres of given radius tangent
to a given line at a given point.

\item[3.] Find the locus of the centers of all spheres of given radius tangent
to a fixed plane.

\item[4.] Find the locus of the centers of all spheres of given radius tangent
to a fixed line.

\item[5.] Find the locus of the centers of all spheres tangent to two given
intersecting planes.
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|)}%

\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 126.png---Folio 118-------

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] The four lines perpendicular to the faces of a tetrahedron
at their circumcenters meet in a point.

\item[2.] The six planes perpendicular to the edges of a tetrahedron
at their middle points all meet in a point.

\item[3.] The planes bisecting the six dihedral angles of a tetrahedron
all meet in a point.
