\Section{MISCELLANEOUS REVIEW EXERCISES}

\subsubsection*{A.~LOCUS PROBLEMS}

\begin{Itemize}
\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|(}%

\item[1.] Find the locus of all points in space equally distant from
two parallel lines and also from two parallel planes. Discuss.

\item[2.] Find the locus of all points in space equally distant
from each of two intersecting straight lines and also from two
intersecting planes. Discuss.

\item[3.] What is the locus of all points at a perpendicular distance
of $2$~feet from a given line and lying in a plane parallel
to the line? Discuss.

\item[4.] Find the locus of all points equidistant from two given
points $A$ and $B$, and also equidistant from two planes $M$ and
$N$. Discuss.

\item[5.] What is the locus of all points on the floor of a room
which are equally distant from two diagonally opposite corners
of the room, one in the floor and one in the ceiling?

\item[6.] Find the locus of all points on a sphere where it is met
by line-segments of equal length drawn from a fixed point~$P$
outside the sphere. Discuss.

\item[7.] Find the locus of all points which are at the same fixed
distance from each of two intersecting planes, $M$ and $N$, and
also equally distant from two planes, $P$ and $Q$.

\item[8.] Given a plane $M$ and a point $P$ not in $M$. Find the
locus of a point which divides in a given ratio each segment
connecting $P$ with a point in $M$: (a)~if the segments are
divided internally; (b)~if they are divided externally.

\item[9.] A segment $AB$ of fixed length is free to move so that
its end-points lie in two fixed parallel planes. Find the locus
of a point~$C$ on $AB$ if $AC$ is of fixed length.

\item[10.] Given two fixed points in space, through each of which
passes a system of straight lines. If each line of one system
is perpendicular to a line of the other system, find the locus
of the intersection points.

\index{Loci@\textbf{Loci problems}|)}%
\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 163.png---Folio 155-------

\subsubsection*{B.~PROBLEMS IN NUMERICAL COMPUTATION}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] A pedestal for a monument is in the shape of a frustum
of a regular hexagonal pyramid, the radius of the upper base
being $4$~ft., that of the lower base $6$~ft., and the altitude of the
frustum $8$~ft. Find its volume, slant height, and lateral surface.

\item[2.] The area of the lower base of a frustum of a pyramid is
$42$~sq.~ft., its altitude $8$~ft., and volume $200$~cu.~ft. Find the
area of the upper base.

\item[3.] The area of the base of a pyramid is $480$~sq.~ft.\ and its
altitude $30$~ft. Find the volume of the frustum remaining
after a pyramid with altitude $10$~ft.\ has been cut off by a plane
parallel to the base.

\item[4.] The area of the base of a pyramid is $250$~sq.~in. If a
plane section of the pyramid parallel to the base and at a distance
of $5$~in.\ from it has an area of $175$~sq.~in., find the altitude
of the pyramid.

\item[5.] The figure below represents a solid whose base is a rectangle
$50$~ft.\ long and $40$~ft.\ wide. Its height is $12$~ft.\ and its
top a rectangle $20$~ft.\ by $10$~ft. Find its volume.

% [** Illustration]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{163a}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\item[6.] A frustum of a right circular cone has an altitude one
half that of the cone. If its slant height is $8$~ft.\ and lateral
area $64\pi$~sq.~ft., find the diameters of its bases.

\item[7.] If the area of the base of a cone is $16\pi$~sq.~in.\ and its
altitude $6$~in., find the distance from the vertex to a plane,
parallel to the base, which cuts out a section of area $9\pi$~sq.~in.

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 164.png---Folio 156-------

\subsubsection*{C.~PROBLEMS IN ALGEBRAIC COMPUTATION}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] Find the total area and the volume of a regular tetrahedron
each of whose edges is~$e$.

\item[2.] If the numerical values of the volume and of the total
area of a regular tetrahedron are equal, what is the length of
its edge?

\item[3.] Find the length of an edge of a regular tetrahedron if
its volume is numerically equal to the square of the edge.

\item[4.] Cut a pyramid of altitude $h$ by means of a plane parallel
to the base so that the perimeter of the section shall be one
third that of the base.\footnoteTrans{This is the declarative form of
Question~5. ---\emph{Trans.}} % [** PP: Added note]

\item[5.] If the altitude of a pyramid is $h$, how far from the base
must a plane parallel to it be drawn so that the area of its
cross section shall be one third that of the base of the pyramid?

\item[6.] In a regular right pyramid a plane parallel to the base
cuts it so as to make a section whose area is one third that of
the base. Find the ratio between the lateral area of the pyramid
and that of the small pyramid cut off by the plane.

\item[7.] If the diameter of a right circular cylinder is equal to
its altitude, determine the diameter so that the total area of
the cylinder shall be equal numerically to its volume.

\item[8.] Cut a right circular cone of altitude $h$ by a plane parallel
to the base so that the area of the section shall be one third
that of the base. Find the distance from the vertex to the plane.

\item[9.] Show that the lateral area of the small cone cut off in
Example~8 is one third the lateral area of the original cone.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[10.] In a right circular cone, with altitude~$h$,
and $r$ the radius of its base, a cylinder
is inscribed as shown in the figure.
Find the radius $OF$ of the cylinder if the
area of the ring bounded by the circles $OF$
and $OA$ is equal to the lateral area of the
small cone cut off at the top.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{164a}{1.25in}}

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 165.png---Folio 157-------

\subsubsection*{D.~PROBLEMS IN CONSTRUCTION}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] Construct a plane tangent to a given sphere and parallel
to a given plane. How many such planes are there?

\begin{Suggestion}
From the center of the sphere pass a line perpendicular
to the given plane and draw tangent planes at the points where this line
meets the surface of the sphere. See ~\Sref{306}, \Sref{129}.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[2.] Construct a plane tangent to a given sphere and perpendicular
to a given line. How many such planes are there?

\item[3.] How many planes may be tangent to a sphere at a point
on the sphere? How many lines? Show how to construct
them.

\item[4.] Through a given point exterior to a sphere construct a
line tangent to the sphere.

\item[5.] How many lines tangent to a sphere can be constructed
from a point outside the sphere?

\item[6.] Through a given point exterior to a sphere construct a
plane tangent to the sphere. How many planes can be passed
through a given exterior point tangent to the sphere?

\item[7.] How many planes tangent to a sphere can be passed
through two given points $A$ and $B$ outside a sphere? Discuss
fully if the line $AB$ (1)~meets the sphere in two points;
(2)~is tangent to the sphere; (3)~does not meet the sphere.
Show how to make the constructions.

\item[8.] Is it possible to construct a spherical triangle each of
whose angles is a right angle? Show how to construct one.
In a trirectangular spherical triangle what is the length of
each side in terms of degrees?

\item[9.] Construct a spherical triangle such that its polar triangle
is identical with the given triangle.

\item[10.] Construct a spherical triangle whose sides are $70$, $80$,
and~$110$, respectively, and find the sides of each of the eight
triangles formed by its polar construction.

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 166.png---Folio 158-------

\subsubsection*{E.~THEOREMS TO BE PROVED}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] Prove that planes perpendicular to the faces of a trihedral
angle and bisecting its face angles meet in a line.

\item[2.] Prove that if in two tetrahedrons three faces of one are
equal respectively to three faces of the other and similarly
placed about a vertex, the tetrahedrons are equal.

\item[3.] Prove that two tetrahedrons are equal if two faces and
the included dihedral angle are equal and similarly placed.

\item[4.] In any regular tetrahedron, an altitude equals three times
the perpendicular from its foot to any face;  or an altitude
equals the sum of the perpendiculars to the faces from any
point within the tetrahedron.

\item[5.] In a cylinder of revolution the diameter of whose base
equals the altitude, the volume equals one third the product of
the total surface by the radius of the base.

\item[6.] If two intersecting planes are each tangent to a cylinder,
show that their line of intersection is parallel to an element
of the cylinder and also parallel to the plane containing the
two elements of contact.

\item[7.] The volume of a frustum of a right circular cone equals
the sum of a cylinder and a cone of the same altitude as the
frustum, and with radii which are respectively the half sum
and the half difference of the radii of the frustum.

\item[8.] Of circles on a sphere whose planes pass through a given
point within the sphere, the smallest is that circle whose plane
is perpendicular to the diameter through the given point.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[9.] A right triangular prism is cut by a
plane not parallel to the base, but such
that its intersection $DE$ is parallel to the
base segment~$AB$. Show that the volume
of the part thus cut off is one third the
product of the sum of the three vertical
edges and the area of the base.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{166a}{1.25in}}

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 167.png---Folio 159-------

\begin{BioSketch}{Legendre}{Adrien Marie Legendre}
(1752--1833) was a celebrated French mathematician.
He was one of three commissioners who introduced the
metric system in France, having also been a member of the commission
for determining the length of the meter.

Besides many treatises on advanced subjects, he wrote a book on
elementary geometry which was the most successful of the many
attempts to supersede Euclid as a text-book. It went through many
editions in French and was translated into almost every other civilized
language.
\end{BioSketch}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 168.png---Folio 160-------

% not \addcontentsline; no number
\addtocontents{toc}{\protect\contentsline{chapter}%
  {APPENDIX TO SOLID GEOMETRY}{}{}}

\Appendix{I}{SIMILAR SOLIDS}
\SecToc{\texorpdfstring{\protect\makebox[1em][r]{I.} Similar Solids}{Similar Solids}}

\index{Similarity@\textbf{Similarity}|(}

\Paragraph{378.}{Similar Cylinders.} Two right circular cylinders are
\index{Similar@\textbf{Similar}!cylinders of revolution}%{\Pnum{378}}%
\emph{similar} if they are generated by similar rectangles revolving
about corresponding sides.

\begin{Theorem}{379.}{I.}
If in two similar cylinders $s$ and
$s'$ are the lateral areas, $S$ and $S'$ the total areas, $V$ and
$V'$ the volumes, $h$ and $h'$ the altitudes, and $r$ and $r'$ the
radii, then
\[
\dfrac{s}{s'} = \dfrac{S}{S'} = \dfrac{r^2}{r'^2} = \dfrac{h^2}{h'^2} \text{ and } \dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{r^3}{r'^3} = \dfrac{h^3}{h'^3}.
\]
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\vspace*{-0.375in}
\begin{center}
\Input{168a}{0.5\textwidth}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.25in}

\textbf{Suggestions for Proof}: To find the ratios $\dfrac{s}{s'}$ and $\dfrac{S}{S'}$, make
use of the following, giving reasons for each in detail.
\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
(1)\quad $s = 2\pi rh$, & (2)\quad $S = 2\pi r(r + h)$, \\
(3)\quad $\dfrac{r}{r'} = \dfrac{h}{h'}$, \text{ and } & (4)\quad $\dfrac{r + h}{r' + h'} = \dfrac{r}{r'} = \dfrac{h}{h'}$.
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

To find the ratio $\dfrac{V}{V'}$ make use of $V = \pi r^2h$, and $V = \pi {r'}^2h'$,
and $\dfrac{r}{r'} = \dfrac{h}{h'}$.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 169.png---Folio 161-------

\Section{RATIOS RELATING TO SIMILAR CONES}

\Paragraph{380.}{Similar Cones.} Two right circular cones are \emph{similar} if
\index{Similar@\textbf{Similar}!cones of revolution}%{\Pnum{380}}%
they are generated by two similar right triangles revolving
about corresponding sides.

\begin{Theorem}{381.}{II.}
If in two similar cones $s$ and $s'$
are the lateral areas, $S$ and $S'$ the total areas, $V$ and
$V'$ the volumes, $h$ and $h'$ the altitudes, and $r$ and $r'$
the radii, then
\[
\dfrac{s}{s'} = \dfrac{S}{S'} = \dfrac{r^2}{r'^2} = \dfrac{h^2}{h'^2} \text{ and } \dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{r^3}{r'^3} = \dfrac{h^3}{h'^3}.
\]
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.5in}
\begin{center}
\Input{169a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

\textbf{Suggestions for Proof}: See suggestions under ~\Sref{379}.

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] The lateral area of a cone is $36$~square inches. What is
the lateral area of a similar cone whose altitude is $\tfrac{3}{4}$ that of
the given cone?

\item[2.] The total area of one of two similar cones is three times
that of the other. Compare their altitudes and also their
radii.

\item[3.] The sum of the total areas of two similar cones is $144$
square inches. Find the area of each cone if one is $1\tfrac{3}{4}$ times
as high as the other.

\item[4.] The volume of one of two similar cones is $5$~times that
of the other. Compare their altitudes.

\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 170.png---Folio 162-------

\Section{CONDITIONS FOR SIMILARITY OF TETRAHEDRONS}

\Paragraph{382.}{Similar Polyhedrons.} Two polyhedrons are \emph{similar} if
\index{Corresponding@\textbf{Corresponding}!parts of similar polyhedrons}%{\Pnum{382}}%
\index{Polyhedrons@\textbf{Polyhedrons}!similar}%{\Pnum{382}}%
\index{Similar@\textbf{Similar}!polyhedrons}%{\Pnum{382}}%
they have the same number of faces similar each to each and
similarly placed, and have their corresponding polyhedral angles
equal.

Any two parts which are similarly placed are called \emph{corresponding
parts}, as corresponding faces, edges, vertices.

\begin{Theorem}{383.}{III.}
Two tetrahedrons are similar
if three faces of one are similar respectively to three
faces of the other, and are similarly placed.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{170ab}{0.6\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the tetrahedrons $P-ABC$ and $P'-A'B'C'$ having
$\triangle APB \sim \triangle A'P'B'$, $\triangle APC \sim \triangle A'P'C'$, and $\triangle BPC \sim \triangle B'P'C'$.}
{To prove $P-ABC \sim P'-A'B'C'$.}

\Proof: (1)~Show that $\triangle ABC \sim \triangle A'B'C'$.

(2)~Show that trihedral $\Angles P$ and $P'$ are equal.

Likewise $\angle A = \angle A'$, $\angle B = \angle B'$, $\angle C = \angle C'$.

Hence, by definition, the
polyhedrons are similar.


\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.5in}
\Indent\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] If the two prisms in the figure
are similar, name the pairs of corresponding
parts. Likewise for two
similar pyramids.

\end{Sightwork}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustrations]
\hfill\raisebox{-0.125in}{\smash{\Input{170cd}{2.25in}}}


\NewFolio %% -----File: 171.png---Folio 163-------

\Section{TETRAHEDRONS HAVING TWO EQUAL TRIHEDRAL ANGLES}

\begin{Theorem}{384.}{IV.}
The volumes of two tetrahedrons,
having a trihedral angle of the one equal to a
trihedral angle of the other, are proportional to the
products of the edges which meet in the vertices of
these angles.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.125in}
\begin{center}
\Input{171ab}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

\Objective{Given the tetrahedrons $P-ABC$ and $P'-A'B'C'$ whose volumes
are $V$ and $V'$ and in which Tri.\ $\angle P = {}$ Tri.\ $\angle P'$.}
{To prove that $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{PA \cdot PB \cdot PC}{P'A' \cdot P'B' \cdot P'C'}$.}

\Proof: Place $P'-A'B'C'$ so that Tri.\ $\angle P'$ coincides with
Tri.\ $\angle P$.

Let $CM$ and $C'M'$ be the altitudes of $P-ABC$ and $P-A'B'C'$
from the vertices $C$ and $C'$ upon the plane $PAB$.

Let $AN$ and $A'N'$ be the altitudes of the $\triangles PAB$ and $PA'B'$.

Then $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{\tfrac{1}{3} CM   \cdot \text{area } PAB}
                            {\tfrac{1}{3} C'M' \cdot \text{area } PA'B'}
  = \dfrac{CM \cdot PB \cdot AN}{C'M' \cdot PB' \cdot A'N'}$.

Now prove $\dfrac{CM}{C'M'} = \dfrac{PC}{PC'}$ and $\dfrac{AN}{A'N'} = \dfrac{PA}{PA'}$.

Hence, we have $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{PC \cdot PB \cdot PA}{P'C' \cdot P'B' \cdot P'A'}$.

Give all the steps and reasons in detail.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 172.png---Folio 164-------

\Section{VOLUMES OF SIMILAR TETRAHEDRONS}

\begin{Theorem}{385.}{V.}
The volumes of two similar
tetrahedrons are in the same ratio as the cubes of their
corresponding edges.
\end{Theorem}

\Objective{Given $P-ABC \sim P'-A'B'C'$, with volumes $V$ and $V'$.}
{To prove that $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{\overline{PA}^3}{\overline{P'A'}^3}$.}

\Proof: We have $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{PA \cdot PB \cdot PC}{P'A' \cdot P'B' \cdot P'C'}$. \hfill ~\Sref{384}

Now use the properties of similar triangles to complete the
proof. Use the figure of ~\Sref{384}.

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Show that two tetrahedrons are similar if they have a
dihedral angle of one equal to a dihedral angle of the other
and the including faces similar each to each  and similarly
placed.

\item[2.] Show that the total areas of two similar tetrahedrons are
in the same ratio as the squares of any two corresponding
edges.

\item[3.] Show that if each of two polyhedrons is similar to a
third they are similar to each other.

\item[4.] Two similar tetrahedral  mounds have a pair of corresponding
dimensions $3$~ft.\ and $4$~ft. If one mound contains
$40$~cu.~ft.\ of earth, how much does the other contain?

\item[5.] The edges of a tetrahedron are $3$, $4$, $5$, $6$, $7$, and~$8$. Find
the edges of a similar tetrahedron containing $64$ times the
volume.

\item[6.] Find what fraction of the altitude of a tetrahedron must
be cut off by a plane parallel to the base, measuring from the
vertex, in order that the new pyramid thus detached may have
one fifth of the original volume.

\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 173.png---Folio 165-------

\Section{FIGURES HAVING A CENTER OF SIMILITUDE}

\Paragraph{386.}{Center of Similitude.} Two figures are said to have a
\index{Center@\textbf{Center}!of similitude}%{\Pnum{386}}%
\index{Similitude@\textbf{Similitude}!center of}%{\Pnum{386}}%
\index{Symmetry@\textbf{Symmetry}!with respect to a point}%{\Pnum{386}}%
\emph{center of similitude}~$O$, if for any two points, $A$ and $B$, of the
one figure the lines $AO$ and $BO$ meet the other in two points,
$A'$ and $B'$, called \emph{corresponding points}, such that
\[
OA : OA' = OB : OB'.
\]

See figures under ~\Sref{387}--\Sref{392}.

\begin{Theorem}{387.}{VI.}
\index{Corresponding@\textbf{Corresponding}!linear dimensions}%{\Pnum{387}}%
Any two figures which have a
center of similitude are similar.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: \Case{(1)}{Two triangles.}

Given\hfil $\dfrac{OA}{OA'} = \dfrac{OB}{OB'} = \dfrac{OC}{OC'}$.\hfil

% [** Illustration]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{173a}{}
\end{center}

Let the student prove that $\triangle ABC \sim \triangle A'B'C'$.

In case the triangles do not lie in the same plane, use ~\Sref{101}
to show that the corresponding $\Angles$ are equal.

\Case{(2)}{Two polygons.}

Given \hfil $\dfrac{OA}{OA'} = \dfrac{OB}{OB'} = \dfrac{OC}{OC'}$, etc.\hfil

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{173b}{}
\end{center}

Give the proof both for polygons in the same plane and
not in the same plane.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 174.png---Folio 166-------

\Case{(3)}{Two tetrahedrons.}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{174a}{}
\end{center}

With the same hypothesis as before, we must prove
% [** PP: Missing triangle symbol added in first forumla]
$\triangle PAB \sim \triangle P'A'B'$,
$\triangle PBC \sim \triangle P'B'C'$,
$\triangle PCA \sim \triangle P'C'A'$, and
then use ~\Sref{383}.

\Case{(4)}{Any two polyhedrons.}

(a)~Prove corresponding polygonal faces similar.

(b)~Prove corresponding polyhedral angles equal.

The last step requires not only equal \emph{face angles} about the
vertex, as in the case of the tetrahedron, but also equal \emph{dihedral}
angles. Note that two dihedral angles are equal if their faces
are parallel right face to right face and left face to left face.
(Why?)

(5)\quad Consider \emph{any two figures whatsoever} having a center of
similitude.

(a)~Take any three points $A$, $B$, $C$, in one figure and the
three corresponding points, $A'$, $B'$, $C'$, in the other.

Then $AB$ and $A'B'$, $AC$ and $A'C'$, etc., are called \emph{corresponding
linear dimensions}, and the triangles $ABC$ and $A'B'C'$
are \emph{corresponding triangles}.

(b)~It is clear that any two corresponding linear dimensions
have the same ratio as any other two, and that any two corresponding
triangles are similar.

In this sense any two figures having a center of similitude
are thus proved to be \emph{similar}.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 175.png---Folio 167-------

\Section{SIMILAR TRIANGLES PLACED IN SIMILITUDE}

\Paragraph{388.}{Ratio of Similitude.} The \emph{ratio of similitude} of two
\index{Ratio@\textbf{Ratio}!of similitude}%{\Pnum{388}}%
\index{Similitude@\textbf{Similitude}!ratio of}%{\Pnum{388}}%
similar figures is the common ratio of their corresponding
linear dimensions. This ratio is the same as the \emph{distance ratio}
of corresponding points from the center of similitude.

\begin{Theorem}{389.}{VII.}
Two similar triangles may be
so placed as to have a center of similitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{175a}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the similar triangles $T$ and $T'$, in which
$\dfrac{A'B'}{AB} = \dfrac{A'C'}{AC} = \dfrac{B'C'}{BC}$.}
{To prove that they may be placed with a center of similitude.}

\Proof: From any point $O$ draw $OA$, $OB$, $OC$.

On these rays take $A_1$, $B_1$, $C_1$ so that
\[
\dfrac{OA_1}{OA} = \dfrac{OB_1}{OB} = \dfrac{OC_1}{OC} = \dfrac{A'B'}{AB}.
\]

Now show the following:

(1)\qquad\qquad $\triangle T_1 \sim \triangle T$, and hence $\triangle T_1 \sim \triangle T'$.

(2)\qquad\qquad $\triangle T_1 = \triangle T'$.

For this show that $A_1B_1 = A'B'$ by means of the equations
\[
\dfrac{A_1B_1}{AB} = \dfrac{OA_1}{OA} \text{ and } \dfrac{A'B'}{AB} = \dfrac{OA_1}{OA}.
\]

\NewFolio %% -----File: 176.png---Folio 168-------

Likewise $A_1C_1 = A'C'$ and $B_1C_1 = B'C'$.

(3)~Finally, $\dfrac{OM_1}{OM} = \dfrac{OA_1}{OA}$,
where $M$ and $M_1$ are any two corresponding points.

Hence $O$ is the required center of similitude.

\Section{SIMILAR TETRAHEDRONS PLACED IN SIMILITUDE}

\begin{Theorem}{390.}{VIII.}
Two similar tetrahedrons may
be so placed as to have a center of similitude.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{176ab}{}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given the similar tetrahedrons $T$ and $T'$.}
{To prove that they can be placed so as to have a center of
similitude.}

\Proof: With $O$ as a center of similitude, construct $T_1$,
making
\[
\dfrac{OA}{OA_1} = \dfrac{OB}{OB_1} = \text{etc.} = \dfrac{AB}{A'B'}.
\]

Now show as in ~\Sref{389} that $T_1 = T'$, and hence that $T'$ can
be placed in the position $T_1$ so as to have with $T$ the center
of similitude~$O$.

Give all the steps in detail.

\begin{Corollary}{391.}{}
Any two similar polyhedrons may be
placed so as to have a center of similitude.
\end{Corollary}

\begin{Suggestion}
The argument is precisely similar to that in ~\Sref{390}.
\end{Suggestion}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 177.png---Folio 169-------

\Section{CENTER OF SIMILITUDE WITHIN THE FIGURE}

\Paragraph{392.}{} In the proofs for the two preceding theorems the center
of similitude was taken \emph{between} the two figures or \emph{on the same
side} of them. The center may be taken equally well \emph{within}
them, as in the following illustrations:

% [** Illustrations] Fig. 1   Fig. 2
\begin{center}
\Input{177ab}{\textwidth}
\end{center}

In the case of similar convex polyhedrons with the center
of similitude thus placed, the faces are the bases of pyramids
whose vertices are all at the center of
similitude.

If, further, the polygonal faces be
divided into triangles by drawing their
diagonals, these triangles become the
bases of tetrahedrons, all of whose vertices
are at the center of similitude.

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.25in}
\Indent Moreover, each inner tetrahedron is
similar to its corresponding outer tetrahedron.
(Why?)

The volumes of the two similar polyhedrons are thus composed
of the sums of sets of similar tetrahedrons.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration] Fig. 3
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.75in}{\Input{177c}{2in}}}
\medskip

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Give the proof of ~\Sref{389}, using \textsc{Fig}.~1 above, and extend the argument
to two similar polygons, using \textsc{Fig}.~2.

\item[2.] Draw two similar tetrahedrons with their center of similitude
within them, and give the proof of ~\Sref{390} with the figure thus made.

\item[3.] Give the proof of ~\Sref{390}, using \textsc{Fig}.~3 above.
\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 178.png---Folio 170-------

\Section{RATIO OF VOLUMES OF SIMILAR POLYHEDRONS}

\begin{Theorem}{393.}{IX.}
The volumes of any two similar
polyhedrons are proportional to the cubes of their corresponding
edges.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: Place the polyhedrons whose volumes are $V$ and $V'$
so as to have their center of similitude within them as in the
third figure of ~\Sref{392}.

Call the volumes of the similar tetrahedrons $T_1$, $T_2$, $T_3$, $\dotsc$,
and $T'_1$, $T'_2$, $T'_3$, $\dotsc$, and let $AB$ and $A'B'$ be two corresponding
edges.

Then we have
\[
\dfrac{\overline{AB}^3}{\overline{A'B'}^3} = \dfrac{T_1}{T'_1} = \dfrac{T_2}{T'_2} = \dfrac{T_3}{T'_3} = \dotsb. \tag*{(Why?)}
\]
And \qquad $\dfrac{T_1 + T_2 + T_3 + \dotsb }{T'_1 + T'_2 + T'_3 + \dotsb } = \dfrac{T_1}{T'_1} = \dfrac{\overline{AB}^3}{\overline{A'B'}^3}$.\hfill (Why?) \\[4pt]
But $T_1 + T_2 + T_3 \dotsb = V$ and $T'_1 + T'_2 + T'_3 \dotsb = V'$.

Hence,\qquad\qquad $\dfrac{V}{V'} = \dfrac{\overline{AB}^3}{\overline{A'B'}^3}$.

\begin{Corollary}{394.}{}
The volumes of any two similar solids are
proportional to the cubes of any two corresponding linear
dimensions.
\end{Corollary}

This proposition may be rendered evident by noticing that
any two similar three-dimensional figures may be built up to
any degree of approximation by means of pairs of similar
tetrahedrons similarly placed. The proposition then holds
for any two corresponding figures used in the process of
approximation.

Note that the ratio of similitude of two similar figures may
be obtained from the ratio of any pair of their corresponding
linear dimensions.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 179.png---Folio 171-------

\Section{APPLICATIONS OF SIMILARITY}

\Paragraph{395.}{The Pantograph.} The theorem that any two figures
\index{Pantograph@\textbf{Pantograph}}%{\Pnum{395}}%
which have a center of similitude are similar is the geometric
basis of many mechanical contrivances for enlarging or reducing
both plane and solid figures; that is, for constructing figures
similar to given figures
and having with them a given
ratio of similitude.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.5in}
\Indent The essential property of all
such contrivances is that one
point $O$ is kept fixed, while
two points $A$ and $B$ are allowed
to move so that $O$, $A$, and $B$
always remain in a straight
line, and so that the ratio
$OA : OB$ remains the same. See page~216 of Plane Geometry.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\smash{\Input{179a}{2in}}\hfil

In the first figure on this page $O$ is a fixed point. Segments
$OD$, $CB$, and the sides of the parallelogram $ACED$ are
of fixed length.

Prove that if $B$ is once so taken on the line $EC$ as to be
in the line $OA$, the points $O$, $A$, and $B$ will always remain
collinear, and that $OA : OB$ remains
a fixed ratio.

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.5in}
\Indent In the second figure is shown
an ordinary \emph{pantograph} used
for copying and at the same
time for reducing or enlarging
maps, designs, etc. The lengths
of the various segments are
adjustable, as shown, thus obtaining
any desired scale.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\smash{\Input{179b}{2in}}\hfil

The same contrivance may be used for copying figures in
\emph{space}, such as relief maps, and at the same time reducing or
enlarging them.
\index{Similarity@\textbf{Similarity}|)}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 180.png---Folio 172-------

\Section{RATIOS RELATING TO ANY TWO SIMILAR SOLIDS}

\Paragraph{396.}{Corresponding Cross Sections.} Now consider any two
\index{Corresponding@\textbf{Corresponding}!cross-sections}%{\Pnum{396}}%
\index{Corresponding@\textbf{Corresponding}!linear dimensions}%{\Pnum{396}}%
\index{Similar@\textbf{Similar}!figures}%{\Pnum{396}}%
similar figures whatever so placed as to have a center of similitude
$O$. We have seen that if points $A$, $B$ and $A'$, $B'$ are corresponding
points of the two figures, then the ratio of the
\emph{corresponding linear dimensions} $AB$ and $A'B'$ is equal to the
ratio of similitude $m : n$ of the two figures.

Also if $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$ and $A'$, $B'$, $C'$, $D'$ are corresponding
points, then $\triangles ABC$ and $A'B'C'$, and the tetrahedrons $ABCD$
and $A'B'C'D'$ are similar, and we have
\[
\dfrac{\text{area } ABC}{\text{area } A'B'C'} = \dfrac{m^2}{n^2} \text{ and }
\dfrac{\text{vol.\ } ABCD}{\text{vol.\ } A'B'C'D'} = \dfrac{m^3}{n^3}.
\]

The points $A$, $B$, $C$ and $A'$, $B'$, $C'$ determine two planes, each
of which intercepts a certain plane figure in the solid figure
to which the points belong. These two plane figures we call
\emph{corresponding cross sections}.

We assume without full argument:

\begin{Theorem}{397.}{X.}
\Inum{(1)}~The ratio of the areas of
any pair of corresponding cross sections or any pair
of corresponding surfaces of similar figures is equal
to the square of their ratio of similitude, {\upshape and}

\Inum{(2)}~The ratio of the volumes of any two similar figures
is equal to the cube of their ratio of similitude.
\end{Theorem}

The fact that the ratio of the areas of corresponding surfaces
of similar solids is equal to the \emph{square} of their ratio of similitude,
while the ratio of their volumes equals the \emph{cube} of this
ratio is one of the most important and far-reaching conclusions
of geometry.

Thus the ratio of the weights of two similar shells used in
gunnery, or the ratio of the weights of two men of similar
build, may be found when their ratio of similitude is known.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 181.png---Folio 173-------

\Section{PROBLEMS AND APPLICATIONS}

\Page{170}%
\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] If it is known that a steel wire of radius $r$ will carry a
certain weight~$w$, how great a weight will a wire of the same
material carry if its radius is~$2r$?

\begin{Suggestion}
The tensile strengths of wires are in the same ratio as
their cross-section areas.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[2.] Find the ratio of the diameters of two wires of the same
material if one carries twice the load of the other; three times
the load.

\item[3.] In a laboratory experiment a heavy iron ball is suspended
by a steel wire. In suspending another ball of twice
the diameter a wire of twice the radius of the first one is used.
Is this perfectly safe if it is known that the first wire will just
safely carry the ball suspended from it? Discuss fully.

\item[4.] In two schoolrooms of the same shape (similar figures)
but of different size, the same \emph{proportion} of the floor space is
occupied by desks. Which contains the larger amount of air
for each pupil?

\item[5.] If the shells used in guns are similar in shape, find the
ratio of the total surface areas of an eight-inch and a twelve-inch
shell.

\item[6.] Find the ratio of the weights of the shells in the preceding
problem, weights being in the same ratio as the volumes.

\item[7.] If a man $5$~ft.\ $9$~in.\ tall weighs $165$~lb., what should be
the weight of a man $6$~ft.\ $1$~in.\ tall, if they are similar in shape?

\item[8.] What is the diameter of a gun which fires a shell weighing
twice as much as a shell fired from an eight-inch gun, supposing
the shells to be similar bodies?

\item[9.] Supposing two trees to be similar in shape, what is the
diameter of a tree whose volume is three times that of one
whose diameter is $2$~feet? What is the diameter if the volume
is five times that of the given tree? What if it is $n$~times
that of the given tree?

\NewFolio %% -----File: 182.png---Folio 174-------

\item[10.] Assuming that the weights of schoolboys vary as the
cubes of their heights, construct a graph representing the
relation between their heights and weights, if a boy $5$~feet $9$~inches
tall weighs $130$~pounds.

\begin{Suggestion}
If $w$ represents the number of pounds in weight and $h$
the number of feet in height, $w = kh^3$. From $w = 130$, when $h = 5 \tfrac{3}{4}$, we
have $k = .684$. For the purpose of the graph, $k = .7$ is accurate enough.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[11.] From the graph constructed in the preceding example
find the weight of a boy $5$~feet tall; one $5$~feet $4$~inches; one
$5$~feet $6$~inches. Compare with the weights of boys in your
class.

\item[12.] If a man $6$~feet tall weighs $185$~pounds, construct a graph
representing the weights of men of similar build and of various
heights.

\item[13.] If steamships are of the same shape, their tonnages vary
as the cubes of their lengths. The \emph{Mauretania} is $790$~feet long,
with a net tonnage of~$32,500$. Construct a graph representing
the tonnage of steamships of the same shape, and of various
lengths.

Other ships which at one time or another have held ocean
records are: the (former) \emph{Deutschland}, length $686$~ft.\ and tonnage
$16,500$; the (former) \emph{Kaiser Wilhelm der Grosse}, length
$648$~ft.\ and tonnage $14,300$; the \emph{Lucania}, length $625$~ft.\ and
tonnage $13,000$ (nearly); and the \emph{Etruria}, length $520$~ft.\ and
tonnage $8000$. By means of this graph decide whether or not
these boats have greater or less tonnage than the \emph{Mauretania}
as compared with their lengths.

\item[14.] Raindrops as they start to fall are extremely small. In
the course of their descent a great many are united to form
larger and larger drops. If $1000$ such drops unite into one,
what is the ratio of the surface of the large drop to the sum
of the surfaces of the small drops?

\NewFolio %% -----File: 183.png---Folio 175-------

\item[15.] The strength of a muscle varies as its cross-section area,
which in turn varies as the square of the height or length of
an animal, while the weight of the animal varies as the cube of
its height or length. Use these facts to explain the greater
agility of small animals. For example, compare the rabbit
and the elephant.

\item[16.] Assuming the velocities the same, the amounts of water
flowing through pipes vary directly as their cross-section areas.
How many pipes, each $4$~in.\ in diameter, will carry as much
water as one pipe $12$~in.\ in diameter?

\item[17.] What must be the diameter of a cylindrical conduit
which will carry enough water to supply ten circular intakes
each $8$~feet in diameter?

\item[18.] A water reservoir, including its feed pipes, is replaced
by another, each of whose linear dimensions is twice the corresponding
dimensions of the first. If the velocity of the
water in the feed pipes of the new system is the same as that
in the old, will it take more or less time to fill the new reservoir
than it did the old? What is the ratio of the new time
to the old?

\item[19.] If two engine plants are exactly similar in shape, but
each linear dimension in one is three times the corresponding
dimension of the other, and if the steam in the feed pipes flows
with the same velocity in both, compare the speeds of the
engines.

\item[20.] If two men, one $5$~ft.\ $6$~in.\ and the other $6$~ft.\ $2$~in.\ in
height, are similar in structure in every respect, how much
faster must the blood flow in the larger person in order that
the body tissues of both shall be supplied equally well?

\begin{Suggestion}
Note that the amount of tissue to be supplied varies as
the cube of the height, while the cross-section area of the arteries varies
as the square of the height.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Itemize}

\Page{172}%

\NewFolio %% -----File: 184.png---Folio 176-------

\Appendix{II}{PROJECTION OF LINE-SEGMENTS}
\SecToc{\texorpdfstring{\protect\makebox[1em][r]{II.} Applications of Projection}{Applications of Projection}}

\Paragraph{398.}{Length of Projection.} The projection of a line-segment
\index{Plane@\textbf{Plane}!projections upon}%{\Pnum{398}}%
\index{Projection@\textbf{Projection}!of a line-segment}%{\Pnum{398}}%
on a plane was shown in ~\Sref{121} to be another line-segment.
The \emph{length of this projection} will now be computed in terms of
the given line-segment.

\Paragraph{399.}{Cosine of Projection Angle.} The acute angle between a
\index{Angle@\textbf{Angle}!of projection}%{\Pnum{399}}%
\index{Projection angle@\textbf{Projection angle}}%{\Pnum{399}}%
line-segment and a given line on which it is projected is called
the \emph{projection angle}.

If $l$ is the length of a line-segment and $p$ the length of its
projection, then the ratio $p:l$ is called the \emph{cosine of the projection
angle}.

\textit{E.g.}, in \textsc{Fig}.~1, $\dfrac{p}{l} = \text{cosine}\, \angle BAE$.

% [** Illustrations] Fig. 1  Fig. 2
\begin{center}
\Input{184ab}{\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Paragraph{400.}{Sine, Cosine, Tangent.} In any right triangle $ABC$
\index{Cosine@\textbf{Cosine of an angle}}%{\Pnum{400}}%
\index{Sine@\textbf{Sine of an angle}}%{\Pnum{400}}%[** PP: Embolden entry]
\index{Tangent@\textbf{Tangent}!of an acute angle}%{\Pnum{400}}%
(\textsc{Fig}.~2), either acute angle, as $\angle A$, is the projection angle
between the hypotenuse and the side adjacent to the angle.

Hence the \emph{cosine} of an acute angle of a right triangle is the
\emph{ratio of the adjacent side to the hypotenuse}.

Likewise we define the \emph{sine} of an acute angle of a right triangle
as the \emph{ratio of the opposite side to the hypotenuse}, and the
\emph{tangent} of an acute angle of a right triangle as the \emph{ratio of the
opposite side to the adjacent side}.

Using the common abbreviations, $\sin$, $\cos$, and $\tan$, we have
in \textsc{Fig}.~2,
\[
\sin A = \dfrac{a}{c},\quad \cos A = \dfrac{b}{c},\quad \tan A = \dfrac{a}{b}. % [** PP: Added period]
\]

\NewFolio %% -----File: 185.png---Folio 177-------

\Section{EXERCISES ON SINES, COSINES, TANGENTS}

The sine, cosine, and tangent are of great importance in
many computations. By careful measurement (and in other
ways) their values may be computed for any acute angle, and
a table formed, like that on page~\Pgref{175}.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\textit{E.g.}, if $\angle A = 35$ (measured with a protractor), and if we measure
$AC$, $AB$, and $BC$, and then compute the ratios
$\dfrac{a}{c}$, $\dfrac{b}{c}$, and $\dfrac{a}{b}$, we shall find the values of $\sin 35$,
$\cos 35$, $\tan 35$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{185a}{1.5in}}

With an ordinary ruler it will not usually be
possible to make these measurements with sufficient
accuracy to obtain more than one decimal
place.

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Using a protractor, construct angles of $10$, $30$, $50$, $70$,
and by measurement determine the sine, cosine, and tangent
of each.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent\item[2.] Prove that the cosine of any
given angle is the same, no matter
what point is taken in \emph{either} side
from which to let fall the perpendicular
to the other side. Prove the same for the tangent.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{185b}{1.75in}}

\item[3.] Show that if the hypotenuse be taken \emph{one decimeter} in
length, then the length of the side adjacent, measured in
decimeters, is the \emph{cosine} of the angle, and the length of the
side opposite is the \emph{sine} of the angle.

\item[4.] Show that if the side adjacent be taken one decimeter in
length, the length of the side opposite, measured in decimeters,
is the \emph{tangent} of the angle.

\item[5.] Without any direct measurement, show how to compute
the three ratios for each of the angles $30$, $45$, $60$.

\begin{Suggestion}
Make use of the fact that if one acute angle in a right
triangle is $30$, the side opposite it is one half the hypotenuse.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Exercises}

\vfil
