\NewFolio %% -----File: 187.png---Folio 179-------

\Section{LENGTH OF PROJECTION OF A LINE-SEGMENT}

\begin{Theorem}{401.}{I.}
The length of the projection of a
line-segment upon a given line is equal to the length of
the line-segment multiplied by the cosine of the projection
angle.
\end{Theorem}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.125in}
\Indent\Objective{Given the projection $p$ of the
line-segment $l$ on the line $CD$,
with the projection angle $A$.}
{To prove that $p = l \cos A$.}

\Proof: By definition we have
$\dfrac{p}{l} = \cos A$. Hence, $p = l \cos A$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\kern -0.375in \smash{\raisebox{-0.25in}{\Input{187a}{2.5in}}}


\begin{Exercises}

\Page{177a}%

\item[1.] Find the cosines of the angles $35\text{}\, 30'$, $54\text{}\, 15'$, $15\text{}\, 45'$.

\begin{Suggestion}
The cosine of $35\text{}\, 30'$ lies between $\cos 35$ and $\cos 36$.
We assume that it lies halfway between these numbers. This assumption,
while not quite correct, is very nearly so for small differences of
angles, as in this case, where the total difference is only one degree.
From the table $\cos 35\text{} = .819$, $\cos 36\text{} = .809$. The number midway
between these is~$.814$, which we take as the cosine of $35\text{}\, 30'$.

This process is called \textbf{interpolation}. A similar process is used for
sines and tangents.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[2.] Find the tangents of the angles $25\text{}\, 20'$, $47\text{}\, 45'$, $63\text{}\, 40'$.

\item[3.] Find the angle whose tangent is $1.74$.

{\small\textit{Solution}.\quad From the table we have $\tan 60\text{} = 1.73$ and $\tan 61\text{} = 1.80$.
Hence, the required angle must lie between $60$ and~$61$. Moreover, the
number $1.74$ is one seventh the way from $1.73$ to~$1.80$. Hence, we
assume the angle to lie one seventh the way from $60$ to~$61$, which gives
$60\text{} + \tfrac{1}{7} \times 1\text{} = 60\text{} + 9'$ nearly. The required angle is $60\text{}\, 9'$.}

\item[4.] Find the angles whose sines are $.276$; $.674$; $.437$.

\item[5.] Find the angles whose cosines are $.940$; $.094$; $.435$.

\item[6.] Find the angles whose tangents are $.781$; $1.41$; $3.64$.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 188.png---Folio 180-------

\Page{177b}%

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[7.] At what angle with the horizontal
must the base of a right circular cylinder
be tilted to make it just topple over if its
diameter is $6$~ft.\ and its altitude $8$~ft.?

\begin{Suggestion}
The center of gravity is at the
middle point~$C$ of the axis of the cylinder. The
base must be tilted so that the line $AC$ becomes
vertical. The required angle is $\angle ACB$.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.25in}{\Input{188a}{1.25in}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[8.] The Leaning Tower of Pisa is $179$~feet
high and $31$~feet in diameter. It now leans
so that a plumb line from the top on the
lower side reaches the ground $14$~feet from
the base. At what angle is its side now
inclined from the vertical? At what angle
would its side have to incline from the
vertical before it would topple over?
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration] [** PP: \smash last]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.25in}{\Input{188b}{1.25in}}}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent\item[9.] A four-inch hole is cut in a board, and a ball $8$~in.\ in
diameter is made to rest on it. At
what angle must the board be held
so that the ball will just roll out
of the hole?

\begin{Suggestion}
The board must be held
so that the line $OA$ becomes vertical.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{188c}{1.75in}

\BeforeSkip
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[10.] Using a ball $8$~inches in diameter, what must be the
radius of the hole in the board of the preceding problem so
that the ball shall just roll out when
the board is inclined at an angle of
$45$ to the horizontal?

\Indent\item[11.] If the figure $ABCD-H$ is a
cube, find each of the following
angles: $\angle ECA$, $\angle AEC$.

Check by using the fact that the sum
of the angles of a triangle is $180$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{188d}{1.5in}

\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 189.png---Folio 181-------

\Section{ALTITUDE OF OBLIQUE PRISM OR CYLINDER}

\begin{Theorem}{402.}{II.}
The altitude of an oblique prism
or cylinder is equal to an element multiplied by the
cosine of the angle between the plane of the base and
that of a right section.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{189a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Objective{Given an oblique prism or cylinder with base $b$ and right section
$c$, and let $BE$ be a perpendicular between the planes of the bases.}{}

Consider the plane determined by $BE$ and the element $AB$.

This plane is $\perp$ to the plane of $b$ and also to the plane of~$c$.
(Why?)

Hence, it is $\perp$ to the line of intersection of the planes of
$b$ and~$c$. (Why?)

Let this plane cut the planes of $b$ and~$c$ in $GD$ and~$FD$
respectively.

Then $\angle D$ is the measure of the dihedral angle between the
planes of $b$ and~$c$. (Why?)
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent \text{\itshape To prove that} & BE &= AB \cdot \cos D. && \\
&\indent \text{\Proof: We have}        & BE &= AB \cdot \cos \angle ABE. && \tag*{Why?} \\
&\indent \text{But}              & \angle D &= \angle ABE. &&\tag*{Why?} \\
&\indent \text{Hence,}                 & BE &= AB \cdot \cos D. &&
\end{flalign*}

\begin{Corollary}{403.}{}
The dihedral angle between the planes of
the base and a right section of an oblique cylinder or prism is
equal to the angle between an element and the altitude.
\end{Corollary}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 190.png---Folio 182-------

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Given a line-segment $10$~inches long. Find the length
of its projection on a plane if the projection angle is~$20$. If
the angle is $30$, $45$, $60$, $90$, $0$.

\item[2.] A kite string forms an angle of $40$ with the ground.
The distance from the end of the string to a point directly
beneath the kite is $200$~ft. Find the length of the string and
the perpendicular height of the kite.

\item[3.] The altitude of an oblique prism is $15$~inches. Find
the length of an element if it makes an angle of $45$ with the
perpendicular between the bases.

\item[4.] A right section of a cylinder makes an angle of $20$ with
the plane of the lower base. Find the ratio between the
altitude and an element.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2in}
\Indent\item[5.] Prove that by joining the
middle points of six edges of a
cube, as shown in the figure, a
regular hexagon is formed.

\item[6.] Prove that in the preceding
example the plane of the
regular hexagon, $KLMNOP$, is
perpendicular to the diagonal
$DF$ of the cube.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.125in}{\Input{190a}{2in}}}

\item[7.] How large a cube will be
required from which to cut a stopper for a hexagonal spout,
each of whose sides is $4$~inches?

\item[8.] In the figure find the angle $KQH$.

\begin{Suggestion}
Let $a$ be a side of the cube. Compute $KH$, $KQ$, and
$HQ$ in terms~$a$. Note that $\angle QKH = \text{rt.\ } \angle$.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[9.] Find the area of the projection of the hexagon $KLMNOP$
on the face $BCGF$. Note that this projection equals the whole
square less $\triangle NCO + \triangle KEL$. See ~\Sref{404}. Find the area of
the hexagon in terms of the side~$a$ of the cube.

\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 191.png---Folio 183-------

\Section{AREA OF THE PROJECTION OF A PLANE-SEGMENT}

\Paragraph{404.}{Projection of a Plane-Segment.} If from each point in
% Next entry gets indexed differently than in the scanned images
\index{Plane@\textbf{Plane}!-segment}%{\Pnum{404}}%
\index{Projection@\textbf{Projection}!of a plane-segment}%{\Pnum{404}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{404}}%
the boundary of a plane-segment a perpendicular is drawn to
a given plane, the locus of the feet of these perpendiculars will
bound a portion of the plane, which is called the \emph{projection of
the plane-segment} on the given plane.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent\begin{Remark}
\text{E.g.}, the plane-segment $A'B'C'$ in the plane
$N$ is the projection of the plane-segment $ABC$
from the plane $M$ upon $N$.
\end{Remark}

\Indent The \emph{angle of projection} is the angle
between the planes $M$ and $N$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\Input{191a}{1.5in}}

\begin{Theorem}{405.}{III.}
The area of the projection of a
plane-segment on a plane is equal to the area of the
plane-segment multiplied by the cosine of the projection
angle.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{191a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}

\Proof: Let the boundary of the given plane-segment $b$ be
any convex polygon or closed curve.

Using a line perpendicular to the given plane as a \emph{generator},
develop a prismatic or cylindrical surface of which $b$
is a section. The given plane will cut this surface in a
right section whose area we denote by~$c$.

Now cut the surface by a plane parallel to~$b$, forming the
upper base $b'$ of a prism or cylinder whose altitude is~$h$, edge~$e$,
and volume~$V$.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 192.png---Folio 184-------

% [** PP: Extra space on right to accommodate figure below]
Then $c$ is the projection of $b$ upon the given plane, and
$\angle 1 = \angle 2$ is the projection angle.
\begin{flalign*}
&\indent \text{We are to show that} & c &= b \cos \angle 1. &&&& \\
&\indent \text{We know that}        & V &= ce = bh. &&&& \tag*{Why?} \\
&\indent \text{But}                 & h &= e \cos \angle 2. &&&& \\
&\indent \text{Hence,}              & ce &= be \cos \angle 2. &&&& \\
&\indent \text{That is,}            & c &= b \cos \angle 2 = b \cos \angle 1. &&&&
\end{flalign*}

\begin{Remark}
\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\textit{Note}. The foregoing theorem may be proved \emph{directly} in case the
plane-segment is a rectangle with one side parallel
to the line of intersection of the two planes.
In the figure let $S$ be the given rectangle and $S'$
its projection, with $AB \parallel$ to the line of intersection
of the planes in which $S$ and~$S'$ lie, and $\angle 1$
the angle between them.
% [** Illustration]
\end{minipage}%
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.5in}{\Input{192a}{1.375in}}}

\begin{flalign*}
&\indent \text{Then}    & S  &= AB \cdot BC && \\
&        \text{and}     & S' &= A'B' \cdot B'C'. && \\
&\indent \text{But}     & AB &= A'B' && \\
&        \text{and}     & BE &= B'C'. && \tag*{Why?} \\
&\indent \text{But}     & BE &= BC \cdot \cos \angle 1 && \tag*{Why?} \\
&        \text{and}     & S' = A'B' &\cdot B'C' = AB \cdot BC \cos \angle 1. && \\
&\indent \text{That is,}& S' &= S \cos \angle 1. &&
\end{flalign*}

In the case of any plane-segment, rectangles may be inscribed in it
in this position and their number increased indefinitely, so that their sum
will approach more and more nearly to the area of the plane-segment,
and in this way it may be shown to any desired degree of approximation,
that the projection of a plane-segment equals the given plane-segment
multiplied by the cosine of the projection angle.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{406.}{Ellipse.} An important special case of the theorem of
\index{Ellipse@\textbf{Ellipse}!area of}%{\Pnum{406}}%
~\Sref{405} is the area of the figure obtained by projecting a circle
upon a plane not parallel to its own plane, nor at right angles
to it. This figure is called an \emph{ellipse}. On page~\Pgref{182} the principle
developed above is used to find the area of the ellipse.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 193.png---Folio 185-------

\Section{THE AREA OF AN ELLIPSE}

\Paragraph{407.}{Projection of a Circle.} In the figure two planes, $M$ and
\index{Projection@\textbf{Projection}!of a circle}%{\Pnum{407}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{407}}%
$M'$, meet in a line $PQ$. The circle $O$ in $M$ has a diameter
$AB \parallel PQ$ and a diameter $CD \perp PQ$.

In projecting the whole figure upon the plane $M'$ the
diameter $AB$ projects into its equal $A'B'$, while $CD$ projects
into $C'D'$ so that $C'D' = CD \cos \angle 1$.

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{193a}{}
\end{center}

\Page{182}%
By theorem ~\Sref{405} the area of the ellipse $A'C'B'D'$ equals the
area of the circle $ACBD$ multiplied by $\cos \angle 1$.

Hence, $\pi r^2 \cdot \cos \angle 1$ is the area of the ellipse.

But $r \cos \angle 1 = O'C'$ and $r = O'B'$. \hfill (~\Sref{401})

Hence, the area of the ellipse is $\pi \cdot O'C' \times O'B'$.

The segments $A'B'$ and $C'D'$ are called respectively the
major and minor axes of the ellipse, and $O'B'$ and $O'C'$ the
semimajor and the semiminor axes. These latter are usually
denoted by $a$ and~$b$.

\textbf{Hence,\qquad\qquad the area of the ellipse is $\pi ab$}.
\index{Ellipse@\textbf{Ellipse}!area of}%{\Pnum{407}}%

\begin{Remark}
Note that when $a$ and $b$ are equal, the ellipse becomes a circle, and
this formula reduces to $\pi a^2$ as it should.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 194.png---Folio 186-------

\Section{PROBLEMS AND APPLICATIONS}

\Page{183}%
\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] Given a right circular cylinder the radius of whose base
is $6$~inches. Find the area of an oblique cross section inclined
at an angle of $45$ to the plane of the base.

\item[2.] Given an oblique circular cylinder the radius of whose
right section is $10$~inches. Find the area of the base if it is
inclined at an angle of $60$ to the right section.

\item[3.] If an oblique circular cylinder has an altitude~$h$, an element
$e$, radius of right section~$r$, and $\angle A$ the inclination of
the base to the right section, express the volume in two ways
and show that these are equivalent.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.5in}
\Indent\item[4.] A six-inch stovepipe has a $45$ elbow
angle, that is, it turns at right angles. (The
angle $CAB$ is called the elbow angle.) Find
the area of the cross section of $AB$. Likewise
if it has a $60$ elbow angle.

\Indent\item[5.] At what angle must the damper in a
circular stovepipe be turned in order to obstruct just half the
right cross sectional area of the pipe?
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\raisebox{0.25in}{\Input{194a}{1.5in}}

\begin{Suggestion}
The damper must be turned so that the projection of
the damper upon a right cross section is equal to half that cross section.
\end{Suggestion}

\item[6.] The comparatively low temperature of the earth's surface
near the pole, even in summer, when the sun does not set for
months, is due partly to the \emph{obliqueness} with which the sun's
rays strike the earth. That is, a given amount of sunlight is
spread over a larger area than in lower latitudes.

\begin{Remark}
Thus, if in the figure $D'C$ is a horizontal line, and $D'D$ the direction
of the sun's rays, then a beam of light
whose right cross section is $ABCD$ is spread
% [****** PP: Width-dependent line break, set for 5in]

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-2.25in}
over the rectangle $A'BCD'$.
In other words, a patch of ground
$A'BCD'$ receives only as much sunlight
as a patch the size of $ABCD$
receives when the sun's rays strike it
vertically. $ABCD = A'BCD' \cos \angle 1$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\smash{\Input{194b}{1.75in}}\hfil
\end{Remark}

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 195.png---Folio 187-------

\begin{Remark}
Hence, each unit of area in $A'BCD'$ receives $\cos \angle 1$ times as much
light as a unit in $ABCD$.

Hence, to compare the heat-producing powers of sunlight in any latitude
with that at the place where the sun's rays fall vertically, we need
to know how the projection angle, $\angle 1$, is related to the difference in latitude
of the two places.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[7.] If $\angle 1 = 30$, compare the amount of heat received by a
unit of area in $ABCD$ and $A'BCD'$.

\item[8.] What must $\angle 1$ be in order that a unit of area in $A'BCD'$
shall receive only $\tfrac{1}{5}$ as much light as a unit in $ABCD$?

\item[9.] The figure represents a cross section of the earth with an
indication of the direction of the rays of light as they strike
it at the summer solstice when they are vertical at $A$, the
tropic of Cancer. $B$ represents the latitude of Chicago, $C$ the
polar circle, and $P$ the north pole. The angles $PDE$, $CGF$,
$BKH$ represent the projection angle, $\angle 1$, for the various
latitudes. Prove:

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-3in}
\begin{align*}
\angle PDE &= \angle POK, \\
\angle CGF &= \angle COK, \\
\angle BKH &= \angle BOK.
\end{align*}

\begin{Remark}
That is, $\angle 1$ for each place
is the latitude of that place
minus the latitude of the
place where the sun's rays
are vertical.
\end{Remark}
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\smash{\Input{195a}{2.75in}}\hfil

\item[10.] Find the relative amount of sunlight received by a unit
of area at the tropic of Cancer and at the north pole at the
time of the summer solstice.

\item[11.] Find the ratio between the amount of light received by
a unit of the earth's area at Chicago and at the tropic of
Cancer at the time of the summer solstice.

\item[12.] Find the same ratio for the equator and Chicago at the
winter solstice when the sun is vertical at latitude $23\tfrac{1}{2}$~south.
\Page{184}%

\end{Itemize}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 196.png---Folio 188-------

\Appendix{III}{VARIABLES. LIMITS}
\SecToc{\texorpdfstring{\protect\makebox[1em][r]{III.} Theory of Limits}{Theory of Limits}}
\index{Theory@\textbf{Theory of Limits}}%{\Pnum{}}%

\Paragraph{408.}{Variables and Functions.} It is often useful to think of
\index{Function@\textbf{Function}}%{\Pnum{408}}%
\index{Variables@\textbf{Variables}}%{\Pnum{408}}%
a geometric figure as \emph{varying} in size and shape.

\begin{Remark}
\textit{E.g.}, if a rectangle has a fixed base, say $10$~inches long, but an altitude
which varies from $3$~inches to $5$~inches, then the area varies from $3 \cdot 10 = 30$
to $5 \cdot 10 = 50$ square inches.

We may even think of the altitude as starting at zero inches and
increasing, in which case the area starts at zero and increases continuously.
\end{Remark}

The altitude which we think of as varying at our pleasure is
called the \emph{independent variable}, while the area, being dependent
upon the altitude, is called the \emph{dependent variable}.

The dependent variable is sometimes called a \emph{function} of the
independent variable, meaning that the two are connected by
a definite relation such that, for any definite value of the independent
variable, the dependent variable also has a definite
value.

\begin{Remark}
Thus, in the formula for the area of a rectangle, $a = bh$, if $b$ is fixed
and $h$ varies, then $a$ is a function of $h$, since for every value of $h$ there is
determined a definite value of~$a$.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{409.}{Illustrations of Limits.} If a regular polygon is inscribed
\index{Limit@\textbf{Limit}!of a variable}%{\Pnum{409}}%
in a circle of fixed radius, and if the number of sides of the
polygon be continually increased, for instance by repeatedly
doubling the number, then the apothem, perimeter, and area
are all variables depending upon the number of sides. That is,
each of these is a function of the number of sides.

Now the greater the number of sides the more nearly does
the apothem equal the radius in length. Indeed, it is evident
that the difference between the apothem and the radius will
ultimately become less than any fixed number, however small.
Hence we say that the apothem \emph{approaches the radius as a limit}
\index{Approach@\textbf{Approach}}%{\Pnum{409}}%
as the number of sides increases indefinitely.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 197.png---Folio 189-------

Similarly the perimeters of the polygons may be made as
nearly equal to the circumference as we please by making the
number of sides sufficiently great.

Hence we may define the circumference of a circle as the
\emph{limit of the perimeter} of a regular inscribed polygon as the
number of sides increases indefinitely.

The circumference of a circle may also be defined as the
limit of the perimeter of a circumscribed polygon as the number
of sides is increased indefinitely.

Likewise we may define the area of a circle as the \emph{limit of
the area} of the inscribed or the circumscribed polygon as the
number of sides is increased indefinitely.

The notion of a limit may be used to define the length of a
line-segment which is incommensurable with a given unit segment.
% [** PP: Keeping wicked which]

\begin{Remark}
Thus, the diagonal $d$ of a square whose side is unity is $d = \sqrt{2}$. Hence
$d$ may be defined as the \emph{limit of the variable line-segment} whose successive
lengths are $1$, $1.4$, $1.41$, $1.414$, $\dotsc$.\footnoteTrans%
  {See \Tref{197a}{Transcriber's Notes}.}
\Erratum{err197a} % [** PP: Bad mathematical definition...]
\end{Remark}

In like manner, the length of any line-segment, whether
commensurable or incommensurable with the unit segment,
may be defined in terms of a limit.

\begin{Remark}
Thus, if a variable segment is increased by successively adding to it
one half the length previously added, then the segment will approach a
limit. If the initial length is $1$, and if the successive additions are $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{1}{4}$, $\tfrac{1}{8}$,
$\tfrac{1}{16}$, $\tfrac{1}{32}$, etc., then the successive lengths are $1$, $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{3}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{7}{8}$, $1 \tfrac{15}{16}$, $1 \tfrac{31}{32}$, etc.
Evidently this segment approaches the limit~$2$.

Hence $2$ may be defined as the limit of the variable segment whose
successive lengths are $1$, $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{3}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{7}{8}$, etc., as the number of successive additions
is increased indefinitely.
\end{Remark}

The idea of a functional relation between variables and the
idea of a limit, as illustrated above, are two of the most important
concepts in all mathematics.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 198.png---Folio 190-------

\Section{DEFINITION OF A LIMIT}

\Paragraph{410.}{Constant.} A  quantity  which remains fixed in value
throughout a discussion is called a \emph{constant}.
\index{Constant@\textbf{Constant}}%{\Pnum{410}}%

\begin{Remark}
\textit{E.g.}, the base of the rectangle mentioned in ~\Sref{408} is a constant.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{411.}{Variable.} A quantity which continuously changes in
\index{Variables@\textbf{Variables}}%{\Pnum{411}}%
value, or which takes on a succession of different values, is
called a \emph{variable}.

\begin{Remark}
\textit{E.g.}, the altitude and the area mentioned in ~\Sref{408} are variables.
\end{Remark}

\Paragraph{412.}{Limit of a Variable.} If a variable may be made to
\index{Limit@\textbf{Limit}!of a variable}%{\Pnum{412}}%
approach a certain constant quantity in such a way that the
difference between the constant and the variable becomes and
remains less than any assignable value, however small, then
the constant is called the \emph{limit of the variable}.

\begin{Remark}
\textit{E.g.}, the fixed circumference of the circle is the limit of the variable
perimeters of the polygons mentioned in ~\Sref{409}.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Sightwork}

\item[1.] Find the limit of a variable line-segment whose initial length is $6$~inches,
and which varies by successive increments each equal to one half
the preceding, the first increment being $2$~inches.

\item[2.] Construct a right triangle whose sides are $1$ and~$2$. By approximating
a square root, find five successive lengths of a segment which approaches
the length of the hypotenuse as a limit.

\item[3.] If one tangent to a circle is fixed and another is made to move so
that their intersection point approaches the circle, what is the limiting
position of the moving tangent?    What is the limit of the measure of the
angle formed by these tangents?

\item[4.] The arc $AB$ of $74$ is the greater of the two arcs intercepted between
two secants meeting at $C$ outside the circle. The points $A$ and $B$
remain fixed while $C$ moves up to the circle. What angle is the limit of
the variable angle formed by the varying secants? What is the limit
of the measure of this angle?

\item[5.] If in the preceding the secants meet within the circle, what is the
limit of their angle and also of the measure of this angle as the intersection
point moves up to the center of the circle?

\end{Sightwork}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 199.png---Folio 191-------

\Section{THE INCOMMENSURABLE CASES}

\Paragraph{413.}{The Incommensurable Cases.} We have seen that there
\index{Incommensurables@\textbf{Incommensurables}}%{\Pnum{413}}%
are segments which are \emph{incommensurable}; that is, which have
no common unit of measure,---for instance, the side and the
diagonal of a square.

For \emph{practical purposes} the lengths of such segments are approximated
to any desired degree of accuracy, and their ratios
are understood to be the ratios of these approximate numerical
measures.

But for \emph{theoretical purposes} it is important to consider these
incommensurable cases further; just as in algebra we not only
\emph{approximate} such roots as $\sqrt{2}$, $\sqrt{3}$, $\sqrt{5}$, etc., but we also deal
with these surds as \emph{exact} numbers.

\begin{Remark}
Instances of this kind occur in such operations as
\[
(\sqrt{3} + \sqrt{2})(\sqrt{3} - \sqrt{2}) = 3 - 2 = 1.
\]
\end{Remark}

While the length of the diagonal of a unit square cannot be
expressed as an integer or as a rational fraction, that is, as the
quotient of two integers, we nevertheless think of such a segment
as having a \emph{definite length}, or what is the same thing, a
\emph{definite ratio} with the unit segment forming the side of the
square.

We now fix our attention on the \emph{incommensurable ratios
themselves}, and the method of determining them, rather than
on the \emph{process of approach} and the practical computation based
on it.

\Paragraph{414.}{Representation by Irrational Numbers.} In general, the
\index{Irrational@\textbf{Irrational}!number}%{\Pnum{414}}%
ratio between any two incommensurable geometric magnitudes
of the same kind may be represented by what is called an
\emph{irrational number}; that is, a number which is \emph{neither an integer
nor a quotient of two integers}.

Examples of irrational numbers are the surds, such as $\sqrt{2}$, % [** PP: Added comma]
$\sqrt[3]{5}$, etc., and the number~$\pi$.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 200.png---Folio 192-------

\Section{NUMBERS DEFINED BY SEQUENCES}

\Paragraph{415.}{Numbers Defined by Sequences.} The following is a
\index{Approach@\textbf{Approach}}%{\Pnum{415}}%
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!numbers defined by}%{\Pnum{415}}%
method for determining any number, whether rational or
irrational. For simplicity it is applied first to the integer~$1$.

Throughout this discussion the expressions ``point on a line''
and ``number'' will be used interchangeably.

\begin{Remark}
\begin{wrapfigure}[3]{r}{2.25in}
% [** Illustration]
\Input{200a}{2in}
\end{wrapfigure}
On a straight line mark a certain point $0$ (zero), and one unit to the
right of it mark another point~$1$. Also
lay off points such that their distances
from $0$ are % [** PP: Remove spurious comma after ``are'']
$\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{3}{4}$, $\tfrac{7}{8}$, $\tfrac{15}{16}$, $\dotsc$.

If this sequence of points is carried ever so far, it will never reach the
point~$1$. If, however, we select a point to the left of~$1$, no matter how
near to it, we may always go far enough along this sequence to reach
points between this point and~$1$. That is, $1$~is the limit of the terms of
this sequence. See ~\Sref{412}.
\end{Remark}

The point $1$ has two definite relations to this sequence:

(a)~\textit{Every point of the sequence is to the left of~$1$}.

(b)~\textit{For any fixed point to the left of $1$ there are points of the
sequence between it and~$1$}.

We see that~$1$ is the \emph{only} point on the whole line such that
both (a) and~(b) are true of it. For every point to the right
of~$1$ (a)~is true, but (b)~is not. For every point to the left of~$1$
(b)~is true, but (a)~is not.

It follows therefore that, while the points of the sequence
merely \emph{approach} the point~$1$ as a limit, the sequence, \emph{taken as
a whole, serves to distinguish that point from all other points on
the line}.

\begin{Remark}
In the above sequence, the terms continually \emph{increase}. The number~$1$
may be determined equally well by a \emph{decreasing} sequence. For instance,
the sequence $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{8}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{16}$, $\dotsc$ has the following properties which distinguish
the point~$1$ from all other points on the line.
\end{Remark}

(a)~\textit{Every point of the sequence is to the right of~$1$}.

(b)~\textit{For any fixed point to the right of~$1$ there are points of the
sequence between it and~$1$}.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 201.png---Folio 193-------

\Section{LIMIT OF AN INFINITE SEQUENCE}

\Paragraph{416.}{Infinite Sequences.} An endless sequence of either kind
just described is called an \emph{infinite sequence}.

Not every infinite sequence serves to single out a definite
point in the manner shown above. Thus the sequence $1$, $2$, $3$,
$4$, $\dotsc$ fails to do so, because its terms grow large beyond all
bound. Such sequences are said to be \emph{unbounded}, while the
sequences $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{3}{4}$, $\tfrac{7}{8}$, $\dotsc$ and $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{8}$, $\dotsc$ are \emph{bounded}.
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!bounded}%{\Pnum{416}}%
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!infinite}%{\Pnum{416}}%
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!oscillating}%{\Pnum{416}}%
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!unbounded}%{\Pnum{416}}%
\index{Limit@\textbf{Limit}!of a sequence}%{\Pnum{416}}%

Again, the sequence $1$, $2$, $1$, $2$, $1$, $2$, $\dotsc$ fails to single out a
definite point. This sequence is said to be \emph{oscillating}, since its
terms increase, then decrease, then increase, etc., while $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{3}{4}$,
$\tfrac{7}{8}$, $\dotsc$ and $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{8}$, $\dotsc$ are \emph{non-oscillating}.

\Paragraph{417.}{Limit of a Sequence.} The number $1$ is said to be the
\index{Bound@\textbf{Bound}!greatest lower}%{\Pnum{417}}%
\index{Bound@\textbf{Bound}!least upper}%{\Pnum{417}}%
\emph{least upper bound} of the sequence $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{3}{4}$, $\tfrac{7}{8}$, $\dotsc$. That is, $1$ is the
\emph{smallest number} beyond which the sequence does not go. $1$ is
also said to be the \emph{limit of the sequence}. See ~\Sref{412}.

Similarly, $1$ is the \emph{greatest lower bound} or the \emph{limit} of the
sequence $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{1}{8}$, $\dotsc$; that is, the \emph{greatest number} such that
the sequence contains no number less than it.
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!limit of}%{\Pnum{417}}%

In the manner described above any integer may be determined
by an increasing or a decreasing infinite sequence.

\begin{Remark}
\textit{E.g.}, the number $2$ is the limit of the increasing sequence $1$, $1 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $1 \tfrac{3}{4}$, $1 \tfrac{7}{8}$,
$1 \tfrac{15}{16}$, $\dotsc$, or of the decreasing sequence $2 \tfrac{1}{2}$, $2 \tfrac{1}{4}$, $2 \tfrac{1}{8}$, $2 \tfrac{1}{16}$, $\dotsc$.
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!decreasing, increasing}%{\Pnum{417}}%
\end{Remark}

Two different sequences, both increasing or both decreasing,
may also define the same number.

\begin{Remark}
\textit{E.g.}, each of the increasing sequences $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{3}{4}$, $\tfrac{7}{8}$, $\tfrac{15}{16}$, $\dotsc$ and $\tfrac{2}{3}$, $\tfrac{4}{5}$, $\tfrac{6}{7}$, $\tfrac{8}{9}$, $\dotsc$
determines the number~$1$.

We notice, however, that no matter what definite number we select in
either of these sequences, there is a number in the other greater than it;
that is, neither sequence contains a number greater than every number in
the other. See ~\Sref{420}.
\end{Remark}

It is apparent that any number may be defined as the limit
of a sequence, and we shall assume the converse, namely, that
any bounded increasing or decreasing sequence has a limit.
\Page{190}%

\NewFolio %% -----File: 202.png---Folio 194-------

\Section{FUNDAMENTAL PRINCIPLES OF LIMITS}

\begin{Axiom}{418.}{}
\index{Axioms@\textbf{Axioms}}%{\Pnum{418}}%
We now assume that: \textit{Every bounded increasing
sequence has a least upper bound, and every bounded decreasing
sequence has a greatest lower bound}.
\end{Axiom}

This axiom may also be stated:

\textit{Every bounded increasing or decreasing sequence has a limit}.

This axiom simply means that every such sequence singles
out a definite number, rational or irrational, in the manner
discussed on page~\Pgref{190}.

\begin{Remark}
Thus, if we attempt to approximate the square root of $2$, we obtain a
sequence, $1$, $1.4$, $1.41$, $1.414$, $1.4142$, $\dotsc$, having for its limit a \emph{definite
number} represented by $\sqrt{2}$, which corresponds to the length of the diagonal
of a square whose side is unity.

Again, if we attempt to approximate the value of~$\pi$, we obtain a
sequence $3$, $3.1$, $3.14$, $3.141$, $3.1415$, $\dotsc$, having for its limit the definite
number represented by~$\pi$, which is the ratio of the circumference to the
diameter of a circle.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Theorem}{419.}{I.}
Two bounded sequences have the
same limit if they are equal term by term.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: If the sequences are $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ and $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$, and
if $a_1 = b_1$, $a_2 = b_2$, $a_3 = b_3$, $\dotsc$, then they are one and the same
sequence and hence have the same limit by ~\Sref{418}.

\begin{Remark}
\textbf{Historical Note}.\quad The rigorous treatment of limits has been developed
during the last fifty years. The foundation on which it rests is the theory
of irrational numbers, which itself was placed on a firm basis in the early
seventies.

The Greeks dealt with the incommensurable cases in an interesting
manner. Thus, to prove that two incommensurable ratios are equal they
showed that neither can be less than the other. However, they failed to
make explicit definitions of incommensurables and they did not explicitly
state the fundamental axiom of ~\Sref{418} or its equivalent. Their treatment
would be complete and rigorous if supplemented by the proper definitions
and axioms.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 203.png---Folio 195-------

\Section{FUNDAMENTAL PRINCIPLES OF LIMITS}

\begin{Theorem}{420.}{II.}
Two increasing bounded sequences
have the same limit if neither sequence contains a number
greater than every number of the other.
\end{Theorem}

Let $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ and $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ denote two infinite sequences
with limits $a$ and~$b$, such that no term of the first sequence is
greater than every term of the second, and no term of the
second is greater than every term of the first.

\textit{To prove that $a = b$}.

\Proof: Suppose that $a$ is not equal to $b$ but is less than~$b$.
Then there must be numbers of the sequence $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ greater
than $a$, since by ~\Sref{415} there are numbers $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, greater than
any fixed number whatever which is less than~$b$. This contradicts
the hypothesis of the theorem, and hence $a$ cannot be
less than~$b$. In like manner we show that $b$ is not less than~$a$.
Hence $a = b$. See ~\Sref{20}.

\begin{Theorem}{421.}{III.}
Two decreasing bounded sequences
have the same limit if neither sequence contains
a number less than every number of the other.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: The proof is exactly similar to that of Theorem~II.

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Show that the sequences $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{3}{4}$, $\tfrac{7}{8}$, $\dotsc$ and $\tfrac{2}{3}$, $\tfrac{4}{5}$, $\tfrac{6}{7}$, $\dotsc$ have the
same limits.

\item[2.] Show that the sequences $\tfrac{1}{2}$, $\tfrac{1}{4}$, $\tfrac{1}{8}$, $\dotsc$ and $\tfrac{1}{3}$, $\tfrac{1}{5}$, $\tfrac{1}{7}$, $\dotsc$ have the
same limits.

\end{Exercises}

The applications of the theory of limits to geometry consist
chiefly in showing that two numbers are equal because they are
the limits of the same sequence, or of sequences having the
property stated in the theorem of ~\Sref{420} or of ~\Sref{421}.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 204.png---Folio 196-------

\Section{APPLICATION OF LIMITS TO PLANE GEOMETRY}

\begin{Problem}{422.}
\index{Commensurable@\textbf{Commensurable}!segments}%{\Pnum{422}}%
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!approximating}%{\Pnum{422}}%
On a given segment $AB$ to lay off a
sequence of points $B_1$, $B_2$, $B_3$, $\dotsc$, of which $B$ is the limit,
such that each of the segments $AB_1$, $AB_2$, $AB_3$, $\dotsc$ is
commensurable with a given segment~$CD$.
\end{Problem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.25in}
\begin{center}
\Input{204a}{}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

{\small\textit{Solution}:\quad Using $m_1$, an exact divisor of $CD$, as a unit of measure,
lay off on $AB$ a segment $AB_1$, an exact multiple of $m_1$, such that the
remainder $B_1B$ is less than $m_1$. Then $CD$ and $AB_1$ are commensurable.

Using a unit $m_2$, likewise a divisor of $CD$, and less than $B_1B$, lay off
$AB_2$ such that $B_2B$ is less than $m_2$. Then $AB_2 > AB_1$, and $CD$ and $AB_2$
are commensurable.

Continuing in this manner, using as units of measure segments $m_3$,
$m_4$, $\dotsc$ each an exact divisor of $CD$ and each less than $B_2B$, $B_3B$, $\dotsc$
respectively, we obtain a sequence of segments $AB_1$, $AB_2$, $AB_3$, $\dotsc$, each
greater than the preceding and each commensurable with~$CD$.

If the units $m_1$, $m_2$, $m_3$, $\dotsc$ are so selected that they approach zero as a
limit, it follows that $B$ is the limit point of the sequence $B_1$, $B_2$, $B_3$, $\dotsc$.

If a different sequence of divisors of $CD$, as $m_1'$, $m_2'$, $m_3'$, $\dotsc$, is used,
we obtain a sequence of points $B_1'$, $B_2'$, $B_3'$, $\dotsc$, likewise satisfying the
conditions of the problem.

Any two such sequences of points $B_1$, $B_2$, $B_3$, $\dotsc$ and $B_1'$, $B_2'$, $B_3'$, $\dotsc$
determined as above, are both \emph{increasing} and each is such that \emph{no} point
of it is to the right of \emph{every} point of the other.

Hence, by ~\Sref{420} any two such sequences have the same limit point~$B$.
}% [** PP: End of \small]

\Paragraph{423.}{Limit of a Sequence of Segments.} If $B_1$, $B_2$, $B_3$, $\dotsc$ is a
\index{Limit@\textbf{Limit}!of segments}%{\Pnum{423}}%
sequence of points on the segment $AB$ having the limit~$B$, then
the segment $AB$ is said to \emph{be the limit of the sequence of segments}
$AB_1$, $AB_2$, $AB_3$, $\dotsc$.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 205.png---Folio 197-------

\Section{INCOMMENSURABLE RATIOS}

\Paragraph{424.}{Ratio of Two Incommensurable Segments.} The ratio of
\index{Incommensurables@\textbf{Incommensurables}}%{\Pnum{424}}%
\index{Irrational@\textbf{Irrational}!ratio}%{\Pnum{424}}%
\index{Ratio@\textbf{Ratio}!incommensurable}%{\Pnum{424}}%
two incommensurable segments has not been explicitly defined,
but this may now be done in terms of the \emph{limit of a sequence}.

Consider two incommensurable segments $AB$ and $CD$. Let
$a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ be the lengths of segments each commensurable
with $CD$, forming a sequence whose limit is the segment $AB$,
and let $b$ be the length of the segment~$CD$.

Then $\dfrac{a_1}{b}$, $\dfrac{a_2}{b}$, $\dfrac{a_3}{b}$, $\dotsc$ is an increasing bounded sequence having
a limit which we call~$R$.

If $a_1'$, $a_2'$, $a_3'$, $\dotsc$ are the lengths of segments forming any
other sequence whose limit is $AB$, the sequence $\dfrac{a_1'}{b}$, $\dfrac{a_2'}{b}$, $\dfrac{a_3'}{b}$, $\dotsc$ is
another increasing bounded sequence with limit~$R'$.

By ~\Sref{420} we now know that $R = R'$.

This number $R$ is defined as \emph{the ratio of the incommensurable
segments} $AB$ and~$CD$.

\Paragraph{425.}{Ratio of Two Incommensurable Arcs.} The considerations
of ~\Sref{422}, \Sref{423}, \Sref{424} apply to two arcs of equal circles.

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.125in}
\begin{center}
\Input{205a}{0.6\textwidth}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

\begin{Remark}
Thus, in the figure, the ratio of the two incommensurable arcs $AB$
and $CD$ is the limit of the sequence $\dfrac{AB_1}{CD}$, $\dfrac{AB_2}{CD}$, $\dfrac{AB_3}{CD}$, $\dotsc$, and the ratio
of the incommensurable angles $AOB$ and $COD$ is the limit of the sequence
$\dfrac{\angle AOB_1}{\angle COD}$, $\dfrac{\angle AOB_2}{\angle COD}$, $\dfrac{\angle AOB_3}{\angle COD}$, $\dotsc$.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 206.png---Folio 198-------

\Section{APPLICATIONS OF LIMITS TO PLANE GEOMETRY}

\begin{Theorem}{426.}{IV.}
\index{Commensurable@\textbf{Commensurable}!segments}%{\Pnum{426}}%
A line parallel to the base of a
triangle, and meeting the other two sides, divides them
in the same ratio.
\end{Theorem}

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent\Objective{Given the $\triangle ABC$ with $DE \parallel BC$ and
cutting $AB$ and $AC$.}
{To prove that $\dfrac{AD}{AB} = \dfrac{AE}{AC}$.}

\Proof: Consider the case when $AD$
and $AB$ are incommensurable.

Let $D_1$, $D_2$, $D_3$, $\dotsc$ be a sequence
of points on $AB$ whose limit is~$D$.
Through these points draw parallels
to $BC$, meeting $AC$ in $E_1$, $E_2$, $E_3$, $\dotsc$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\Input{206a}{1.75in}

Then $E$ is the limit of the sequence of points $E_1$, $E_2$, $E_3$, $\dotsc$.

For suppose it is not, and that there is a point $K$ on $AE$
such that there is no point of $E_1$, $E_2$, $E_3$, $\dotsc$ between $K$ and~$E$.
Draw a line parallel to $BC$ through~$K$, meeting $AB$ in~$H$.

But there are points of the sequence $D_1$, $D_2$, $D_3$, \emph{between} $H$
and~$D$, and hence there must be points of the sequence $E_1$, $E_2$,
$E_3$, $\dotsc$ between $K$ and~$E$, which shows that $E$ is the limit of
the sequence $E_1$, $E_2$, $E_3$, $\dotsc$.

Then the sequence (1)~$\dfrac{AD_1}{AB}$, $\dfrac{AD_2}{AB}$, $\dfrac{AD_3}{AB}$, $\dotsc$ has a limit~$R$
which, by definition, is the ratio of the segments $AD$ and~$AB$.
\hfill ~\Sref{424}

Similarly, the sequence (2)~$\dfrac{AE_1}{AC}$, $\dfrac{AE_2}{AC}$, $\dfrac{AE_3}{AC}$, $\dotsc$ has a limit
$R'$ which is the ratio of $AE$ to~$AC$.

Now $\dfrac{AD_1}{AB} = \dfrac{AE_1}{AC}$, $\dfrac{AD_2}{AB} = \dfrac{AE_2}{AC}$, $\dfrac{AD_3}{AB} = \dfrac{AE_3}{AC}$, $\dotsc$ ~321, P.~G.
% [** PP: ``Plane Geometry''; period added]

Hence, the two sequences, (1) and~(2), are identical and have
the same limit (~\Sref{419}). Hence $R = R'$.
\QED

\NewFolio %% -----File: 207.png---Folio 199-------

\Section{APPLICATIONS TO PLANE GEOMETRY}

\begin{Theorem}{427.}{V.}
\index{Commensurable@\textbf{Commensurable}!angles, arcs}%{\Pnum{427}}%
In the same circle or in equal
circles the ratio of two central angles is the same as the
ratio of their intercepted arcs.
\end{Theorem}

% [** Illustrations]
\vspace*{-0.125in}
\begin{center}
\Input{207a}{0.6\textwidth}
\end{center}
\vspace*{-0.125in}

\Proof: In case the arcs are commensurable the proof is
obvious. For, in that case, the common measure of the arcs
divides each into a number of equal parts, say $m$ and~$n$ respectively,
and these arcs subtend the same number of equal angles
at the center (~\Sref{244}, Plane Geometry.) Hence the ratio of the
arcs is $m : n$ and the ratio of the angles is also $m : n$.

If the arcs $AB$ and $CD$ are not commensurable, let $AB_1$,
$AB_2$, $AB_3$, $\dotsc$ be a sequence of arcs whose limit is~$AB$, each
arc being commensurable with the arc~$CD$.

Then the sequence $\dfrac{AB_1}{CD}$, $\dfrac{AB_2}{CD}$, $\dfrac{AB_3}{CD}$, $\dotsc$ has a limit $R = \dfrac{AB}{CD}$.

Similarly the sequence $\dfrac{\angle AOB_1}{\angle COD}$, $\dfrac{\angle AOB_2}{\angle COD}$, $\dfrac{\angle AOB_3}{\angle COD}$, $\dotsc$ has
a limit $R' = \dfrac{\angle AOB}{\angle COD}$.

But $\dfrac{AB_1}{CD} = \dfrac{\angle AOB_1}{\angle COD}$, $\dfrac{AB_2}{CD} = \dfrac{\angle AOB_2}{\angle COD}$, $\dotsc$,
as shown above for the commensurable case.

Hence, these two sequences are identical and have the same
limit (~\Sref{419}). Therefore it follows that $R = R'$.
\QED

\begin{Remark}
\textit{Note}.---The student should note the successive steps in the proofs of
~\Sref{426}, \Sref{427}: (a)~Definitions of the incommensurable ratios by means
of infinite sequences; (b)~Proof that these sequences are identical;
(c)~Conclusion that the limiting ratios are equal.
\end{Remark}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 208.png---Folio 200-------

\Paragraph{428.}{Products of Irrational Numbers.} If  $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ is a
sequence with limit~$a$, then $ka_1$, $ka_2$, $ka_3$, $\dotsc$ is a sequence whose
limit is \emph{defined} as $ka$ where $k$ is any number.

If $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ and $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ are two sequences with limits
$a$ and~$b$ respectively, then $a_1b_1$, $a_2b_2$, $a_3b_3$, $\dotsc$ is a sequence whose
limit is \emph{defined} as~$ab$.

Similarly if $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$, $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ and $c_1$, $c_2$, $c_3$, $\dotsc$ are
sequences with limits $a$, $b$, $c$, then $abc$ is defined as the limit
of the sequence $a_1b_1c_1$, $a_2b_2c_2$, $a_3b_3c_3$, $\dotsc$.

For a complete treatment it would be necessary to show that
these definitions of multiplication of irrational numbers are
consistent with the rest of arithmetic and also that these new
sequences are such as to determine definite limits. This is
possible but is beyond the scope of this book.

\Paragraph{429.}{Area of a Rectangle.} If the sides of a rectangle are incommensurable
with the unit segment, we \emph{define its area} as
follows:
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{429}}%

Let $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ be a sequence of rational numbers whose
limit is the altitude~$a$; and let $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ be a sequence whose
limit is the base~$b$.

Then the area of the rectangle is the limit of the sequence $a_1b_1$,
$a_2b_2$, $a_3b_3$, $\dotsc$.

% [** Illustration]
\begin{center}
\Input{208a}{0.4\textwidth}
\end{center}

But by definition the limit of $a_1b_1$, $a_2b_2$, $a_3b_3$, $\dotsc$ is the product
$ab$. Hence we have the theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{430.}{VI.}
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a rectangle}%{\Pnum{430}}%
The area of a rectangle is the
product of its base and altitude.
\end{Theorem}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 209.png---Folio 201-------

\Section{APPLICATIONS TO PLANE GEOMETRY}

\Paragraph{431.}{Incommensurable Ratios Related to Two Circles.} In a
\index{Incommensurables@\textbf{Incommensurables}}%{\Pnum{431}}%
\index{Sequence@\textbf{Sequence}!approximating}%{\Pnum{431}}%
circle inscribe a sequence $P_1$, $P_2$, $P_3$, $\dotsc$ of regular polygons,
each having twice the number of sides of the one preceding it.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent Let the perimeters of these polygons be $p_1$, $p_2$, $p_3$, $\dotsc$ and
their areas $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $\dotsc$. Then the
length $c$ of the circle is defined to be
the limit of the sequence $p_1$, $p_2$, $p_3$, $\dotsc$
and its area $A$ is defined to be the
limit of $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $\dotsc$.

\Indent The sequence of polygons thus inscribed
is called an \emph{approximating
sequence of polygons}.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfil\Input{209a}{1.5in}\hfil

That these sequences are \emph{increasing}
and \emph{bounded} is obvious from the figure.

\begin{Theorem}{432.}{VII.}
The lengths of two circles are
in the same ratio as their radii, and their areas are in
the same ratio as the squares of their radii.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: Let the radii of the two circles be $r$ and~$r'$. Denote
the ratio $r':r$ by~$k$. Then $r' = kr$.

Inscribe in one circle an approximating sequence of polygons
with perimeters $p_1$, $p_2$, $p_3$, $\dotsc$ and areas $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $\dotsc$. In the
other circle inscribe a sequence of similar polygons. By
~476, 477 (Plane Geometry), the perimeters of the latter are
$kp_1$, $kp_2$, $kp_3$, $\dotsc$ and their areas are $k^2A_1$, $k^2A_2$, $k^2A_3$, $\dotsc$.

By ~\Sref{428}, if the limit of $p_1$, $p_2$, $p_3$, $\dotsc$ is $c$ and the limit of
$A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $\dotsc$ is $A$, then the limits of $kp_1$, $kp_2$, $kp_3$, $\dotsc$, and
$k^2A_1$, $k^2A_2$, $k^2A_3$, $\dotsc$ are~$kc$ and~$k^2A$, respectively.

That is, the ratio of the lengths of the circles is $\dfrac{kc}{c} = k= \dfrac{r'}{r}$
and the ratio of their areas is $\dfrac{k^2A}{A} = k^2 = \dfrac{r'^2}{r^2}$.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 210.png---Folio 202-------

\Section{APPLICATIONS OF LIMITS TO SOLID GEOMETRY}

\Paragraph{433.}{Incommensurable Solids.} The areas of curved surfaces,
\index{Incommensurables@\textbf{Incommensurables}}%{\Pnum{433}}%
the volumes of cones, cylinders, and spheres, and even of rectangular
solids involve \emph{incommensurable quantities}. We have
already treated these cases informally and have devised means
for computing them \emph{approximately}.

We now give a formal treatment for logical completeness.

\Paragraph{434.}{Rectangular Parallelopiped.} If the three concurrent
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!as a limit of a sequence}%{\Pnum{434}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{434}}%
edges $a$, $b$, $c$ of a rectangular parallelopiped are incommensurable
with the unit segment, the
volume inclosed is defined as follows:

Let $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ be a sequence of
rational numbers whose limit is the
side~$a$. Let $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ and $c_1$, $c_2$, $c_3$, $\dotsc$
be similar sequences whose limits
are respectively the dimensions $b$ and~$c$.

\noindent\begin{minipage}[b]{\textwidth-1.75in}
\Indent Then the volume is the limit of the
sequence $a_1b_1c_1$, $a_2b_2c_2$, $a_3b_3c_3$, $\dotsc$.

But the limit of this sequence is by
definition the product of the limits of the three sequences
$a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$, $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$, $c_1$, $c_2$, $c_3$, $\dotsc$, or $abc$.
\end{minipage}%
% [** Illustration]
\hfill\smash{\raisebox{-0.25in}{\Input{210a}{1.625in}}}

Hence, we have the theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{435.}{VIII.}
The volume of a rectangular
parallelopiped is equal to the product of its three
concurrent edges.
\end{Theorem}

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Give in decimals four terms of a sequence which approximates
the volume of a rectangular parallelopiped whose
dimensions are $2$, $4$, $\sqrt{5}$.

\item[2.] Give in decimals four terms of a sequence which approximates
the circumference of a circle whose radius is $10$~in.;
also of a sequence which approximates the area.

\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 211.png---Folio 203-------

\Section{APPLICATION OF LIMITS TO THE PYRAMID}

\Paragraph{436.}{Triangular Pyramid.} In a triangular pyramid inscribe
a set of prisms in a manner similar to that shown in the figure
of ~\Sref{243}.
\index{Circumscribed@\textbf{Circumscribed}!prism}%{\Pnum{436}}%
\index{Inscribed@\textbf{Inscribed}!prism}%{\Pnum{436}}%
\index{Prism@\textbf{Prism}!inscribed}%{\Pnum{436}}%
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!as a limit of a sequence}%{\Pnum{436}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{436}}%

Call $V_1$ the sum of the volumes of these inscribed prisms.

Using as altitudes one half the altitudes of the first set of
prisms, inscribe a second set the sum of whose volumes is~$V_2$.
Continuing in this manner, we obtain a sequence of sets of
prisms with volumes $V_1$, $V_2$, $V_3$, $\dotsc$. The limit $V$ of this sequence
we define as \textbf{the volume of the pyramid}.

\begin{Remark}
Circumscribed prisms may also be used for defining the volume of a
pyramid, in which, case we get a \emph{decreasing} sequence of volumes
$U_1$, $U_2$, $U_3$, $\dotsc$, with limit~$U$. That $U = V$ follows from the fact that
the sum of the volumes of the circumscribed prisms exceeds that of the
inscribed prisms by the volume of the lowest circumscribed prism, and
this may be made as small as we please.
\end{Remark}

\begin{Theorem}{437.}{IX.}
Two pyramids with the same
altitudes and equivalent bases have equal volumes.
\end{Theorem}

\Proof: Call a sequence of inscribed prisms in one pyramid
$V_1$, $V_2$, $V_3$, $\dotsc$, and in the other $V'_1$, $V'_2$, $V'_3$, $\dotsc$. Since corresponding
sets of these prisms have equal volumes (~\Sref{200}), that
is, $V_1 = V'_1$, $V_2 = V'_2$, $V_3 = V'_3$, % [** PP: Added comma]
$\dotsc$, the theorem follows from
~\Sref{419}.

\Paragraph{438.}{Convex Closed Curves.} We assume that in a \emph{convex
closed curve} (~\Sref{202}) it is possible to inscribe a sequence of
\index{Curves@\textbf{Curves}!convex, closed}%{\Pnum{438}}%
polygons $P_1$, $P_2$, $P_3$, $\dotsc$, having perimeters $p_1$, $p_2$, $p_3$, $\dotsc$ and
areas $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $\dotsc$ with limits $p$ and $A$ respectively, and to
circumscribe a sequence of polygons $P'_1$, $P'_2$, $P'_3$, $\dotsc$, having
perimeters $p'_1$, $p'_2$, $p'_3$, $\dotsc$ and areas $A'_1$, $A'_2$, $A'_34$, $\dotsc$ with limits
$p'$ and $A'$ respectively, such that $p = p'$ and $A = A'$.

These limits $p$ and $A$ we now define as \textbf{the perimeter and the
area respectively of the curve}.

\NewFolio %% -----File: 212.png---Folio 204-------

\Section{APPLICATION OF LIMITS TO THE CYLINDER}

\Paragraph{439.}{The Cylinder.} Given any cylinder with a convex right
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{439}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{439}}%
cross-section and an element~$e$. In this cross-section inscribe
a sequence $P_1$, $P_2$, $P_3$, $\dotsc$ of polygons, as in ~\Sref{438}, with perimeters
$p_1$, $p_2$, $p_3$, $\dotsc$ and areas $A_1$, $A_2$, $A_3$, $\dotsc$, thus defining the
perimeter $p$ and the area $A$ of the cross-section.

Consider a set of prisms inscribed in this cylinder, of which
$P_1$, $P_2$, $P_3$, $\dotsc$ are right cross-sections.

Then the areas and the volumes of these prisms are respectively
$p_1e$, $p_2e$, $p_3e$, $\dotsc$ and $A_1e$, $A_2e$, $A_3e$, $\dotsc$.

\textbf{The lateral area and the volume of the cylinder are now defined
as the limits of these sequences}.\quad But by ~\Sref{428} these limits are
equal respectively to $pe$ and~$Ae$.

Hence, we have the theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{440.}{X.}
The lateral area of a cylinder is
the product of an element, and the perimeter of a
right section and its volume is the product of an element
and the area of a right section.
\end{Theorem}

\begin{Exercises}

\item[1.] Prove as above that the volume of any convex cylinder
is equal to the product of its altitude and the area of its base.

\item[2.] Prove that the lateral area of a right circular cone is
equal to half the product of the slant height and the perimeter
of its base.

\item[3.] Prove that the volume of any convex cone is equal to
one third the product of its altitude and the area of its base.

\begin{Suggestion}
The treatment required in these exercises is a very close
paraphrase of the definitions and proof given in ~\Sref{439}. Observe that we
cannot begin to make a proof until we have \emph{defined} the subject matter
of the theorem. That is, we must first define \textbf{the areas and volumes in
question}.
\end{Suggestion}
\end{Exercises}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 213.png---Folio 205-------

\Section{APPLICATION OF LIMITS TO THE SPHERE}

\Paragraph{441.}{The Sphere.} Through two points $P$ and $Q$ of a sphere
pass a great circle forming a hemisphere with center~$O$.
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!as a limit of a sequence}%{\Pnum{441}}%

% [** Illustrations]
\begin{center}
\Input{213ab}{\textwidth}
\end{center}

Divide $OA$, the radius perpendicular to the plane of the
great circle, into the equal parts $OC$, $CB$, and $BA$. Through
$C$ and $B$ pass planes parallel to the plane of $POQ$, meeting
the sphere in points $D$ and~$E$, respectively.

Construct right circular cylinders with axes $OC$ and $CB$
and radii $CD$ and $BE$. Denote by $V_1$ the sum of the volumes
of these cylinders.

Now divide the radius $OA$ into six equal parts and construct
five cylinders in the same manner as above. Let the sum of
these volumes be $V_2$.

Continuing in this manner, each time dividing $OA$ into
twice as many equal parts as in the preceding, we obtain a
sequence of sets of cylinders and a corresponding sequence
$V_1$, $V_2$, $V_3$, $\dotsc$ of volumes.

We now define the volume of the hemisphere as the limit of
the sequence $V_1$, $V_2$, $V_3$, $\dotsc$.

\Paragraph{442.}{} Construct a right circular cylinder with its base in the
plane of $POQ$ and with radius and altitude both equal to~$OA$.

Denote by $F$ the figure formed by the lower base of the
cylinder, its lateral surface and the lateral surface of the cone
whose base is the upper base of the cylinder and whose vertex
is at $O'$. (See the right-hand figure.)

\NewFolio %% -----File: 214.png---Folio 206-------

Draw segments $O'M$ and $O'N$. Let the planes through $C$
and $B$ cut $O'A'$ in $C'$ and $B'$ and $O'M$ in $H$ and $K$.

Now form the cylinder $O'C'H$ whose axis is $O'C'$ and whose
radius is $C'H$. Likewise form the cylinder $C'B'K$.

Let $V'_1$ denote the sum of the volumes of $O'C'D'$ and $C'B'E'$
minus the sum of the volumes of $O'C'H$ and $C'B'K$.

In a similar manner, using the planes which divide $OA$ and
hence $O'A'$ into six equal parts, we form another set of five
cylinders, the sum of whose volumes minus that of the smaller
inside cylinders we denote by $V'_2$.

Continuing in this manner, we obtain a sequence of volumes
$V'_1$, $V'_2$, $V'_3$, $\dotsc$ whose limit $V'$ we define as \textbf{the volume of the
given figure}~$F$.

We now prove that $V_1 = V'_1$, $V_2 = V'_2$, $\dotsc$.

Denote $OA$ by $r$, and note that $O'B' = B'K$.

% [** PP: Re-breaking next line]
(1)~$\text{Vol.\ } C'B'E' - \text{Vol.\ } C'B'K
  = \pi C'B'(\overline{B'E'}^2 - \overline{B'K}^2)$ \\
\indent$\phantom{\text{(1)~Vol.\ } C'B'E' - \text{Vol.\ } C'B'K}
  = \pi C'B'(r^2 - \overline{O'B'}^2)$.

% [** PP: Re-breaking]
(2)~$\text{Vol.\ } CBE
  = \pi CB \cdot \overline{BE}^2
  = \pi CB(r^2 - \overline{OB}^2)$, since \\
\indent$\overline{BE}^2 = r^2 - \overline{OB}^2$. But $OB = O'B'$ and $CB = C'B'$.

Hence, $\text{Vol.\ } CBE = \pi C'B'(r^2 - \overline{O'B'}^2)$.

Hence, $\text{Vol.\ } CBE = \text{Vol.\ } C'B'E' - \text{Vol.\ } C'B'K$.

Similarly we show that
\[
\text{Vol.\ } OCD = \text{Vol.\ } O'C'D' - \text{Vol.\ } O'C'H.
\]

Hence, $V_1 = V'_1$. In like manner $V_2 = V'_2$, $V_3 = V'_3$, $\dotsc$.

Hence, $V = V'$, since they are the limits of the same
sequences.

But the volume of the cylinder $O'A'M$ is $\pi r^3$ and of the cone
whose volume was subtracted, $\tfrac{1}{3} \pi r^3$. That is, the volume of
$F$ is $\tfrac{2}{3} \pi r^3$, and hence that of the hemisphere is $\tfrac{2}{3} \pi r^3$. % [** PP: Changed exponent to 3]

Hence, we have the theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{443.}{XI.}
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!volume of}%{\Pnum{443}}%
\index{Volume@\textbf{Volume}!of a sphere}%{\Pnum{443}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{443}}%
The volume of the sphere is $\tfrac{4}{3} \pi r^3$.
\end{Theorem}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 215.png---Folio 207-------

Note that the above proof consists essentially in showing
that the area of the circle $BE$ is equal to that of the ring
between the circles $B'E'$ and $B'K$, and that the area of the
circle $CD$ is equal to that of the ring between $C'D'$ and $C'H$
and so on.

Indeed, this theorem and also that of ~\Sref{437} are special cases
of what is known as \textbf{Cavalieri's Theorem}.
\Page{202}%

\begin{Theorem}{444.}{XII.}
\index{Cavalieri@\textbf{Cavalieri's theorem}}%{\Pnum{444}}%
If two solid figures  are regarded
as resting on the same plane $b$, and if in every
plane parallel to $b$ the sections of the two figures have
equal areas, the figures have equal volumes.
\end{Theorem}

The proof of this general theorem is more difficult than any
thus far given, inasmuch as it involves sequences which \emph{oscillate};
that is, which are neither constantly increasing nor constantly
decreasing.

\Paragraph{445.}{The Area of the Sphere.} About a sphere of radius $r$
\index{Area@\textbf{Area}!of a sphere}%{\Pnum{445}}%
\index{Sphere@\textbf{Sphere}!area of}%{\Pnum{445}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of surfaces}%{\Pnum{445}}%
\index{Measurement@\textbf{Measurement}!of volumes}%{\Pnum{445}}%
construct a sequence of circumscribed polyhedrons such that
the largest face in each polyhedron becomes as small as we
please when we proceed along the sequence. Let $s_1$, $s_2$, $s_3$, $\dotsc$
be the total surfaces of these polyhedrons. This forms a decreasing
sequence with limit $S$ which we define as \textbf{the surface
of the sphere}.

The volumes of these polyhedrons will be $\tfrac{1}{3}rs_1$, $\tfrac{1}{3}rs_2$, $\tfrac{1}{3}rs_3$, $\dotsc$.
Then the volume $V$ of the sphere is defined\footnote{This definition can be shown to be consistent with that of ~\Sref{441}.}
as \textbf{the limit
of this sequence of volumes}.

\settowidth{\TmpLength}{Hence, by ~\Sref{428}, }%
Hence, by ~\Sref{428}, $V = \tfrac{1}{3}rS$. But by ~\Sref{443}, $V = \tfrac{4}{3} \pi r^3$.

\makebox[\TmpLength][l]{Then}$S = \tfrac{3}{r} \cdot \tfrac{4}{3} \pi r^3 = 4 \pi r^2$.

Hence, we have the theorem:

\begin{Theorem}{446.}{XIII.}
The area of the sphere is $4 \pi r^2$.
\end{Theorem}

\NewFolio %% -----File: 216.png---Folio 208-------

\Section{EXERCISES ON LIMITS}

\begin{Itemize}

\item[1.] In addition to those which are found in the text give
other examples of infinite sequences which do not determine
definite numbers.

\item[2.] Give two increasing sequences which determine the
number~$3$. Show that the theorem of ~\Sref{420} applies and proves
that these sequences determine the same number.

\item[3.] Give two decreasing sequences each of which determines
the number~$5$. Apply ~\Sref{421} to show that these sequences
determine the same number.

\item[4.] State fully the relation between a bounded increasing
sequence and the number determined by it. State also the
relations between a bounded decreasing sequence and the number
determined by it.

\item[5.] State fully what is meant by ``a limit of a sequence''
both for increasing and decreasing sequences.

\item[6.] Given two incommensurable segments $AB$ and $CD$.
Lay off on the line $AB$ a \emph{decreasing} sequence of segments,
each of which is commensurable with $CD$, such that the limit
of the sequence is the segment~$AB$.

\item[7.] If $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, $\dotsc$ is an increasing sequence defining the
number~$4$, prove that $3a_1$, $3a_2$, $3a_3$, $\dotsc$ defines the number
$3  4 = 12$.

\item[8.] If $a_1$, $a_2$, $a_3$, and $b_1$, $b_2$, $b_3$, $\dotsc$ are increasing sequences
defining the numbers $3$ and~$5$, show that the sequence $a_1b_1$,
$a_2b_2$, $a_3b_3$, $\dotsc$ defines the number~$15$.

\item[9.] Describe in more detail the meaning of the ratio of two
incommensurable arcs as indicated in ~\Sref{425}. Show that this
ratio is independent of the sequence of units of measurement
used, so long as the limit of this sequence is zero.

\item[10.] Treat the ratio of two incommensurable angles in a
manner similar to the treatment of arcs in the preceding
exercise.

\end{Itemize}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% TRANSCRIBER'S NOTE %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\pdfbookmark[0]{Back Matter}{Back Matter}

\phantomsection
\pdfbookmark[1]{Transcriber's Notes}{Transcriber's Notes}
\fancyhead[C]{\textit{TRANSCRIBER'S NOTES}}

\Section{Transcriber's Notes}

The spellings \emph{inclose} and \emph{parallelopiped} have been
retained. Minor typographical corrections and regularizations of
spelling have been made without comment.
\medskip

\textbf{\hyperref[err019a]{Page~\pageref{err019a}}} In the
third (rightmost) figure after Theorem~73, the labels $\ell_1$
and~$\ell_2$ are exchanged relative to their use in the subsequent
exposition.
\Note{019a}
\medskip

The book contains at least five potentially ambiguous assertions. Some
are complicated to resolve completely.

\textbf{\hyperref[err058a]{Page~\pageref{err058a}}} (\emph{How many
vertices and edges has a hexahedron}?) Combinatorially there are
multiple types of hexahedron, including but not limited to (i)~a
parallelepiped (8~vertices and 12~edges), (ii)~a square-based pyramid
with one base corner shaved off (7~vertices and 11~edges), (iii)~a
pentagonal pyramid (6~vertices and 10~edges), and (iv)~a compound of
two regular tetrahedra placed base-to-base (5~vertices and 9~edges).
\Note{058a}

\textbf{\hyperref[err078a]{Page~\pageref{err078a}}} (\emph{A
closed plane curve is \emph{convex} if it can be cut by a straight
line in not more than two points}.) Presumably this was to be
interpreted as ``\ldots if it \textbf{cannot} be cut by a straight
line in \textbf{more} than two points.'' However, even the latter is
inconsistent with usage throughout the book, since some polygons are
convex while no polygon satisfies this condition.
\Note{078a}

\textbf{\hyperref[err082a]{Page~\pageref{err082a}}}: Approximating a
curved surface by planar patches is more subtle than the analogous
approximation of a curve by an inscribed sequence of segments. For
example, bounded polyhedra inscribed in a right circular cylinder may
have arbitrarily large area.
\Note{082a}

\textbf{\hyperref[err152a]{Page~\pageref{err152a}}}: The construction
of a sequence of polyhedra circumscribed about a sphere depends on the
points of tangency chosen at each stage, and these points cannot be
chosen arbitrarily.
\Note{152a}

\textbf{\hyperref[err197a]{Page~\pageref{err197a}}}: The ``definition''
of~$\sqrt{2}$ as the limit of its sequence of finite decimal
truncations is circular since it requires the first $n$~decimal places
to be known in order to generate $n$~terms of the sequence.
\Note{197a}

\cleardoublepage
